<a id="paper-2606.10665"></a>
### [Equilibrating continuous-variable open quantum systems using stochastic classical trajectories in path-integral space](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10665v1)

**Authors:** William H. D. Moore, Stuart C. Althorpe  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10665v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **5/5** · 🔥 `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **4/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10665_figures/2606.10665_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. The moments ⟨Q2 n(t)⟩, ⟨Q4 n(t)⟩, ⟨P 2 n(t)⟩and the imaginary component of ⟨Q¯nPn(t)⟩for a quartic oscillator coupled to a white bath following the dynamics of Eq. (50) from the initial distribution of Eq. (69) are plotted with solid lines. In the long-time limit, these match the corresponding moments of the quantum Boltzmann distribution of Eq. (42) which are plotted with dotted lines.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper investigates simulating the thermalization of continuous-variable open quantum systems using stochastic classical trajectories within path-integral phase space. The authors demonstrate that these trajectories correctly equilibrate to the exact quantum equilibrium state by solving a derived Fokker-Planck Equation. This offers a novel, albeit numerically challenging, theoretical framework for modeling complex open quantum dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides an alternative, trajectory-based methodology—stochastic classical paths in phase space—to simulate complex open quantum dynamics, which is valuable for developing new approximate simulation techniques beyond standard master equation approaches.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is determining if stochastic classical trajectories evolving in path-integral phase space can correctly equilibrate an open quantum system to its exact thermal equilibrium state.

**Main result.** The study finds that the stochastic trajectories generated via a Matsubara generalized Langevin equation successfully equilibrate to the exact quantum equilibrium state, recovering necessary imaginary momentum-position correlations.

**Method.** The authors employ path-integral formulations and derive a corresponding Fokker-Planck Equation (FPE) from a generalized Langevin equation (GLE). They then analyze the stationary solution of this FPE.

**Model / system.** The system is an open quantum system modeled by coupling a system Hamiltonian ($\hat{H}_s$) to a bath ($\hat{H}_b$) via $\hat{H}_{sb}$. The equilibrium state is analyzed using continuous-variable phase space path integrals in imaginary time.

**Key observables.** The stationary solution of the FPE ($ho_{stat}$), which must marginalize to the known quantum equilibrium distribution ($ho_{eq}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature ($eta$), system frequency/potential ($V(q)$), and bath spectral density (e.g., Debye-Drude).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method relies on mapping non-Markovian dynamics to a Markovian FPE using auxiliary variables, although the resulting GLE itself is numerically unstable when evolved into the complex plane.

**Figures summary.** Figures show convergence of moments (like $\langle Q^2_n(t) angle$) towards the quantum Boltzmann distribution moments, confirming equilibration.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the path-integral formulation for open systems, derives a generalized Langevin equation (GLE), maps this to a Markovian FPE using auxiliary variables, and finally solves/analyzes the stationary solution of the FPE to confirm agreement with known quantum equilibrium results.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Beyond the weak-coupling limit, open quantum systems equilibrate to a highly entangled thermal state. For continuous-variable systems, this state can be written explicitly as an imaginary-time phase-space path integral, in which the positions are directly entangled with the bath, and the momenta are correlated with the positions through a phase term. Here, we ask to what extent this state can be reached by propagating stochastic classical trajectories in path-integral phase space. Surprisingly, we find that the trajectories equilibrate to the exact quantum equilibrium state, recovering the purely imaginary momentum-position correlation in the phase term. The trajectories are generated using a recently derived Matsubara generalized Langevin equation, which produces the imaginary correlations by evolving the stochastic variables into the complex plane. This makes the dynamics numerically unstable, but we are nonetheless able to demonstrate the equilibration of a quartic oscillator coupled to a white-noise bath. These unexpected findings could lead to new approximate methodologies for simulating continuous-variable open quantum systems.

</details>

<img src="2606.11000_figures/2606.11000_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Fig. 5: Graph classification task: Training accuracy of QA-AEGNN on the polarity augmented dataset for</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper introduces QA-AEGNN, a novel framework that implements asynchronous event-based graph neural networks on neutral-atom quantum computers. By programming the Rydberg Hamiltonian to mimic message passing via controlled atomic interactions, it achieves an analog quantum computation of GNNs. This approach demonstrates high accuracy and robustness compared to classical methods for processing neuromorphic vision data.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly applies complex many-body physics—specifically controlled Rydberg interactions—to solve a cutting-edge problem in AI/ML (event-based GNNs). It showcases how continuous Hamiltonian dynamics can serve as a powerful, native analog computational primitive for information processing beyond simple quantum gates.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is developing an efficient quantum analog for processing sparse, high-temporal-resolution event data using Graph Neural Networks (GNNs). This requires mapping the asynchronous graph structure onto a controllable physical quantum system.

**Main result.** The proposed Quantum Analog AEGNN (QA-AEGNN) successfully implements GNN message passing by programming the native Rydberg Hamiltonian dynamics of neutral atoms. It demonstrates superior performance compared to classical counterparts on both synthetic and neuromorphic datasets.

**Method.** A hybrid quantum-classical training scheme is employed where analog Hamiltonian parameters are optimized classically. The graph computation is performed via time evolution governed by the Rydberg interaction, which naturally realizes message passing across physically placed atoms.

**Model / system.** The system utilizes neutral-atom quantum processors with qubits encoded in ground/Rydberg states. The dynamics are governed by a programmable Rydberg Hamiltonian incorporating global drives, local detunings (for feature embedding), and strong pairwise van der Waals interactions ($V_{ij}$) that enforce graph connectivity via the Rydberg blockade.

**Key observables.** Test accuracy on classification tasks (Graph Classification, N-MNIST); final observable feature vector $\mathbf{z}_s$ constructed as a histogram of excited atoms after quantum evolution.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rydberg blockade radius ($R_b$), Rabi frequency ($\Omega(t)$), local detuning parameters ($\delta_i(t)$), and the time evolution duration ($\Delta	au_s$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The physical placement of atoms must accurately map graph adjacency (neighbors within $R_b$) to ensure correct message passing. The framework assumes the ability to perform quantum state readout as a histogram.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the pipeline: event embedding $ightarrow$ Hamiltonian evolution ($\Omega(t), R_b$) $ightarrow$ Readout. Results compare QA-AEGNN vs AEGNN performance across different noise models and data types (e.g., Fig 6, Fig 7).

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the need for quantum GNNs on event data. It details the physical mapping onto neutral atoms using the Rydberg Hamiltonian to simulate message passing. The methodology covers feature encoding (state initialization vs. detuning) and the hybrid training loop. Finally, performance is benchmarked against classical methods under various noise conditions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Asynchronous, event-based graph neural networks (AEGNNs) have recently emerged as an efficient paradigm for processing the sparse and high-temporal-resolution data from event cameras. In this paper, we propose quantum analog AEGNNs (QA-AEGNNs), a novel framework to implement an AEGNN on a neutral-atom quantum computer. Neutral-atom quantum processors offer a programmable analog quantum computing platform based on controllable Rydberg-atom interactions. To this end, we map the streaming event data to an array of trapped neutral atoms, where each atom represents a graph node (event) and is positioned such that geometric proximity reflects the spatio-temporal neighborhood of events. The native Rydberg Hamiltonian of the quantum processor is programmed to mirror the message-passing computations of the AEGNN, with atomic qubit states serving as node feature embeddings and inter-atom interactions realizing graph edges. Furthermore, we propose a hybrid quantum-classical training scheme in which the analog Hamiltonian parameters (e.g., laser pulse amplitudes and detunings) are optimized using classical feedback to learn the quantum AEGNN model from data. Our approach leverages the continuous Hamiltonian dynamics and massive parallelism of neutral-atom quantum systems to natively execute event-based graph computations with potential accuracy improvements

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10510"></a>
### [Single-photon scattering in a dissipative superconducting-qubit--SSH lattice hybrid](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10510v1)

**Authors:** Xiao-Xue Zhang, Jie Zhou, Xi-Zheng Zhang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10510v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `analog quantum simulation` **4/5** · 🔥 `interference shaping light` **4/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **2/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **2/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops a theoretical framework to analyze single-photon scattering in a hybrid system combining an SSH photonic lattice and a dissipative/amplifying superconducting qubit. By deriving the energy-dependent scattering matrix $S(E)$, the authors show that topological features (dimerization, flux) combined with non-Hermitian coupling allow for precise control over light transmission and reflection. This establishes a compact model for realizing tunable topological absorbers and amplifiers in quantum networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete framework for engineering topological quantum devices by controlling light-matter interaction using superconducting circuits, directly relevant to photonic quantum information processing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates single-photon scattering dynamics when a topological photonic lattice (SSH) is locally coupled to a superconducting qubit exhibiting tunable loss or gain.

**Main result.** The system's reflection and transmission spectra are highly controllable by tuning the SSH dimerization, synthetic magnetic flux, and the non-Hermitian coupling strength ($\gamma$).

**Method.** The authors derived an explicit real-space scattering matrix $S(E)$ formulation and benchmarked its analytical results against time-domain wave-packet simulations.

**Model / system.** The model combines a Su–Schrieffer–Heeger (SSH) photonic lattice Hamiltonian with a superconducting qubit Hamiltonian, leading to a total system governed by non-Hermitian dynamics in the single-excitation sector.

**Key observables.** Reflection probability ($R$), Transmission probability ($T$), and the eigenvalues of the scattering matrix $S(E)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** SSH dimerization ($\delta$), synthetic gauge phase ($\Phi$), and the qubit non-Hermitian coupling magnitude $|\gamma|$ (controlling loss/gain).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to the single-excitation sector, and the scattering problem is solved using asymptotic matching techniques.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate reflection landscapes ($R^{(s)}_L$) vs. momentum $k$ for varying gain/loss ($\gamma$), wave-packet evolution in transmission/reflection regimes, and the eigenvalue spectrum of $S(E)$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from deriving the general scattering formalism to specializing it for the SSH lattice structure, finally incorporating the qubit scatterer and analyzing the resulting spectral features.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study single-photon scattering in a Su--Schrieffer--Heeger (SSH) photonic lattice locally coupled to a superconducting qubit with tunable loss or gain. Working in the single-excitation sector, we derive an explicit real-space scattering formulation for the full energy-dependent scattering matrix $S(E)$ and identify how its eigenvalues encode coherent perfect absorption, amplification, and spectral singular behavior. The analytical results are benchmarked against time-domain wave-packet simulations, which reproduce the stationary scattering probabilities with high accuracy. We show that the SSH dimerization, the qubit-induced non-Hermitian self-energy, and the synthetic gauge phase cooperate to reshape the reflection and transmission spectra in a highly selective way. In particular, changing the dimerization can switch the system between transmission-dominated and reflection-dominated regimes, while the flux provides a direct handle on interference and symmetry-controlled response. We also find a robust loss--gain correspondence in the reflection landscape and show that the linewidth broadening is governed predominantly by the magnitude $|γ|$ of the non-Hermitian coupling. These results establish a compact and experimentally relevant framework for topological scattering in superconducting quantum networks.

</details>

### [Anomalous mobility edges and extended-localized transition in a quasiperiodic emitter-cavity array](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10585v1)

**Authors:** H. T. Cui, H. Z. Shen, M. Qin, X. X. Yi

**Summary.** The paper analyzes single excitation transport in quasiperiodic emitter-cavity arrays, showing that dissipation can induce anomalous mobility edges. By linking localization to the nature of bound states (discrete vs. continuum), they construct a full phase diagram. This demonstrates that quantum excitation localization can be precisely controlled by tuning only the cavity field parameters.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed theoretical framework for controlling quantum transport in structured media, directly relevant to designing artificial photonic or solid-state quantum simulators where disorder/quasiperiodicity is key.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how dissipation induces anomalous mobility edges and extended-localized transitions in quasiperiodic emitter-cavity arrays.

**Main result.** The localization properties are governed by the nature of quantum bound states (Discrete vs. Continuum), allowing for full phase diagram construction solely by tuning cavity fields.

**Method.** Analytical determination using generalized duality transformations, combined with calculating effective Hamiltonians derived from eliminating cavity degrees of freedom.

**Model / system.** The system is a one-dimensional array of two-level emitters coupled to an array of single-mode cavities via a quasiperiodic onsite potential. The dynamics are analyzed in the single excitation subspace using $H = H_s + H_c + H_{int}$.

**Key observables.** Inverse Participation Ratio (IPR), Fractal Dimension ($	au$), and long-time population $|\alpha_n(t)|^2$ of bound states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Quasiperiodic potential strength ($\Delta$), cavity central frequency ($\omega_c$), coupling strength ($g$), and energy level ($E$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to the single excitation subspace, and approximations like the rotating-wave approximation are used.

**Figures summary.** Figures show phase diagrams mapping extended, localized, and critical phases based on parameters; other figures illustrate $|\alpha_n|^2$ and IPR variations across these predicted mobility edges.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the effective Hamiltonian by eliminating cavity modes, uses duality transformations to find anomalous mobility edges analytically, maps out a full phase diagram (Fig. 1(b)), and verifies localization regimes using long-time dynamics simulations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The manipulation of localization in quasiperiodic systems by mobility edges or localization transition holds significant physical importance. In this letter, we demonstrated that the dissipation can induce the emergence of anomalous mobility edges and extended-localized transition in emitter-cavity arrays controlled by quasiperiodic potentials. Specifically, we observe that the localization properties of emitters is governed by the nature of quantum bound states, either discrete or embedded in continuum, providing a unified mechanism linking the emitter-photon bound physics to quasiperiodic criticality. Depending on the bound state discrete or continuumlike, the induced effective excitation hopping exhibits either exponentially decaying or sinusoidally oscillating, giving rise to the formation of localized or critical states, respectively. Through a generalized duality transformation, we analytically determine the anomalous mobility edges and the critical strength of potential, enabling the construction of a full phase diagram. The study reveals that the physical characteristics of cavity exert a significant influence on excitation localization. Therefore, the manipulation of excitation localization can be achieved solely by adjusting the cavity fields.

</details>

### [Interplay between photon condensation and electron-electron interactions in molecular systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11060v1)

**Summary.** This paper models photon condensation in molecular systems by coupling electrons to an electromagnetic cavity mode. It rigorously incorporates on-site Coulomb repulsion via the Hubbard term, demonstrating that this interaction can drive the system into a first-order phase transition regime, contrasting with simpler non-interacting predictions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses cavity QED phenomena (photon condensation/superradiance) in realistic, correlated solid-state or molecular settings, providing a detailed mechanism for how electron correlations modify fundamental quantum optical transitions.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the complex interplay between photon condensation (a magnetostatic instability) and electron-electron interactions within molecular quantum systems.

**Main result.** The inclusion of Hubbard repulsion can fundamentally change the nature of the phase transition, allowing for a first-order transition in regimes where non-interacting models predict only second-order transitions.

**Method.** The analysis employs mean-field theory coupled with exact diagonalization techniques on small molecular plaquettes to analyze energy functionals and order parameters.

**Model / system.** The model consists of multiple identical square planar plaquettes hosting electrons, described by a tight-binding Hamiltonian augmented by an on-site Hubbard repulsion term ($U$). This system is coupled to a spatially nonuniform cavity mode.

**Key observables.** Photon condensate order parameter ($ar{\alpha}$), polaritonic excitation energies, and the character (first vs. second order) of the phase transition.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Electron filling factor ($N_e$), Hubbard repulsion strength ($U/t$), light-matter coupling strength ($\eta$), and hopping parameters ($	au/t$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on mean-field decoupling for light-matter coupling, and in some parts, approximations like Holstein-Primakoff transformations are used to linearize the Hamiltonian around the mean-field state.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the order parameter $ar{\alpha}$ versus hopping ratio ($	au/t$) showing discontinuities (first-order) or smooth changes (second-order), and plot polaritonic energies revealing spectral signatures of the instability.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the coupled Hamiltonian, analyzing the single-molecule electronic structure using SU(2) symmetry, deriving a mean-field effective Hamiltonian for light-matter coupling, and finally determining the phase transition order by examining energy functionals and polaritonic spectra.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate a minimal molecular model consisting of square planar plaquettes hosting multiple electrons, whose dynamics is governed by a tight-binding Hamiltonian supplemented by on-site Hubbard repulsion. By coupling this system to a spatially nonuniform cavity mode, we analyze the emergence of a magnetostatic instability, namely photon condensation, originating from the paramagnetic Van Vleck mechanism. The global behavior of the system is analyzed for different electronic filling factors, and we find that, except for the special cases of half-filling and single electron, where the transition, if it occurs, is necessarily a second order phase transition, the global system may also undergo a first order transition because of the action of the electron-electron interaction. The polaritonic excitation energies are analyzed, providing clear spectroscopic signatures of the magnetostatic instability and of its order.

</details>

</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10145_figures/2606.10145_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 5. (Color online) (a) Delay function ˜𝑑(˜𝑡) for different memory exponents 𝛼= 𝛼𝑅= 𝛼𝑇. The delay vanishes in the Newtonian limit (𝛼= 1) and develops a pronounced peak as memory strengthens. (b) Peak delay 𝑑peak = max[ ˜𝑑(˜𝑡)] in the (𝛼𝑅, 𝛼𝑇) plane. The delay is maximized when both translational and rotational memory are strong, demonstrating cooperative enhancement of force-orientation lag.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper provides an analytical theory for active particles moving in viscoelastic fluids whose friction dynamics are described by power-law memory. By solving non-Markovian Langevin equations, the authors predict novel phenomena like stretched exponential decay of orientation and a measurable time-delay between propulsion force and particle direction. This framework is crucial for understanding biological swimmers in complex polymeric environments.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on soft matter, the mathematical framework of solving coupled, non-Markovian GLEs with power-law memory kernels is directly applicable to modeling open quantum systems where environmental dissipation (bath interaction) exhibits anomalous or long-range correlations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study addresses active particle motion in viscoelastic media, moving beyond simplified Markovian friction models that fail to capture complex relaxation dynamics.

**Main result.** The theory derives analytical expressions showing that the memory kernel controls anomalous scaling exponents and introduces a measurable time-delay between propulsion force and swimmer orientation.

**Method.** The authors developed an analytical framework by solving coupled, non-Markovian Generalized Langevin Equations (GLEs) for translational and rotational motion.

**Model / system.** The model describes a self-propelled microswimmer moving in a 2D viscoelastic bath characterized by power-law memory kernels ($\Gamma(t) \sim t^{-\alpha}$). The dynamics are governed by coupled GLEs incorporating both activity ($v_0$) and viscoelastic damping.

**Key observables.** Orientational Correlation Function $C(	ilde{t})$, Mean-Square Displacement (MSD), and the Memory Delay Function $d(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Memory exponents ($\alpha_T, \alpha_R$), activity strength ($Pe$), and intrinsic rotation frequency ($\omega_0$). The key regimes include $\alpha=1$ (Newtonian) versus $0 < \alpha < 1$ (power-law memory).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis operates in the overdamped limit, assumes zero-mean Gaussian noise processes consistent with the Fluctuation-Dissipation Theorem.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the stretched exponential decay of $C(	ilde{t})$ for $\alpha < 1$, and show the dependence of the memory delay function $d(t)$ on the viscoelastic exponents ($\alpha_R, \alpha_T$).

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the GLEs, solves them using Laplace transforms to find correlation functions ($C(t)$), derives expressions for $\langle\Delta r(t)angle$ and MSD, and finally analyzes the non-Markovian coupling via the memory delay function $d(t)$.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Active particles from bacteria to synthetic microswimmers often navigate viscoelastic media with complex relaxation dynamics. The classical active Brownian model that assumes instantaneous friction is clearly not applicable to describe such motility, while the non-Markovian processes combined with viscoelasticity are relatively unexplored. Here, we develop an analytical theory for an active particle in a power-law viscoelastic medium by solving coupled non-Markovian generalized Langevin equations for translational and rotational degrees of freedom. The viscoelastic memory results in novel phenomena such as fractional short-time transport, enhanced long-time persistence, and de-correlation of the instantaneous force and the swimmer orientation. We demonstrate that the memory kernel controls the anomalous scaling exponents, while the activity determines the crossover between sub-diffusive, ballistic and diffusive regimes. Our work provides a framework for theoretical description of biological and synthetic micro swimmers in complex biological and polymeric environments.

</details>

### [Precision measurements at the interface between unitary and non-unitary encoding](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10529v1)

**Authors:** Peng Xu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10529v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This paper develops a unified theory for quantum metrology under generalized noise, analyzing parameter estimation for both unitary and non-unitary dynamics. It derives analytic scaling laws showing that Heisenberg limited precision ($\propto 1/N$) is achievable in specific noisy regimes, even when dissipation is present. The findings offer practical guidance for designing robust quantum sensing protocols.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides crucial theoretical benchmarks for designing quantum sensors in realistic noisy environments by quantifying when entanglement can overcome decoherence to reach fundamental precision limits.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how the precision of quantum parameter estimation scales when the encoding process transitions between unitary (coherent) and non-unitary (dissipative/noisy) dynamics.

**Main result.** Heisenberg scaling ($\propto 1/N$) can be achieved for both unitary and non-unitary estimations if specific commutation relations hold, demonstrating entanglement's role in enhancing metrology even under noise.

**Method.** The authors employ linear response theory and the error propagation formula to derive analytic precision expressions, supplemented by numerical validation using the Discrete Truncated Wigner Approximation (DTWA).

**Model / system.** The model involves quantum systems subjected to generalized noise, including single-particle and collective dephasing/decay. Dynamics are governed by non-Hermitian Hamiltonians incorporating both intrinsic evolution ($\Omega$) and dissipation ($\gamma$).

**Key observables.** Precision scaling for the unitary parameter $\Delta\Omega$ and the dissipation strength $\Delta\gamma$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size $N$, noise rates ($\gamma$), encoding time ($t$), and commutation relations between observables and noise operators.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivations rely on linear response theory, cumulant expansions, and approximations up to first order in the noise parameters.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate precision dynamics for both unitary and non-unitary regimes, confirming Heisenberg scaling ($\propto 1/N$) under optimal conditions but showing degradation towards Standard Quantum Limit (SQL) or constant values otherwise.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops a unified framework by deriving analytic expressions for $\Delta\Omega$ and $\Delta\gamma$. It analyzes specific noise types (single-particle vs. collective) and compares the scaling laws based on whether observables commute with noise operators, providing practical guidance for protocol design.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate precision scaling at the interface between unitary and non-unitary encoding under generalized noise including single-particle and collective dephasing and decay. Using linear response theory and the error propagation formula, we derive analytic precision expressions for both the unitary parameter $Ω$ and the dissipation strength $γ$. For unitary encoding, when the observable commutes with a Hermitian noise operator, the optimal encoding time is independent of $N$, yielding the Heisenberg limit $ΔΩ\propto 1 / N$; otherwise the precision degrades to the standard quantum limit or ceases to improve with $N$. For non-unitary encoding, when $[\hat{A}, \hat{O}] = 0$, the precision is insensitive to intrinsic dynamics and encoding time, scaling as $Δγ\propto \sqrt{γ/ \expval*{\hat{L}^\dagger \hat{L}}}$. Notably, for collective decay, the Dicke state reaches the Heisenberg limit $Δγ\propto 1 / N$, demonstrating that entanglement can enhance non-unitary estimation. Our results provide a unified framework and practical guidance for designing quantum metrology protocols in noisy environments.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11002"></a>
### [Revealing the topology of quantum states via Kirkwood-Dirac quasiprobabilities](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11002v1)

**Authors:** Stefano Gherardini, Luca Lepori  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11002v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `interference shaping light` **3/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.11002_figures/2606.11002_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Energy spectra of the Hamiltonian in Eq. (4) at M = 1, for periodic boundary conditions (left panel) and with open boundary conditions along y (right panel), respectively. In the right panel, gapless edge modes, topologically protected and interpolating between the two bands, are present.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.11002_figures/2606.11002_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">

</div>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a novel diagnostic tool to detect the topology of many-body quantum states by analyzing 'strange correlators.' It achieves this by showing these correlators can be expressed as sums over Kirkwood-Dirac quasiprobabilities (KDQs). The ultimate goal is proposing an interferometric protocol that allows experimental measurement of these topological signatures.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous theoretical bridge between abstract topological invariants and measurable quantum correlation functions using quasiprobability formalisms, which is highly relevant for designing next-generation quantum sensing protocols.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central goal is developing a theoretical method to distinguish between different topological classes of quantum states in many-body systems.

**Main result.** The authors propose using 'strange correlators,' which can be mathematically decomposed into Kirkwood-Dirac quasiprobabilities (KDQs), forming a measurable quantum topology witness.

**Method.** The approach links strange correlators to KDQs, interpreting them as weak values of an observable governing the transition between trivial and topological phases. This link is then exploited via interferometric protocols.

**Model / system.** The analysis applies to general many-body quantum systems exhibiting topological order, with specific examples including the 2D Chern insulator (BHZ model) and AKLT chains. The dynamics are often analyzed through sudden quench transformations or time evolution under perturbations.

**Key observables.** Strange correlators ($s[\hat{o}]$), Kirkwood-Dirac quasiprobabilities ($q_{k,j}$), and the characteristic function $G(u)$ measured via quantum interferometry.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The scaling behavior of strange correlators with momentum/distance ($|k|^{-\alpha}$ vs $|k|^{eta}$) is key to determining topology. The validity of linear response theory depends on time scales relative to energy gaps ($\Delta$ and $V_0$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies heavily on establishing the mathematical equivalence between strange correlators and sums over KDQs, and proposing an interferometric measurement scheme for reconstruction.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate spectral properties (BHZ model), the dependence of quasiprobability modulus $|q(kx)|$ on topological assumptions, and schematic diagrams of the quantum interferometry protocol used to measure correlation functions.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining strange correlators and KDQs, establishing their mathematical link, applying this framework to specific models (like BHZ), deriving linear response relations for the imaginary part of the correlator, and finally detailing an experimental/interferometric protocol for measurement.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We discuss a theoretical approach to discriminate whether two states of a many-body quantum system belong or not to different topology classes. This approach is based on expressing a strange correlator - a recently established tool for quantum topology discrimination - between the states as a function of Kirkwood-Dirac quasiprobabilities (KDQs). KDQs provide a first-principles representation of two-time quantum correlators. The link between strange correlators and KDQs allows to establish that strange correlators are weak values of an observable converting an initial trivial state into a topologically non-trivial one. We thus propose a quantum topology witness that is achievable measuring the prior and subsequent effects on a many-body system of a sudden quench transformation that realizes the transition between trivial and topological phases. The witness is evaluated on a probe quantum state whose main features are detailed within the paper. Finally, directly exploiting schemes that allows for the complete reconstruction of KDQs, we address an interferometric protocol for topology discrimination, along with a general discussion of the main lines and challenges towards its implementation.

</details>

### [The Two Dimensional Dynamical Bulk Boundary Correspondence: Beyond Two Band Models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10731v1)

**Authors:** Tomasz Masłowski, Nicholas Sedlmayr  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10731v1>

**Summary.** This paper investigates the dynamical bulk-boundary correspondence in 2D topological superconductors after sudden quenches. Using the Loschmidt amplitude as a diagnostic tool, the authors tested this concept across various topological phase transitions. They conclude that the dynamic correspondence is not simply dictated by the initial and final equilibrium Chern numbers, suggesting a more nuanced, edge-dependent topological structure.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges non-equilibrium quantum dynamics (Loschmidt echo) with topological invariants (Chern numbers), providing a complex, quantitative test case for extending bulk-boundary principles into time evolution.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research investigates a dynamical equivalent of the bulk-boundary correspondence (DBBC) for topological models subjected to sudden quenches, extending beyond simple two-band descriptions.

**Main result.** The authors find that there is no straightforward correlation between the equilibrium phases quenched between and the observed dynamical bulk-boundary correspondence. Furthermore, this correspondence can depend on the specific orientation of the edges, suggesting a weak topological variant.

**Method.** The analysis relies on calculating the Loschmidt amplitude/return rate ($\ell(t)$) and identifying Dynamical Quantum Phase Transitions (DQPTs) where these quantities become non-analytic or exhibit zero eigenvalues in the Loschmidt matrix spectrum.

**Model / system.** The primary model studied is a complicated two-dimensional topological superconductor defined on a honeycomb lattice, allowing quenches between states characterized by different Chern numbers ($
u$).

**Key observables.** Loschmidt amplitude/return rate ($\ell(t)$), Loschmidt eigenvalues ($\lambda_{n,k}(t)$), and the system's Chern number ($
u$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The quench path between initial and final topological phases (e.g., $
u: 0 	o 1$ or $
u: -2 	o -3$), and the geometry of the edges (armchair vs. zig-zag).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is performed in a cylindrical geometry, which may limit direct applicability to physical systems with single open edges; the exact cause and generality of the phenomenon remain unknown.

**Figures summary.** Figures plot the lowest Loschmidt eigenvalue $\lambda_{0,k}(t)$ vs. momentum/time for various quenches on different edge types (AC/ZZ), illustrating where in-gap modes appear near DQPTs; Figure 6 shows the return rate derivative ($\dot{l}(t)$) across quench paths.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the theoretical framework of DBBC, applies it to a complex 2D topological superconductor model undergoing quenches between different Chern number sectors, and concludes by detailing specific results showing deviations from simple equilibrium predictions based on edge orientation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A dynamical equivalent of the bulk-boundary correspondence has been suggested to occur in one and two dimensional topological models following sudden quenches. Depending on the topological invariant of the time evolving and initial phases involved large boundary contributions to a dynamical free energy occur. Moreover they occur periodically between the critical times at which this dynamical free energy becomes non-analytic, \emph{i.e.}~at dynamical quantum phase transitions. At these critical times the eigenvalue spectrum of the Loschmidt matrix which underlies the dynamical free energy closes its gap. The boundary contributions are understood to be due to zero-modes or in-gap bands of this matrix, forming a close analogy with equilibrium topological models and their edge modes. The exact cause of this phenomena and its generality remain unknown. In this article we test the dynamical bulk-boundary correspondence for a complicated two dimensional topological superconductor with a rich phase diagram, allowing quenches between many different Chern numbers. We show that there is no straightforward correspondence between the equilibrium phases quenched between and the dynamical bulk boundary correspondence. Furthermore the correspondence can depend on the orientation of the edges, suggesting a possible weak topological variant.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.10319"></a>
### [Nonreciprocal Photon Blockade in an Asymmetric Cavity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10319v1)

**Authors:** Shao-Xiong Wu, Jin-Na Fan, Dan Yan, Cheng-Hua Bai, Qiannan Wu, Mengwei Li  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10319v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `interference shaping light` **4/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10319_figures/2606.10319_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1: Schematic of the system. A two-level atom is trapped inside an asymmetric Fabry-P´erot cavity, the cavity is driven by a coher- ent laser and the atom is pumped by a control laser with the same frequency. When the driving laser is injected from the left mirror toward the right, it is deﬁned as the forward case, and the backward case corresponds to the opposite propagating direction. By adjust- ing the control laser, the photons exhibit photon antibunching in the forward case and bunching behaviour in the backward case, i.e., the nonreciprocal PB effect.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10319_figures/2606.10319_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">

Panels (a) and (c) correspond to the optimal control condition Ω+ c , while panels (b) and (d) represent the optimal control condition Ω− c . Red solid lines and blue dashed lines denote the analytical results derived via the probability amplitude method for the forward and backward cases, respectively. Black circles o and asterisks ∗mean the corresponding numerical solutions obtained from the quantum master equation.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10319_figures/2606.10319_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">

**Summary.** The authors propose using an asymmetric cavity QED system driven by two lasers to achieve strong nonreciprocal photon blockade. By carefully tuning the control laser's phase and amplitude, they engineer destructive quantum interference pathways. This results in robust antibunching (blockade) when light propagates forward, while observing bunching backward, demonstrating a high degree of directional control.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a highly practical theoretical blueprint for generating directional quantum light sources, which is crucial for advancing quantum networking and measurement protocols that require time-reversal symmetry breaking.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is realizing tunable and strong nonreciprocal photon blockade (PB) within an asymmetric Fabry-Pérot cavity.

**Main result.** Strong nonreciprocal PB, with a ratio exceeding 30 dB, is achieved by engineering quantum interference pathways using an auxiliary control laser field.

**Method.** The analysis employs a phenomenological non-Hermitian Hamiltonian approach and utilizes the weak-driving regime approximation to solve for time evolution in a truncated Hilbert space.

**Model / system.** The system is a single-mode optical cavity trapping a two-level atom, driven by a coherent laser ($\Omega$) and controlled by an auxiliary field ($\Omega_c$). The asymmetry is inherent in the cavity decay rates ($\kappa_1 
eq \kappa_2$).

**Key observables.** Second-order photon correlation function ($g^{(2)}(0)$) to diagnose blockade (antibunching, $g^{(2)}<1$) and nonreciprocal ratio ($\eta = -10 \log_{10} (g^{(2)}_f(0)/g^{(2)}_b(0))$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Control field amplitude ($\Omega_c$), relative phase ($	heta$), cavity decay rate ($\kappa$), and detunings ($\Delta_c, \Delta_a$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the weak-driving regime approximation ($\{\Omega, \Omega_c\} \ll \kappa$) and a truncated Hilbert space up to three excitations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the system setup, schematic pathways for quantum interference (for $\Omega_+^c$ vs $\Omega_-^c$), $g^{(2)}(0)$ dependence on control parameters, and time-delayed correlation functions ($g^{(2)}(	au)$) showing distinct forward (PB) and backward (bunching) behavior.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the non-Hermitian Hamiltonian model, applies weak-driving approximations to derive coupled differential equations for probability amplitudes, solves for steady-state observables like $g^{(2)}(0)$, and finally compares two optimal control conditions ($\Omega_+^c$ vs $\Omega_-^c$) to quantify the resulting nonreciprocity.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a scheme to realize tunable and strong nonreciprocal photon blockade (PB) in an asymmetric Fabry-Pérot cavity. The setup consists of a single-mode optical cavity trapping a two-level atom, with the cavity coherently driven by a laser and the atom pumped by an auxiliary control field of the same frequency. By engineering quantum interference between multiple excitation pathways by adjusting the amplitude and relative phase of the control laser, we identify two distinct optimal control conditions that enable directional suppression of two-photon states. Under optimal control conditions, strong nonreciprocal PB is achieved, with a nonreciprocal ratio exceeding 30 dB over a broad operational bandwidth. The proposed protocol requires only standard coherent laser sources and is compatible with current cavity QED experimental setups, offering a practical and scalable platform for nonreciprocal quantum photonics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Tame the Umklapp Processes in Real-Time Lattice Simulation for Hydrodynamics: An Ising Field Theory Study](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.09984v1)

**Summary.** The paper simulates a 1+1D Ising field theory using real-time lattice Hamiltonian methods to study how the system reaches hydrodynamic equilibrium. By analyzing the stress-energy tensor correlation function, the authors confirm that Umklapp scattering is suppressed, allowing sound waves to propagate. This allowed them to nonperturbatively calculate fundamental transport properties like the ratio of bulk viscosity to entropy density and the speed of sound.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it uses advanced quantum many-body simulation techniques (MPS) to tackle non-equilibrium dynamics—a key area of modern condensed matter theory that bridges microscopic quantum rules to macroscopic fluid behavior, similar to studies in AMO systems undergoing rapid quench or thermalization.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand non-equilibrium dynamics, specifically hydrodynamization, and calculate transport coefficients like bulk viscosity ($\zeta$) and the speed of sound ($c_s$) in a system exhibiting Umklapp processes.

**Main result.** The simulations successfully demonstrated that Umklapp processes are suppressed in the scaling region, allowing for the emergence of relativistic hydrodynamic sound modes. Quantitative results were obtained: $\zeta/s = 14.19 \pm 0.90$ and $c_s/c = 0.76 \pm 0.02$.

**Method.** The research employs real-time lattice Hamiltonian simulation techniques, utilizing both classical exact diagonalization and advanced Matrix Product State (MPS) tensor network methods to calculate the symmetric correlation function of the stress-energy tensor.

**Model / system.** The model is a 1+1 dimensional Ising field theory, derived from deforming the 1+1D Ising conformal field theory. The system evolves according to a lattice Hamiltonian, and observables are extracted from the time evolution of the stress-energy tensor correlation function.

**Key observables.** Symmetric correlation function of the stress-energy tensor ($G_{\mu
u}^s$), bulk viscosity ($\zeta/s$), speed of sound ($c_s/c$), and momentum conservation checks.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis focuses on the 'scaling region' defined by specific limits on lattice couplings, requiring extrapolation to the continuum limit. Key parameters include temperature $T \approx 7.14$ (in units of particle mass).

**Assumptions / limitations.** It is assumed that hydrodynamics accurately describes the late-time and long-wavelength behavior, and that Umklapp processes must be suppressed for sound modes to emerge.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the transition from diffusive dynamics (outside scaling region) to propagating sound modes (inside scaling region) in momentum space correlators. Plots also show linear fits of derived parameters ($c_s/c$ and $\zeta/s$) versus lattice coupling strength for continuum extrapolation.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the necessity of real-time simulation over Euclidean methods, details the Ising model setup and stress-energy tensor derivation, analyzes the transition from diffusive to propagating modes using MPS simulations, and finally extrapolates results to extract quantitative transport coefficients in the continuum limit.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We calculate the real-time symmetric correlation function of the stress-energy tensor for a non-integrable Ising field theory consisting of three stable scalar particles via lattice Hamiltonian simulation. Using classical exact diagonalization and the matrix product state tensor network methods, we find that in the scaling region of the lattice theory, Umklapp processes are suppressed and the sound modes of relativistic hydrodynamics emerge at long wavelength and late time. The extracted ratio of bulk viscosity to entropy density is $ζ/s=14.19\pm 0.90$ and the speed of sound is $c_s/c=0.76 \pm 0.02$ at the temperature $T\approx 7.14$ in units of the lowest stable particle's mass. Our study demonstrates the utility of real-time lattice Hamiltonian simulation for describing hydrodynamization and calculating transport coefficients nonperturbatively.

</details>

### [Finite-Time Orientational Relaxation Restructures Collective Motion in Polar Active Matter](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10474v1)

**Summary.** This work analyzes polar active matter using a model where particle orientation relaxes at a finite rate. Through extensive simulations, the authors map out a complex phase diagram showing transitions between distinct structural states like banded and cross-sea phases. The key finding is that this finite relaxation time acts as a crucial control parameter, fundamentally restructuring the system's collective behavior.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical active matter, the study of non-equilibrium phase transitions, first-order transitions diagnosed by cumulants, and the role of dissipation/relaxation rates ($D_r$ vs $J$) provides analogous concepts to understanding critical dynamics in open quantum systems or driven many-body models.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how finite-time orientational relaxation restructures the collective motion and phase transitions in polar active matter systems.

**Main result.** The system exhibits a rich sequence of structural phases (Isotropic $	o$ Polar Banded $	o$ Cross-Sea $	o$ Micro-clustered) driven by increasing alignment rate, with transitions being strongly first order.

**Method.** Large-scale numerical simulations and mean-field analysis are used to map the non-equilibrium phase diagram in terms of activity and alignment rates.

**Model / system.** The model consists of Vicsek-like active particles whose orientations evolve via a Langevin formulation that incorporates finite-rate relaxation towards the local mean direction, combining consensus dynamics with XY-like rotational diffusion.

**Key observables.** Polar order parameter ($\langle w angle$), density fluctuations ($\Deltaho$), structural order parameter ($\langle \ell angle$), and Binder cumulants for transition characterization.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Activity (Péclet number, $	ext{Pe}$) and effective alignment rate ($g = J/D_r$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on a specific Langevin formulation incorporating finite relaxation time, and some theoretical work uses mean-field approximations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the phase diagram in $(	ext{Pe}, g)$ space showing HI, PB, CS, HP, and mC regimes; local probability distributions transition from unimodal to bimodal near first-order transitions.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the Langevin dynamics, analyzes the resulting non-equilibrium phase diagram across varying activity parameters, characterizes multiple structural phases using order parameters, and concludes that finite relaxation acts as a key control parameter restructuring collective behavior.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce a Langevin formulation of Vicsek-like active particles in which orientations evolve through finite-rate relaxation toward the local mean direction, with alignment strength $J$ and rotational diffusivity $D_r$, thereby combining Vicsek-type local consensus with XY-like orientational dynamics. Using large-scale numerical simulations, we determine the nonequilibrium phase diagram as a function of activity and alignment rate. Increasing the alignment rate drives a sequence of transitions from a homogeneous isotropic state to polar bands, a cross-sea phase of intersecting bands, a homogeneous polar state, and ultimately a micro-clustered regime. The isotropic-to-polar transition is strongly first order, as evidenced by Binder cumulants and bimodal distributions of local polarization and density, indicating coexistence of gas-like and liquid-like regions. Near the onset of collective motion, band size increases with activity but depends non-monotonically on alignment rate. Further increasing the alignment rate drives the system through the cross-sea and homogeneous polar phases before enhanced density fluctuations lead to micro-clustering. Our results demonstrate that finite-time orientational relaxation acts as a control parameter that qualitatively restructures collective behavior in polar active matter.

</details>

### [Magnetic HIP-NN for spin dynamics in disordered itinerant magnets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10349v1)

**Summary.** This work introduces mHIP-NN, a magnetic neural network extension designed to simulate spin dynamics in disordered itinerant magnets. It overcomes computational barriers by learning the effective magnetic energy landscape and local torques directly from microscopic electronic calculations. The method successfully reproduces non-equilibrium spin correlation evolution, establishing a scalable ML framework for coupled atom-spin systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The framework provides a powerful ML tool for simulating complex many-body quantum dynamics (spin/electron coupling) that are typically intractable due to the need for repeated solving of electronic structure equations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an efficient, scalable framework to simulate electron-mediated spin dynamics in disordered itinerant magnets, circumventing the computational bottleneck of repeated exact electronic structure calculations.

**Main result.** The magnetic HIP-NN (mHIP-NN) accurately reproduces local torques and captures non-equilibrium spin correlation evolution by learning effective interactions directly from microscopic data.

**Method.** The authors employ a specialized Graph Neural Network architecture that incorporates rotationally invariant spin correlations into message-passing layers to construct a differentiable energy functional for coupled atom-spin dynamics.

**Model / system.** The system is disordered itinerant magnets governed by an s-d exchange Hamiltonian. The dynamics are simulated using the Landau-Lifshitz-Gilbert (LLG) equation, where effective magnetic fields arise from electronic structure calculations.

**Key observables.** Local torques ($\mathbf{T}_i$), non-equilibrium spatial spin correlations ($C(\mathbf{r}, t)$), and the characteristic correlation length $\xi(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The model handles structural disorder, itinerant electron hopping amplitudes (e.g., $e^{-|\mathbf{r}_i-\mathbf{r}_j|/l}$), and spin-spin coupling strengths.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the complex effective magnetic interactions can be accurately approximated by a differentiable ML potential trained on data generated from exact diagonalization simulations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the spatial decay of interaction terms (Hessian matrix elements) confirming locality, and show the time evolution of spin-spin correlation functions $C(\mathbf{r}, t)$ comparing ED-LLG to ML-LLG results.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces mHIP-NN for coupled atom-spin dynamics, details its graph structure using invariant descriptors ($r_{ij}, b_{ij}$), benchmarks it against LLG dynamics by predicting local torques, and finally demonstrates its ability to track non-equilibrium spin correlation growth ($\xi(t)$).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a magnetic extension of the Hierarchically Interacting Particle Neural Network (HIP-NN) that enables large-scale simulations of electron-mediated spin dynamics in disordered itinerant magnets. The resulting magnetic HIP-NN (mHIP-NN) incorporates rotationally invariant spin correlations directly into hierarchical message-passing layers, enabling the network to learn emergent magnetic energy landscapes and effective local fields from coupled geometric-spin environments while preserving spin-rotation symmetry. As a benchmark application, we consider structurally disordered itinerant $s$-$d$ exchange models in which the effective magnetic forces arise dynamically from the instantaneous electronic structure and are computationally prohibitive to evaluate using conventional exact-diagonalization-based approaches. We show that mHIP-NN accurately reproduces the local torques governing Landau-Lifshitz-Gilbert dynamics and faithfully captures the nonequilibrium evolution of spatial spin correlations following thermal quenches. Our results establish symmetry-aware hierarchical message-passing networks as an efficient and scalable framework for large-scale simulations of frustrated itinerant spin systems and nonequilibrium magnetic dynamics. More broadly, because the learned energy functional remains fully differentiable with respect to both atomic coordinates and spin variables, the framework also provides a natural foundation for spin-dependent interatomic potentials and coupled atom-spin dynamics.

</details>

<img src="2606.10548_figures/2606.10548_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4: System-size dependence of excitation-space convergence. Time evolution of the orbital entropy for periodic (a–c) and hard-wall (d–f) boundary conditions for systems of increasing size: 4×2 (a,d), 4×3 (b,e), and 4×4 (c,f). Grey, blue, and olive curves correspond to simulations restricted to n = 1, n = 2, and n = 3 particle–hole excitations, respectively. Low-order excitation spaces remain accurate for periodic graphene across system sizes, whereas hard-wall systems show increasing deviations and growing importance of higher-order correlations.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10548_figures/2606.10548_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5: Interaction dependence of excitation-space convergence for a 4 × 4 graphene flake. Time evolution of the orbital entropy is shown for periodic (a–d) and hard-wall (e–h) boundary conditions at increasing nearest-neighbor interaction strength U1 = 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, and 0.8 (from left to right), with U2 = U3 = 0. Grey, blue, and olive curves correspond to simulations restricted to n = 1, n = 2, and n = 3 particle–hole excitation sectors, respectively. Increasing interaction strength enhances higher-order contributions in all cases, but periodic systems retain near- convergence at low excitation order over a much wider parameter range than hard- wall systems.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper analyzes ultrafast carrier relaxation in interacting graphene flakes after an optical quench by comparing full time evolution against simplified models restricted to low-order excitations. It establishes that while periodic boundaries allow for good descriptions using few terms, hard-wall confinement necessitates including high-order correlations, providing a crucial benchmark for quantum simulation methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous, quantitative testbed for many theoretical approaches used in non-equilibrium quantum dynamics, particularly highlighting how boundary conditions can fundamentally alter the required complexity of the underlying physics description.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether interaction-driven ultrafast dynamics (carrier relaxation) following an optical quench in graphene can be accurately described using only low-order particle–hole excitation subspaces.

**Main result.** Convergence depends critically on geometry: periodic flakes are well-described by low-order excitations, whereas hard-wall confined geometries require substantial higher-order correlation contributions.

**Method.** The authors compare exact real-time evolution (full Fock space) against truncated simulations restricted to subspaces containing up to $n$ particle–hole excitations.

**Model / system.** The system is finite graphene flakes modeled by an interacting tight-binding Hamiltonian for spinless fermions. The dynamics are initiated via a single particle–hole excitation quench protocol.

**Key observables.** Single-particle orbital entropy ($S_{sp}$), which diagnoses dynamic correlation growth, and the occupation of the initially excited level ($	ilde{n}_p$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Boundary conditions (Periodic vs. Hard-wall), interaction strengths ($U_1, U_2, U_3$), and excitation order truncation limit ($n=1, 2, 3$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study tests the limits of low-order descriptions against full many-particle exact real-time dynamics; convergence is used as a diagnostic criterion for method applicability.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare time evolution of orbital entropy and excited level occupation across different excitation orders ($n=1, 2, 3$) for both periodic and hard-wall boundary conditions, highlighting the divergence in confined systems.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes a benchmark problem by simulating quench dynamics. It systematically compares full Fock-space evolution against increasingly truncated subspaces (low-order excitations), using entropy convergence as the primary diagnostic tool to map out the regime where simple approximations fail due to geometry or confinement.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study interaction-driven ultrafast dynamics in finite graphene flakes following an optical pump quench in an interacting tight-binding model. By comparing exact real-time evolution with simulations restricted to particle-hole excitation subspaces, we assess when relaxation can be captured by low-order many-body processes and when this is not sufficient. The single-particle orbital entropy provides a compact diagnostic for dynamic correlation growth. For the systems studied here, periodic graphene flakes are well described by low-order excitations, whereas confined geometries require substantial higher-order contributions even for relatively small interaction strengths. The quench protocol combines simple initial-state preparation with strongly correlated dynamics, identifying a promising benchmark problem for future quantum-computing simulations.

</details>

### [Nonequilibrium Green Functions Simulations for Large Correlated Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10773v1)

**Authors:** Erik Schroedter, Michael Bonitz, Jan-Philip Joost

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10773_figures/2606.10773_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. Levels of self-consistency for the different approaches. Here, “fully self-consistent” refers to the self-consistent solu- tion of the KBEs, for any of the discussed self-energy ap- proximations. Ξ denotes the contribution arising from the collision term inside the integral term of the BSE (18) and is of the form Ξ ∼ R IGwχ. Application of the GKBA is equivalent to neglecting Ξ. Within the δNEGF approach, the R term is neglected what constitutes an intermediate level of self-consistency. Finally, “mean-field propagators” refers to inserting single-particle NEGFs at the mean-field level into the BSE (“one-shot” scheme).</figcaption>

**Summary.** This paper introduces $\delta$NEGF, a novel quantum-fluctuation formulation for Nonequilibrium Green Functions. It solves the critical scaling bottleneck faced by previous methods when simulating correlated systems over long times or large unit cells. This breakthrough enables accurate study of complex dynamics in materials like Hubbard lattices and graphene nanoribbons.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a scalable theoretical tool to study non-equilibrium dynamics in strongly correlated materials that were previously computationally inaccessible due to the exponential growth of required correlation functions.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is accessing accurate, real-time dynamics for large, spatially inhomogeneous quantum systems where electron correlations are strong.

**Main result.** The proposed $\delta$NEGF method successfully extends dynamical self-energy simulations to basis sizes of $N_b \sim 10^4$, overcoming the severe scaling bottlenecks of previous NEGF implementations.

**Method.** It introduces a quantum-fluctuation formulation ($\delta$NEGF) that represents two-particle correlations via fluctuations of field operators, avoiding explicit storage of the full two-particle Green function.

**Model / system.** The theory applies to general correlated fermionic systems, benchmarked on lattice models like the 2D Hubbard lattice and graphene nanoribbon heterostructures with long-range Coulomb interactions.

**Key observables.** Dynamical self-energies (GW, T-matrix), charge migration dynamics, and ultrafast relaxation processes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Basis size ($N_b$) up to $10^4$, time steps ($N_t$), coupling strength (strong coupling regime).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method relies on a quantum-fluctuation formulation that guarantees stable dynamics by preserving the positivity of reduced density matrices, and uses stochastic low-rank decomposition for efficiency.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds upon existing NEGF frameworks (like G1–G2) to address computational scaling limits. It introduces $\delta$NEGF by reformulating correlations through fluctuations ($\delta \hat{G}$), which allows for time-linear scaling and large basis sizes, culminating in benchmarks on physical systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Correlated real-time dynamics in large, spatially inhomogeneous quantum systems remain difficult to access with nonequilibrium many-body methods. Two-time nonequilibrium Green functions (NEGF) retain dynamical correlations but their computational runtime grows cubically with the number of time steps $N_\mathrm{t}$. This scaling bottleneck could recently be overcome by introducing the G1--G2 scheme that is linear in $N_\mathrm{t}$, but requires propagation of a two-particle correlation function and may suffer from numerical instabilities. This has restricted simulations to small systems with $N_\mathrm{b} \sim 10^2$ basis states. Here we introduce a quantum-fluctuation formulation of nonequilibrium Green functions, denoted $δ$NEGF, that represents dynamical two-particle correlations through fluctuations of field-operator products, $δ\hat G$. This guarantees stable dynamics by preserving the positivity of the reduced density matrices, avoids the explicit storage of the two-particle Green function, and reduces the propagation to a finite ensemble of Hartree-Fock-like trajectories. Combined with a stochastic low-rank decomposition of the correlation functions, the method retains time-linear scaling while extending dynamical $GW$ and particle-particle and particle-hole $T$-matrix simulations to basis sizes of order $N_\mathrm{b}\sim 10^4$. We benchmark $δ$NEGF against exact and HF-GKBA results for lattice systems, finding stable correlated dynamics also at strong coupling. We further demonstrate large-scale simulations of diffusion in two-dimensional Hubbard lattices and ultrafast relaxation in graphene nanoribbon heterostructures with long-range Coulomb interactions. These results establish $δ$NEGF as a scalable route to dynamical self-energy simulations of large, spatially inhomogeneous correlated quantum systems beyond the reach of existing NEGF implementations.

</details>

### [Hybrid Hamiltonian-diagrammatic quantum impurity solver](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11095v1)

**Summary.** This work presents a novel hybrid computational method to solve complex quantum impurity models central to strongly correlated electron physics. By augmenting diagrammatic techniques with a small set of counterterm bath sites, the authors achieve unprecedented precision and convergence speed compared to established methods. This establishes a practical route for high-accuracy theoretical predictions in materials like those exhibiting strong electronic correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter, the technique of systematically improving convergence by adding auxiliary degrees of freedom to stabilize a perturbative expansion is highly relevant for open quantum system dynamics where bath coupling must be accurately modeled.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is solving quantum impurity models, which describe electron interactions coupled to a non-interacting bath, requiring advanced non-perturbative numerical methods.

**Main result.** The proposed hybrid approach successfully combines diagrammatic expansions with auxiliary bath levels (counterterms) to achieve high precision in solving these models, surpassing existing benchmark calculations by orders of magnitude.

**Method.** It augments standard diagrammatic impurity solvers by introducing a small set of counterterm bath sites. This reduces the residual problem to a regime where low-order perturbation theory converges rapidly and accurately.

**Model / system.** The primary model is the quantum impurity model, tested first on a spinless Anderson impurity model coupled to a semi-circular bath, and later extended to an interacting two-orbital model relevant for Hund's metal physics.

**Key observables.** Green's function $G(	au)$ and its deviation from the exact solution $|G(	au) - G_{	ext{Exact}}(	au)|$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The number of counterterms ($n_{CT}$) and the order of perturbation theory ($k$) are key tuning parameters used to control convergence.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes that augmenting the bath with a small, carefully chosen set of auxiliary levels (counterterms) can systematically reduce the residual error sufficiently for low-order perturbation theory to become highly accurate and rapidly converging.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare $G(	au)$ and its deviation from exact results across varying numbers of counterterms ($n_{CT}$) and expansion orders ($k$), demonstrating superior convergence compared to bare series expansions, especially in strongly correlated regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem (quantum impurity solvers), details the limitations of existing methods (Hamiltonian vs. Diagrammatic), proposes the hybrid methodology using counterterms, validates it on simple benchmarks (spinless model), and finally tests its efficacy on complex, interacting systems exhibiting sign problems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum impurity models, which describe the coupling between interacting orbitals and a non-interacting bath, play a central role in the physics of strongly correlated electron systems. Solving a quantum impurity model in general requires the use of non-perturbative numerical methods. Hamiltonian-based approaches, which rely on an explicit bath discretization, are typically limited to a small number of bath sites or small entanglement, and diagrammatic methods suffer from sign problems, slow convergence, or diagram truncation approximations. Here we show that these two classes of methods can be combined: augmenting diagrammatic methods with a small auxiliary bath can reduce the residual problem to a regime where low-order perturbation theory is highly accurate and rapidly converging. In a simple benchmark, the precision of the hybrid approach surpasses bold-line calculations by several orders of magnitude; for a strongly interacting two-orbital model with a severe sign problem, convergence is achieved at three orders of magnitude lower computational cost than competing methods; and convergence to the unknown exact result is rapidly accelerated in a difficult realistic problem. Our results establish a practical route to high-precision quantum impurity solutions in correlated quantum systems.

</details>

### [Quantum resources in non-stoquastic quantum annealing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.09995v1)

**Authors:** Chiara Capecci, Sebastian Nagies, Naga Dileep Varikuti, Philipp Hauke

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**
