**Summary.** This paper investigates whether introducing non-stoquastic terms into quantum annealing can provide a genuine speedup over classical simulation. By calculating entanglement and stabilizer Rényi entropy, the authors demonstrate that such protocols enhance these quantum resources when they successfully modify phase transitions from first to second order. This suggests that achieving quantum advantage requires generating significant, measurable quantum correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous theoretical framework linking the presence of specific Hamiltonian terms (non-stoquasticity) directly to measurable quantum complexity measures (entanglement/SRE), which is crucial for understanding hardware limitations in quantum computation.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is determining if non-stoquastic quantum annealing protocols can achieve a true quantum advantage for optimization problems over classical simulation methods.

**Main result.** Non-stoquastic catalysts can modify the energy landscape to change first-order phase transitions into second-order ones, leading to potential exponential speedups in quantum performance.

**Method.** The authors analyze this by computing quantum resources—entanglement entropy and stabilizer Rényi entropy—and comparing their scaling against the minimum spectral gap across different annealing paths.

**Model / system.** The study uses two benchmark models: the fully connected p-spin model and a geometrically local Ising model, analyzed via time-dependent Hamiltonians incorporating non-stoquastic catalyst terms.

**Key observables.** Minimum Energy Gap ($\Delta E$), Bipartite Entanglement Entropy, Stabilizer Rényi Entropy (SRE), and Magnetization $\langle m_x angle$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The annealing path parameters $(s, \lambda)$, the order $p$ of the p-spin model, and the catalyst strength $\lambda$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that quantum computational resources (entanglement/SRE) quantify the difficulty for classical simulation methods like tensor networks.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate how entanglement entropy and SRE scale differently along annealing paths crossing first-order versus second-order transitions in both the p-spin and local Ising models.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically compares stoquastic (QMC simulable) vs. non-stoquastic protocols, analyzing resource scaling for two distinct spin models to show that quantum performance improvements correlate with enhanced quantum resources.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum annealing promises to solve combinatorial optimization problems by preparing the ground state of a target Hamiltonian. Standard annealing protocols are, however, stoquastic and can thus be simulated by sign-problem-free quantum Monte-Carlo methods. To obtain a true quantum advantage, it has been proposed to use non-stoquastic catalyst Hamiltonians. Active only at intermediate stages of the protocol, these can, for certain problems, convert first-order into second-order quantum phase transitions and thus permit an exponential speedup over the stoquastic protocol. At the same time, the non-stoquastic catalyst renders quantum Monte-Carlo methods inefficient. It remains, however, an open question how other classical computation methods are affected by the non-stoquastic terms. We address this question by computing quantum resources -- entanglement entropy and stabilizer Rényi entropy -- whose presence makes classical computations based on tensor networks and stabilizer-tableau methods exponentially hard. We compare these with the spectral gap along the annealing path for two paradigmatic benchmark models, the fully connected $p$-spin model and a geometrically local Ising model. While the exact behavior shows a subtle dependency on the underlying model and the annealing path, our numerics suggest consistently that the scaling of entanglement and non-stabilizerness is at least maintained in the deeply non-stoquastic regime and in some cases even significantly enhanced. Our results thus suggest that improvements of quantum performance in non-stoquastic annealing coincide with significant presence of quantum computational resources.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10422"></a>
### [Supermoiré Chern mosaic in helical trilayer WSe2](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10422v1)

**Authors:** Zhenyu Wang, Mingjie Zhang, Hai Meng, Xiuzhen Li, Subi Du, Yaotian Liu, Siyu Fan, Xiaofan Shi, Kenji Watanabe, Takashi Taniguchi, Wei Yang, Guangyu Zhang, Bingbing Tong, Guangtong Liu, Li Lu, Jie Shen, Gang Li, Jing Song, Enke Liu, Song Liu, Fengcheng Wu, Yang Xu

**Summary.** This work reports on the magnetotransport properties of helical trilayer WSe2, revealing that its moiré superlattice structure creates a 'Chern mosaic' with distinct topological domains. The authors demonstrate novel magnetic and topological insulating states—including a ferromagnetic state at $\nu=-1$ and a field-induced Chern insulator—establishing this material as a platform for studying spatially organized topology beyond simple bilayer models.

**Why it may be interesting.** The concept of a 'Chern mosaic' arising from real-space structural relaxation provides an analogy to spatially modulated order parameters, which is relevant for understanding emergent phenomena in strongly correlated systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the unexplored correlated and topological transport properties arising from helical trilayer WSe2 moiré superlattices, moving beyond simple bilayer models.

**Main result.** The system exhibits a robust ferromagnetic insulating state at $\nu = -1$ due to a time-reversal-symmetry-breaking supermoiré Chern mosaic, and a distinct field-induced Chern insulator ($C=1$) emerges near $\nu = -2/3$.

**Method.** The research combines dual-gated magnetotransport measurements with continuum moiré Hamiltonian calculations to map out the topological phase diagram.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is helical trilayer WSe2 ($	ext{HTWSe}_2$), which forms a relaxed supermoiré landscape composed of inequivalent local topological domains (e.g., $\text{ab}$ and $\text{ba}$).

**Key observables.** Anomalous Hall response ($R_{xy}$), longitudinal resistance ($R_{xx}$), Chern numbers ($C$), effective mass ($m^*$), and Curie temperature ($T_C$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Moiré filling factor ($\nu$), displacement field ($D$), and external magnetic field ($B$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that local structural reconstruction dictates the formation of distinct topological domains whose contributions lead to measurable macroscopic transport properties.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate real-space supermoiré patterns, phase diagrams mapping insulating/magnetic states vs. $\nu$ and $D$, and field evolution plots showing transitions between different Chern insulating regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically explores the electronic structure by first establishing the supermoiré landscape (theory/calculation), then characterizing zero-field magnetic/insulating phases ($\nu=-1$), followed by mapping out high-field topological reconstructions ($C=1$ at $\nu=-2/3$).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Helically twisted multilayers offer access to moiré physics beyond the single-superlattice paradigm, yet their correlated and topological transport properties remain largely unexplored in semiconductor moiré materials. Here we report magnetotransport measurements of helical trilayer WSe2, in which two coupled moiré patterns relax into a supermoiré landscape composed of inequivalent local topological domains with distinct electronic structures and unequal spatial areas. By electrostatic tuning, we identify a trilayer-hybridized regime where interactions and real-space reconstruction combine to generate a plethora of magnetic and topological states absent in the twisted bilayers. At moiré filling factor $ν$ = -1, we observe a ferromagnetic insulating state that is robust against magnetic field and accompanied by a non-quantized anomalous Hall response ~-4 kOhms. This behaviour is consistent with a time-reversal-symmetry-breaking supermoiré Chern mosaic, in which the Hall response arises from the non-cancelling contributions of local domains with opposite Chern character arranged by the relaxed structure. Under strong magnetic fields, a symmetry-broken Chern insulating state (C = 1) emerges near $ν$ = -2/3, displaying a much larger positive Hall response together with strongly enhanced longitudinal resistance, suggestive of field-reconstructed topological minibands and domain-boundary scattering. These results establish relaxed supermoiré semiconductor trilayers as a platform for spatially organized magnetism and topology beyond the bilayer limit.

</details>

### [Dynamical Partition Functions of Stochastic Dynamics via Variational Flows](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10757v1)

**Summary.** This paper introduces a novel neural variational framework that computes the dynamical partition function for stochastic processes by reformulating the problem as an optimization task. By using Normalizing Flows within a TDVP scheme, it accurately calculates key non-equilibrium observables like work and entropy production in high-dimensional systems. This offers a scalable alternative to traditional path integral methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides powerful, scalable numerical tools to calculate non-equilibrium quantities like entropy production and free energy differences in complex systems where traditional path integral methods fail due to dimensionality.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is computing the dynamical partition function for continuous-state stochastic dynamics, which governs non-equilibrium thermodynamics.

**Main result.** The authors develop a mesh-free neural variational framework that successfully computes finite-time and asymptotic trajectory thermodynamics in high dimensions with high accuracy.

**Method.** They reframe the problem as minimizing the KL divergence of a tilted Fokker-Planck evolution, parameterizing the evolving density using Normalizing Flows (NF) within a Time-Dependent Variational Principle (TDVP) framework.

**Model / system.** The method applies to general continuous-state stochastic systems described by SDEs, such as Ornstein-Uhlenbeck processes and high-dimensional overdamped Langevin dynamics. The analysis is validated across various dimensions up to 100.

**Key observables.** Dynamical partition function ($Z_t(\lambda)$), work, entropy production, current fluctuations, Scaled Cumulant Generating Function (SCGF) $\psi(\lambda)$, and the rate function $I(a)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The noise strength ($D$), the path observable weighting ($\lambda$), and system dimensionality ($d$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework relies on parameterizing the evolving density using Normalizing Flows (NF) and treating the evolution as a variational inference problem.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the computational flow involving the tilted FPE, NF ansatz, and the variational training loop minimizing KL divergence. Later figures show tracking of mean dynamics and covariance for high-dimensional systems.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the theoretical mapping to a time-dependent optimization problem, details the Normalizing Flow/TDVP implementation using gradient descent on the tangent space, validates it across multiple physical benchmarks (OU process, $S^1$, 16D), and finally analyzes computational scaling.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nonequilibrium thermodynamics is governed by the dynamical partition function, and its computation in high-dimensional continuous-state dynamics is a longstanding challenge. The Feynman-Kac formalism provides a rigorous representation for generating functions of arbitrary path observables; however, practical evaluation beyond low dimensions or the weak-noise limit is hindered by the curse of dimensionality and the exponentially growing replica demands of trajectory-based methods. Here we develop a mesh-free neural variational framework that realizes the Feynman-Kac theorem with generative flow models, recasting tilted stochastic evolution as a time-dependent optimization problem. This approach enables the direct computation of both finite-time and asymptotic trajectory thermodynamics in a unified manner. The method applies to general observables, enabling the evaluation of work, entropy production, and current fluctuations. We demonstrate the accuracy and scalability of this method in various nonequilibrium systems including high-dimensional cases.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper theoretically investigates how Majorana zero modes emerging from two-channel Kondo impurities can be coherently manipulated for quantum information processing. By analyzing adiabatic evolution in specific geometries (Y-junctions), the authors demonstrate that braiding operations realize non-Abelian geometric holonomy, offering a robust platform for topological quantum gates.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the realization of topological quantum computation using strongly correlated electron physics, providing concrete theoretical pathways for controlling exotic anyonic states beyond simple free-fermion models.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research focuses on achieving coherent manipulation of Majorana zero modes (MZMs) within two-channel Kondo setups to realize robust topological quantum operations.

**Main result.** The study establishes a proof-of-principle route for controlling non-Abelian anyons, demonstrating that braiding and teleportation can be achieved using the geometric holonomy associated with Y-junction geometries in these interacting systems.

**Method.** The analysis employs theoretical techniques including mapping to compactified lattice models, adiabatic evolution protocols, and calculating Berry/Wilczek-Zee holonomies. The system dynamics are governed by complex Hamiltonians derived from Kondo interactions.

**Model / system.** The physical model is the two-impurity two-channel Kondo (2CK) system coupled to chiral edge states, which is mathematically mapped onto a compactified tight-binding chain of Majorana fermions. This setup allows for the emergence of interacting Kondo MZMs.

**Key observables.** Ground State Degeneracy (GSD), Rabi oscillations, non-Abelian Berry holonomy, and fidelity of teleportation operations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Kondo coupling asymmetry ($J_L/J_R$), chain length parity ($N$), and the degree of channel anisotropy ($\Delta H$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on mapping to a compactified description, which makes non-local degrees of freedom operationally accessible, and approximations inherent in bosonization techniques.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the two-impurity 2CK setup, energy level diagrams showing Rabi oscillations, GSD dependence on asymmetry, and schematic representations of Majorana fusion and braiding operations.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by defining the Hamiltonian for the 2CK system, analyzing its ground state degeneracy (GSD) via ED, demonstrating coherent control through adiabatic protocols (teleportation/fusion), and finally establishing that braiding is realized as a non-Abelian geometric holonomy in Y-junction geometries.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study coherent manipulation of Majorana zero modes emerging in overscreened two-channel Kondo systems. Using compactified lattice models, we show that these interacting Kondo Majoranas support non-local qubits and admit teleportation, fusion, and braiding operations. In particular, we identify a distinction between non-topological and genuinely topological Y-junction geometries, the latter realizing a non-Abelian geometric holonomy. Our results establish a proof-of-principle route toward coherent control of non-Abelian anyons beyond conventional free-fermion platforms.

</details>

### [Graviton-mediated entanglement due to light bending from a quantum rotor](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.09991v1)

**Authors:** Dripto Biswas, Sougato Bose, Anupam Mazumdar, Marko Toroš  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.09991v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.09991_figures/2606.09991_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Schematic of the setup showing the various quantities defined in Sec. III. At the centre there is a mass in a ground state initially |0⟩, rotating counterclockwise with an angular momentum J. The opti- cal beam (purple) lies in the XY plane, and the tangential direction senses (σ = ±1), e.g, the photon beam can prograde and retrograde with respect to the rotation of the mass at the centre. We assume that the photon is in a coherent state |α⟩.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper explores how the quantum nature of gravity can be tested by measuring entanglement between a photon and a spinning mechanical rotor. The interaction is mediated by virtual gravitons, leading to corrections in the system's Hamiltonian proportional to the rotor's angular momentum $J$. The key prediction is that the difference in entanglement entropy for clockwise versus counter-clockwise light propagation directly measures this spin-dependent gravitational coupling.

**Why it may be interesting.** It bridges quantum information theory (entanglement measures) with fundamental physics (quantum gravity effects), providing concrete, measurable predictions based on rotational dynamics in an optomechanical setting.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates testing the quantum nature of gravity by calculating graviton-mediated entanglement between a photon and the spatial position of a quantum rotor.

**Main result.** The difference in linear entanglement entropies for prograde versus retrograde photon motion provides a measurable signature proportional to the rotor's angular momentum, $J$.

**Method.** The analysis uses quantum field theory techniques applied to an optomechanical setup, deriving effective Hamiltonians and calculating entanglement entropy using Gaussian approximations.

**Model / system.** The system is an optomechanical setup involving a photon mode (optical cavity) coupled to a mechanical oscillator (quantum rotor) via the exchange of virtual gravitons. The interaction strength depends on the rotor's angular momentum $J$.

**Key observables.** Gravitationally induced entanglement entropy, specifically the difference in linear entanglement entropies ($\Delta S$) for prograde and retrograde motion.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Angular momentum ($J$), coupling constants ($g_0$), mechanical frequency ($\omega_m$), and cavity field amplitude ($\alpha$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the semi-classical limit for $J$ (or treats it as a classical background) and focuses on leading order corrections linear in $J$. It also ignores decoherence effects.

**Figures summary.** Not specified by notes, but calculations involve deriving potentials ($V(d)$), coupling strengths ($g(J)_{0,\sigma}$), and entanglement entropy evolution over time ($	ext{Tr} ho_b^2(t)$).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the theoretical framework using off-shell graviton amplitudes, deriving effective potentials incorporating spin corrections, constructing a full Hamiltonian for the coupled system, and finally calculating the entanglement entropy difference as the key observable.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

One of the key tests of the quantum nature of gravity is to test whether the virtual mediator of gravity between matter and photon gives rise to the quantum light-bending phenomenon. The off-shell degrees of freedom, involving the spin-2 and spin-0 components of graviton, reproduce the classical deviation of light rays, as well as have been predicted to generate entanglement between matter and photon. This paper explores the generation of entanglement due to the quantum gravitational interaction in an optomechanical setup with a quantum rotor and photon. The virtual exchange of a graviton provides entanglement between the photon degrees of freedom and the spatial position of the quantum rotor, with the rotational state affecting its magnitude. We analyze the case of a high spinning rotor, in an approximately classical state of angular momentum, and quantify its effect on the gravitationally induced entanglement between the photon and the position of the quantum rotor. We show that the difference in the linear entanglement entropies, of prograde-and-retrograde motion of the photon with respect to the quantum rotor, provide tangible observable consequences.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper presents a novel, camera-enabled homodyne detection technique to overcome the scalability limitations of measuring quantum light. By utilizing CCD arrays, they can simultaneously measure quadrature outcomes for dozens of optical modes using extremely low local oscillator power. This breakthrough enables direct experimental verification of entanglement and squeezing in large-scale multimode quantum states.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a highly scalable experimental platform for quantum measurement that directly addresses a major bottleneck in realizing large-scale quantum information processing protocols like quantum error correction or complex quantum simulations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge addressed is achieving scalable, efficient quantum measurement, specifically homodyne detection, necessary for large-scale quantum technologies.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated a camera-based system capable of simultaneously measuring quadrature outcomes for 60 optical modes with significantly reduced local oscillator power and negligible crosstalk.

**Method.** They leverage the pixel array of a CCD camera to perform weighted summations of photocurrents, enabling simultaneous measurement in a superposed mode basis without extra interferometers.

**Model / system.** The system uses a camera-coupled homodyne detector setup to measure quantum light generated by sources like SPOPOs. It is designed to handle multimode states and test entanglement criteria on these measured quadratures.

**Key observables.** Quadrature outcomes ($\hat{x}, \hat{p}$), squeezing variances, entanglement indicators (e.g., nullifier variances), and correlations in superposed modes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Scalability to 60+ modes; nanowatt-level local oscillator power per mode (a six-order-of-magnitude reduction); clearance exceeding 24 dB.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes shot-noise-limited operation is achieved, and the low LO power is attributed to the CCD camera's readout characteristics.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the camera-based detection scheme mapping modes to pixels, show noise measurements for superposed modes testing entanglement criteria (Duan criterion), and detail protocols for verifying multipartite entangled states like GHZ and cluster states.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scalability problem in quantum measurement, details the novel camera-based homodyne setup, demonstrates its ability to measure nonclassical noise (squeezing/entanglement) in multimode light, and finally applies this technique to verify multipartite entanglement and perform conditional state preparation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Scalability is a key challenge in advancing quantum technologies such as quantum computing, communication, and metrology. Photonic systems offer a promising route to scalability by enabling the deterministic generation of large-scale entangled states. Homodyne detection is an essential quantum measurement to exploit such entangled states, enabling quantum-enhanced measurement, deterministic quantum teleportation, GKP-state breeding, and quantum error correction. Despite the recent progress in generating large-scale quantum states, realizing quantum measurement at scale remains a major challenge. Here we realize scalable and efficient homodyne detection by leveraging a large number of pixels in a charge-coupled-device (CCD) camera. Our approach enables shot-noise-limited quadrature measurements of 60 optical modes simultaneously, while requiring only nanowatt-level local oscillator power per mode -- a six-order-of-magnitude reduction compared to conventional methods. The system achieves clearance exceeding 24 dB for all modes with negligible crosstalk. We demonstrate its compatibility with a large-scale quantum state by directly observing squeezing and entanglement in 60 optical modes. Furthermore, we showcase applications in verifying multipartite entanglement and in the conditional preparation of multimode states. This work provides a scalable method for quantum measurement, paving the way for large-scale quantum information processing.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.10003"></a>
### [Exactly solvable non-planar $\mathbb{Z}_{2}$ dimer liquids on checkerboard and ruby lattices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10003v1)

**Summary.** This work develops an advanced theoretical framework for exactly solvable quantum dimer models on non-planar lattices like the checkerboard and ruby. By mapping these systems onto $\mathbb{Z}_2$ lattice gauge theories, the authors confirm their robust topological nature, calculating key observables like entanglement entropy and torus partition functions.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit mapping of a dimer model Hamiltonian onto a pure-flux $\mathbb{Z}_2$ lattice gauge theory provides concrete, solvable examples of topological order relevant to frustrated magnetism and quantum spin liquids.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research generalizes exactly solvable quantum dimer models from planar (trivalent) to non-planar (tetravalent) parent lattices, extending the study of $\mathbb{Z}_2$ topological phases.

**Main result.** They establish a broad framework for crossed-medial quantum dimer models on lattices like the checkerboard and ruby lattice, showing they map exactly onto $\mathbb{Z}_2$ gauge theories (like the Toric Code).

**Method.** The authors employ generalized Kasteleyn methods and an 'arrow formalism' to construct and analyze the Hamiltonians for these non-planar systems.

**Model / system.** The system is a Quantum Dimer Model (QDM) defined on crossed-medial lattices, which are dual to Ising Gauge Theories. The model exhibits $\mathbb{Z}_2$ topological order, characterized by deconfined vison excitations.

**Key observables.** Topological Entanglement Entropy (TEE), torus partition function ($Z_{\text{torus}}$), and the energy gap for vison pair creation ($E_{\Delta} = 4\gamma$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Link weights ($x, y, z$) and lattice geometry parameters defining planarity vs. crossed plaquettes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on extending Kasteleyn methods to non-planar graphs and assuming specific isotropic weight limits for closed-form solutions.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the local constraints (e.g., forbidden parallel dimers) and provide diagrams detailing the arrow representation, resonance moves, and the underlying square lattice structure used for gauge theory mapping.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the generalized framework on crossed-medial lattices, then applying it to the checkerboard lattice to show equivalence with the Toric Code via an arrow formalism. It concludes by calculating exact torus partition functions and TEE, confirming the $\mathbb{Z}_2$ topological nature.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We generalize an influential framework for exactly solvable quantum dimer models with dual Ising gauge theory descriptions realizing the $\mathbb{Z}_{2}$ topological phase from trivalent to tetravalent parent lattices. The resulting quantum dimer models live on crossed-medial lattices and possess the remarkable feature of having crossed plaquettes, leading to transition graphs with generically intersecting loops. This non-planar structure runs counter to established templates for analytically tractable dimer liquids. As the simplest realization of the construction, we introduce an exactly solvable quantum dimer model on the checkerboard lattice that allows an exact mapping to the toric code, thus providing a particularly direct connection between the latter and Anderson's short range resonating valence bond paradigm. We further show that a corresponding crossed ruby lattice construction, dual to an Ising gauge theory on the kagome lattice, naturally falls within the same framework. More generally, the construction gives rise to a broad class of exactly solvable crossed-medial quantum dimer models and admits natural iteration, generating cascades of solvable $\mathbb{Z}_{2}$ topological dimer liquids beyond the standard planar setting. We furthermore extend Kasteleyn methods to the relevant non-planar graphs, enabling controlled wave-function deformations away from the commuting-projector points while retaining efficient evaluation of correlation functions.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper theoretically analyzes the transport properties, especially nonlinear shot noise, in an SU(N) Kondo system using a sophisticated combination of Slave Boson Mean Field Theory and Fermi Liquid theory. It shows how higher-order correlation functions, beyond simple two-body terms, become essential for accurately describing current fluctuations away from equilibrium. The results advance the understanding of correlated electron physics in quantum dots by predicting measurable fractional noise signatures.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides advanced theoretical tools (FLT extensions to slave bosons) to calculate non-equilibrium quantum noise signatures that are highly sensitive indicators of strong electronic correlations and symmetry structure.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates transport properties, including nonlinear conductance and fractional shot noise, in an SU(N) Kondo system operating in the strong coupling regime.

**Main result.** It demonstrates that while linear noise is captured by mean-field approaches, accurate description of nonlinear noise requires incorporating three-body correlation functions when far from electron-hole symmetry.

**Method.** The analysis combines the Kotliar-Ruckentein slave boson approach (SBMFA) with Fermi Liquid Theory (FLT) to calculate higher-order corrections beyond mean-field approximations.

**Model / system.** The system is a multi-level interacting quantum dot (N-QD) modeled by an N-level Anderson Hamiltonian, analyzed for SU(N) symmetries up to N=6.

**Key observables.** Linear and nonlinear conductance, susceptibilities ($\chi^{(2)}, \chi^{[3]}$), and fractional shot noise ($S$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Kondo temperature ($T_K$), degeneracy $N$, bias voltage ($V$), and Coulomb interaction strength ($U$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on extending the SBMFA by incorporating Fermi liquid corrections, which is necessary because the basic SBMFA neglects crucial fluctuations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate slave boson amplitudes and fluctuation parameters ($\delta E_
u$) as a function of bias voltage ($V$), and show how generalized Wilson ratios characterize correlation strengths.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the SBMFA framework for linear response, to applying FLT corrections for nonlinear transport, culminating in explicit formulas for shot noise dependent on higher-order correlations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider transport through a multi-level interacting quantum dot (N-QD) in Kondo regime. Using the Kotliar-Ruckentein slave boson approach (SBMFA) for N-level Anderson model we define effectively noninteracting quasiparticles of the SU(N) Kondo system ($N=2,3,4,5,6$). Kondo resonance transmission coefficients determine linear noise describing quasiparticle partitioning. To discuss nonlinear conductance, susceptibilities and shot noise in the strong coupling regime, we apply Fermi liquid theory with parameters expressed by susceptibilities of pseudofermions determined within SBMFA. Nonlinear shot noise is dominated by two-quasiparticle scattering. However, we demonstrate that for occupation regions distant from the electron-hole symmetry point, the role of three-body correlations must be revealed.

</details>

### [Magic and entanglement in 1+1-dimensional SU(2) lattice gauge theory](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.09971v1)

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This work investigates how two distinct measures of quantum complexity—entanglement and non-stabilizerness ('magic')—behave in the SU(2) lattice gauge theory. By calculating these quantities using tensor networks, the authors identify a crossover point where entanglement and magic decouple as the system moves from a critical state towards confinement. This provides theoretical insight into how quantum correlations are suppressed in physical gauge theories.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit calculation of 'magic' (non-stabilizerness) alongside standard entanglement entropy provides a deeper diagnostic tool for characterizing quantum resources relevant to fault-tolerant quantum computation in strongly interacting systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand the interplay between entanglement (non-local correlations) and non-stabilizerness ('magic') in quantum field theories to pinpoint regimes where quantum advantage might emerge.

**Main result.** A clear decoupling of entanglement entropy and magic is observed across the confinement crossover in the SU(2) lattice gauge theory, suggesting different physical mechanisms suppress these two measures of quantum complexity.

**Method.** The authors calculate gauge-invariant entanglement entropy and stabilizer Rényi entropy using tensor networks on a dressed-site basis that enforces Gauss's law exactly.

**Model / system.** The system is the (1+1)-dimensional SU(2) lattice gauge theory with matter fields, analyzed in various coupling regimes ($g$ and $m$). The Hamiltonian is formulated in an effective irrep-truncated dressed-site basis.

**Key observables.** Gauge-invariant entanglement entropy ($S$), stabilizer Rényi entropy ($\mathcal{M}_n$), particle density ($ho_{	ext{particle}}$), and anti-flatness ($	ext{A}_{	ext{F}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling constants $g$ and $m$; the crossover coupling $g^\star$; system size $L$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on restricting the Hilbert space to physical states satisfying local Gauss's law within an irrep-truncated basis, and utilizing tensor network methods for large systems.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate how entanglement entropy components decompose ($S = S_{	ext{classical}} + S_{	ext{quantum}} + S_{	ext{edge}}$) and map out the behavior of $S$, $\mathcal{M}_2/L$, and $ho_{	ext{particle}}$ across the coupling plane, highlighting the crossover point $g^\star$.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the physical model using a dressed-site basis to enforce Gauss's law. It then calculates key diagnostics like entanglement entropy and stabilizer Rényi entropy. Finally, it analyzes how these measures behave across different coupling regimes, identifying the crossover point $g^\star$ where the interplay between magic and entanglement changes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Entanglement and non-stabilizerness (magic) quantify two distinct departures of quantum systems from classical description: the former measures non-local correlations, while the latter measures the deviation from stabilizer states that can be efficiently simulated classically. Understanding magic in physically relevant quantum field theories is essential for identifying where quantum advantage may be realized in the early fault-tolerant quantum computing era. We calculate the gauge-invariant entanglement entropy and stabilizer Rényi entropy of the ground state of the (1+1)-dimensional SU(2) lattice gauge theory formulated in a dressed-site basis that enforces Gauss's law exactly. Using tensor networks, we obtain results for system sizes up to $L=100$ (300 qubits). We find a crossover denoted by $g_{\star}$ where the ground state passes from a more magic-rich regime into a regime with less magic; this is also tracked by the sharpest change of both the entanglement entropy and lattice particle density. Our large-scale study of non-stabilizerness and entanglement entropy in a non-Abelian lattice gauge theory with matter provides new insight into the interplay of magic and entanglement in gauge theories, both of which are relevant for classical and early fault-tolerant quantum simulations.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a reconfigurable Quantum Key Distribution (QKD) platform that uses electro-optic modulation on a single laser source to operate over 20 km of fiber. It successfully demonstrates high photon indistinguishability necessary for quantum interference, while also proving the ability to switch between MDI-QKD and BB84 protocols by adjusting only one half-wave plate. This significantly enhances hardware efficiency for future quantum networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and photonic QI as it presents a practical engineering solution for building versatile quantum communication hardware using established components like EOMs and CW lasers.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal was developing a practical, reconfigurable quantum key distribution (QKD) platform capable of switching between MDI-QKD and BB84 protocols using shared hardware to minimize system redundancy.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrated high indistinguishability ($V \approx 0.5$) over 20 km channels and achieved seamless reconfiguration between MDI-QKD and BB84 modes by rotating a single half-wave plate, confirming robust operational flexibility.

**Method.** The system utilized electro-optic modulation (EOM) on a shared CW laser to generate two mutually phase-randomized weak coherent states. Performance was characterized using time-resolved Hong–Ou–Mandel (HOM) interference and by measuring Quantum Bit Error Rates (QBERs) in both protocols.

**Model / system.** The platform is an EOM-based QKD system operating over 20 km optical fiber, designed to support both MDI-QKD (via partial Bell-state measurement) and BB84. Reconfigurability is achieved by manipulating a single Half-Wave Plate (HWP) in the central module.

**Key observables.** HOM interference visibility ($V$), Mutual Coherence Time ($	ext{T}_c$), Quantum Bit Error Rates (QBERs) for both MDI-QKD and BB84.

**Important parameters / regimes.** 20 km optical channel length; Visibility approaching the classical limit of 0.5; HWP rotation angle of $22.5^\circ$ for mode switching.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The security during protocol switching relies on using a physical beam block to obstruct unused channels in BB84 mode.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the optical architecture, spectral filtering performance (sideband isolation), time-resolved TPI histograms confirming indistinguishability across detunings, and comparisons of QKD count rates across different modes.

**Paper structure.** The paper details the hardware setup using EOMs for state generation, validates channel indistinguishability via HOM interference measurements, demonstrates MDI-QKD implementation through partial BSM, and concludes by showing the simple reconfiguration to BB84 mode.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Measurement-device-independent quantum key distribution (MDI-QKD) is designed to eliminate detector side-channel vulnerabilities. However, its practical deployment remains experimentally demanding because it requires two-photon interference (TPI) between mutually phase-randomized optical states. In this study, we demonstrate a reconfigurable platform that supports both polarization encoded MDI-QKD and BB84 measurements utilizing the same optical hardware over 20 km optical fiber channels. Two mutually phase-randomized weak coherent states (WCSs) are generated from a shared continuous-wave (CW) laser via electro-optic phase modulation and subsequent etalon-based first-order sideband filtering. Channel indistinguishability is verified through Hong-Ou-Mandel (HOM) interference, combining time-resolved coincidence measurements and polarization mismatch scans, confirming a high degree of indistinguishability that robustly approaches the classical upper limit of 0.5 for WCSs. The transmitted states go through partial Bell-state measurement (BSM) to implement MDI-QKD. Here, the sytem can be directly reconfigured for BB84 simply by rotatinga single half-wave plate (HWP) by 22.5 degree in one arm of the module. This seamless reconfiguration drastically reduces hardware redundancy and enhances operational flexibility in dynamic network environments. These results indicate that EOM-based frequency engineering using a shared CW laser offers a highly practical route toward scalable and reconfigurable quantum communication systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** The research experimentally implements CV-QAOA on a programmable continuous-variable quantum computer utilizing a quad-rail lattice cluster state. By mapping quadratic cost functions onto this architecture, they demonstrate parameter optimization using Bayesian methods. The findings establish the feasibility of the algorithm while attributing observed performance plateaus to noise and classical optimization hurdles rather than fundamental algorithmic limits.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete experimental realization of a variational quantum algorithm (CV-QAOA) on a photonic/continuous-variable platform, advancing the practical demonstration of quantum computation beyond simple proof-of-concept measurements.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper experimentally demonstrates the Continuous-Variable Quantum Approximate Optimization Algorithm (CV-QAOA) for solving multi-variable quadratic optimization problems.

**Main result.** While increasing QAOA depth improves performance from $d=1$ to $d=2$, further increases show limited gains, suggesting experimental limitations are due to noise and classical optimization challenges rather than algorithmic failure.

**Method.** The authors use Bayesian Optimization (BO) to tune variational parameters ($\eta, \gamma$) while implementing the CV-QAOA ansatz on a programmable platform.

**Model / system.** The system is a continuous-variable quantum computing platform based on a quad-rail lattice (QRL) cluster state, enabling Measurement-Based Quantum Computing (MBQC). The algorithm maps quadratic cost functions onto this architecture using Gaussian operations.

**Key observables.** Optimized cost values ($\log_{10}\langle f(\hat{\mathbf{x}})angle$), performance vs. QAOA depth ($d$), and the mean/covariance of output quadratures.

**Important parameters / regimes.** QAOA depth ($d$), resource squeezing level (e.g., -4.5 dB), problem dimension (up to 100 modes).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Experimental limitations are attributed primarily to noise accumulation and classical optimization challenges, contrasting with ideal numerical simulations which show continued improvement with depth.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the CV-QAOA schematic, mapping onto the QRL structure, showing performance vs. depth for varying squeezing levels, and comparing experimental results against idealized infinite-shot simulations.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically introduces the CV-QAOA framework on the QRL platform, details the Hamiltonian decomposition into Gaussian operations, presents optimization using BO, and concludes by comparing finite-shot experimental performance to ideal theoretical limits.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We experimentally demonstrate the continuous-variable quantum approximate optimization algorithm (CV-QAOA) for multi-variable problems and multiple QAOA depths using a measurement-based CV quantum computing platform on a quad-rail lattice (QRL) cluster state. We propose a systematic method to map arbitrary quadratic cost functions onto the QRL architecture and examine the resulting construction in settings involving up to 100 modes. Using the programmable platform, we prepare the CV-QAOA ansatz and optimize the variational parameters via Bayesian optimization. We then investigate the performance on quadratic optimization problems and observe that increasing the depth from 1 to 2 improves performance, whereas further increases yield only limited gains. In contrast, numerical simulations under idealized conditions, assuming an infinite number of measurement shots and gradient-based optimization, indicate that the performance of CV-QAOA can improve with increasing depth, suggesting that the experimentally observed limitations primarily arise from noise accumulation and classical optimization challenges. This work provides an experimental demonstration of CV-QAOA on a programmable CV platform and establishes a foundation for future developments of variational quantum algorithms in CV systems.

</details>

**Summary.** The authors synthesized and characterized $	ext{TmZnGaO}_4$, a triangular lattice antiferromagnet. They found strong magnetic anisotropy and observed a characteristic one-third magnetization plateau under an applied field, suggesting a specific spin ordering ($\uparrow\uparrow\downarrow$). Furthermore, low-temperature specific heat measurements reveal distinct anomalies, confirming the presence of complex, frustrated quantum spin dynamics in this material.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides direct experimental evidence on how geometric frustration interacts with crystal field effects in a non-Kramers system, leading to exotic, field-tunable magnetic ground states relevant to modern strongly correlated electron physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates exotic quantum spin states and frustrated magnetism within a non-Kramers triangular antiferromagnet.

**Main result.** The material exhibits strong magnetic anisotropy, clear evidence of field-induced one-third magnetization plateaus suggesting an up-up-down spin configuration, and specific heat anomalies pointing to complex low-temperature quantum fluctuations.

**Method.** Measurements include single crystal synthesis, magnetic susceptibility ($\chi$), magnetization ($M-H$) under various fields, and zero-field/field-dependent specific heat ($C_p$).

**Model / system.** The system is $	ext{TmZnGaO}_4$, a layered triangular lattice (TL) antiferromagnet where the magnetic moments reside on non-Kramers $	ext{Tm}^{3+}$ ions. The physics is governed by strong easy-$c$-axis anisotropy and geometric frustration.

**Key observables.** One-third magnetization plateau ($\sim 1.65 	ext{ T}$), specific heat anomalies at $0.11 	ext{ K}$ and $2.81 	ext{ K}$, magnetic entropy ($S_M$), and power-law decay in differential susceptibility ($dM/dH$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Low temperatures (down to 50 mK), strong anisotropy ratio ($\approx 10$), and the presence of a triangular lattice geometry.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis requires subtracting phonon contributions from total specific heat data, and some measurements rely on fitting models like Curie-Weiss law or Debye/Einstein models for extrapolation.

**Figures summary.** Figures show crystal structure details, magnetic susceptibility vs. T (anisotropy), $M(H)$ curves revealing plateaus, and $C_p(T)/S_M$ plots detailing anomalies under varying fields.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a standard experimental structure: structural characterization is presented first, followed by measurements of magnetic susceptibility and magnetization to establish anisotropy and field-induced phases. Finally, specific heat and entropy measurements probe the underlying quantum spin fluctuations at very low temperatures.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A previously unreported triangular lattice (TL) antiferromagnet, TmZnGaO4, was synthesized as single crystals, and its crystal structure, magnetic susceptibilities, and specific heat were reported. Its crystal structure is isomorphic to that of the transverse-field Ising antiferromagnet TmMgGaO4, with Tm3+ ions located in the TLs, separated by a nonmagnetic bilayer composed mainly of Ga3+ and Zn2+ ions. The magnetic susceptibilities indicate the dominating antiferromagnetic interactions. The magnetization curves (M-H) exhibit strong easy-c-axis anisotropy, with a clear one-third magnetic plateau emerging, consistent with a field-induced up-up-down spin configuration. Instead of forming a conventional long-range magnetic order, the system exhibits two broad anomalies at 0.11 K and 2.81 K in zero-field specific heat measurements, highlighting the persistence of strong spin fluctuations and the potential for exotic quantum spin states. The above results reveal its future interest in exploring exotic quantum spin states in TmZnGaO4.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a novel technique to combat quantum noise by superposing multiple noisy quantum channels. Using NMR spectroscopy on multi-qubit registers, the authors experimentally achieved the recovery of quantum coherence and demonstrated 'superactivation' of quantum capacity when combining zero-capacity noise sources. This work validates coherent control methods for enhancing quantum information processing capabilities.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, experimentally validated platform (NMR) for implementing advanced quantum information protocols—specifically noise mitigation via channel superposition—which is highly relevant to open quantum systems research.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research addresses how noise degrades quantum resources by proposing a method to cancel these detrimental effects through the superposition of noisy quantum channels.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated the cancellation of two dephasing channels using NMR and achieved 'superactivation' of quantum capacity by superposing two zero-capacity depolarizing channels in a five-qubit NMR setup.

**Method.** They theoretically derived conditions for valid channel superposition using Stinespring dilations, which was then experimentally realized using controlled unitary operations on multi-qubit Nuclear Magnetic Resonance (NMR) registers.

**Model / system.** The system involves quantum channels modeled via Pauli maps and implemented experimentally on NMR registers of varying sizes (3-qubit for dephasing, 5-qubit for depolarization). The control mechanism relies on superposing Stinespring dilation unitaries acting on an ancillary qubit.

**Key observables.** Quantum coherence recovery ($\langle\sigma_iangle$ expectation values), normalization factor ($\gamma$), effective error probabilities ($p'_i$), and the quantum capacity of the resulting channel.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The superposition parameter $\alpha$, which controls the mixture between the two channels, is crucial for observing interference effects.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes ancillary qubits are initialized in pure states ($ho_A = |0_nangle\langle 0_n|_A$) for simplicity, although extensions to mixed states are possible.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the quantum circuits used for superposition (using control qubits and postselection) and plot key observables like $\langle\sigma_iangle$, normalization factor $\gamma$, and effective channel strength $p$ as a function of the superposition parameter $\alpha$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from theoretical derivation of valid superposed channels to analyzing specific cases (dephasing, depolarizing). It then details the experimental realization using NMR for both coherence recovery and capacity enhancement.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Noisy quantum channels degrade quantum resources such as coherence and entanglement and hence pose challenges for realizing quantum technologies. Coherent control of noisy channels allows us to minimize their effects on the quantum system. Here we achieve the cancellation of two noisy quantum channels by superposing their corresponding Stinespring dilation unitaries. We first arrive at conditions under which superposition of channels results in a valid quantum channel. We then consider superposing two dephasing channels and observe their destructive interference, thereby effectively recovering the quantum coherence. On superposing two zero-capacity depolarizing channels, we show superactivation of quantum capacity. We experimentally realize the cancellation of two dephasing channels using a three-qubit NMR register. Furthermore, using a five-qubit NMR register, we realize the cancellation of two depolarization channels and demonstrate superactivation of quantum capacity.

</details>

**Summary.** This work applies Variational Quantum Algorithms to solve dynamic portfolio optimization problems. It systematically investigates how crucial design choices—including adaptive sampling strategies, multi-stage classical optimization, and hardware-aware ansatz layouts (like HNDC)—impact performance on real quantum processors. The results guide the development of practical VQA protocols for near-term quantum devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on finance, the core methodology—designing quantum circuits to respect underlying hardware connectivity while maximizing computational depth/expressivity—is directly relevant to developing algorithms for complex many-body Hamiltonians in AMO physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses dynamic portfolio optimization (DPO), a complex multi-period financial problem, by mapping it onto a Variational Quantum Algorithm (VQA) framework.

**Main result.** The proposed Heavy-Hex Native Deep-Chain (HNDC) ansatz layout achieved the best performance on the physical QPU compared to other hardware-aware layouts. The study provides practical guidance for improving VQA performance despite not achieving quantum advantage over classical solvers.

**Method.** It employs a sampling-based VQA approach using Conditional Value-at-Risk (CVaR) estimation, combined with an adaptive two-stage classical optimization schedule (PSO $	o$ NFT).

**Model / system.** The model is a 150-qubit DPO instance. The physical platform involves superconducting qubits, specifically tested on the ibm quebec QPU, requiring careful consideration of hardware connectivity and transpilation overhead.

**Key observables.** Final objective value, CVaR-tail performance, circuit depth, and gate count after transpilation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Problem size ($N_q=150$), optimization parameters ($\lambda, \delta, 
u$, etc.), sampling budget (e.g., $N_{	ext{shots}}$), and CVaR tail parameter $\alpha$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that hardware-aware layout design and advanced scheduling can significantly improve VQA performance on near-term noisy devices, even if quantum advantage is not demonstrated.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the performance of various ansatz layouts (Bilinear, Colored, DGC, HNDC) on the QPU across different optimization settings; kernel density estimates visualize how optimized circuits bias sampling towards better objective values compared to random samples.

**Paper structure.** The paper details the DPO formulation, introduces the VQA framework using CVaR estimation, proposes and evaluates advanced components like adaptive CVaR scheduling and two-stage optimizers, and rigorously compares multiple hardware-aware ansatz layouts (DGC, HNDC) on a physical QPU.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Variational quantum algorithms are increasingly explored for optimization problems at scales relevant to near-term quantum devices. Their practical performance depends strongly on design choices such as the sampling objective, classical optimizer, and ansatz layout before and after hardware transpilation. We study these factors for dynamic portfolio optimization, a multi-period financial problem balancing return, risk, transaction costs, cash-interest effects, and constraints. Using a sampling-based VQA framework on a 150-qubit dynamic portfolio instance, we evaluate several components of the optimization workflow. We propose a specific adaptive CVaR schedule that gradually tightens the sampled tail used for optimization, together with a two-stage optimizer combining global exploration with Particle Swarm Optimization and local refinement with the Nakanishi-Fujii-Todo optimizer. We also study ansatz depth and sequential growth strategies. Finally, we introduce two hardware-aware ansatz-layout modifications: a data-guided colored layout that assigns correlated variables to qubits connected by entangling gates, and a heavy-hex-native deep-chain layout designed to increase native two-qubit interaction depth without additional routing overhead after transpilation. Simulator studies select CVaR, optimizer, and depth configurations, while the ansatz comparison is performed on the ibm_quebec QPU. The results show that sampling strategy, optimizer scheduling, and hardware-aware layout design materially affect performance. In the reported QPU layout comparison, the proposed heavy-hex-native deep-chain layout achieves the best final objective value and CVaR-tail performance among the tested layouts. Although we do not observe quantum advantage over a state-of-the-art exact classical solver, our results provide practical guidance for improving VQA performance on near-term hardware.

</details>

### [A Cryogenic Hybrid Photonic/CMOS Controller Architecture for Scalable Superconducting Qubit Control](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10114v1)

**Authors:** Bowen Liu, Zhaoran Rena Huang

**Summary.** The paper introduces a novel hybrid photonic/CMOS controller architecture designed to overcome the severe scaling challenges in superconducting qubit control. By using optical fibers to distribute pulse templates and local 4 K CMOS circuits for fine-grained programming, it drastically reduces power consumption compared to fully sampled cryogenic electronics. The analysis confirms this approach is feasible by meeting stringent error budget requirements while maintaining programmability.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the physical engineering bottleneck of quantum computing hardware by proposing a scalable, low-power control interface that integrates photonics and cryogenics, which is crucial for building large-scale quantum processors.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Scaling superconducting quantum computers requires a control hardware architecture that minimizes wiring density, cryogenic heat load, and controller-induced errors.

**Main result.** The proposed hybrid photonic/CMOS architecture successfully mitigates scaling bottlenecks by using optical links for shared pulse templates while retaining local 4 K programmability, achieving low power dissipation and error rates below the target threshold.

**Method.** The authors developed a template-based control framework, modeling power dissipation and gate errors using first-order approximations derived from comparing the proposed architecture against high-speed RF-AWG baselines.

**Model / system.** The system involves superconducting transmon qubits controlled by microwave pulses generated via an optical path (room T $ightarrow$ 4 K) followed by local Cryo-CMOS processing. The error analysis is cross-checked using a three-level Transmon Hamiltonian simulation.

**Key observables.** Controller-induced gate fidelity ($\epsilon$), 4 K power dissipation ($\mu	ext{W}/	ext{ch}$), and waveform storage requirements (bits).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Target operation fidelity of $99.9\%$ ($10^{-3}$ error budget); operating temperature range (mK to 4 K); pulse durations ($10-500 	ext{ ns}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on first-order models for power and error, assuming feasibility of cryogenic components like MRRs and PDs; full device calibration is not performed.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate waveform examples (single/two-qubit gates), the optical multi-tone LO distribution path, and comparative plots showing power dissipation vs. RF-AWG baselines and error contributions vs. $10^{-3}$ budget.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by identifying scaling limitations, proposing the hybrid architecture to solve these limits (combining photonic links with local CMOS), quantifying feasibility through detailed power modeling against alternatives, and finally validating the approach via first-order error analysis cross-checked with numerical simulations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Scaling superconducting quantum computers toward thousands of qubits remains a difficult control hardware problem. It requires hardware that reduces room-temperature to cryogenic wiring and cryogenic power while preserving in-fridge programmability for microwave pulse generation. This work develops a 4 K hybrid photonic/CMOS control architecture in which optical fibers distribute shared shaped pulse templates, while local cryogenic CMOS (Cryo-CMOS) circuits provide transmission control, amplitude programming, sample-and-hold envelope shaping, LO-tone and phase selection, and microwave upconversion, enabling both single-qubit and two-qubit gate generation within the same control path. Compared with fully Cryo-CMOS controllers, this architecture reduces per-channel active dissipation by moving high-speed sampled RF/IF waveform synthesis and waveform-memory access out of each cryogenic channel. Compared with purely photonic-link qubit-control approaches, it adds local 4 K programmability for pulse selection, amplitude scaling, timing updates, and LO-phase control, while remaining compatible with room-temperature real-time feedback and quantum error correction (QEC) workflows. We present architecture-level first-order models for 4 K power dissipation, waveform-memory scaling, and controller-induced fidelity limits, and cross-check the dominant fidelity terms using a three-level transmon simulation. The analysis shows that shared optical pulse template distribution with local 4 K envelope programming is a feasible path toward scalable superconducting qubit control.

</details>

### [Thermal Signatures of the Slater-Mott Crossover in the Hubbard Model: From Double Occupancy to Antiferromagnetic Correlation Length](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10976v1)

**Summary.** The paper establishes a definitive thermal signature—the sign change of the temperature derivative of double occupancy—to locate the Slater-Mott crossover in the Hubbard model. Using advanced Quantum Monte Carlo simulations, they determine this critical ratio to be $U_{	ext{cross}}/t \approx 4.0$. This provides a direct, measurable framework for experimentalists studying strongly correlated electron systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a highly quantitative and experimentally accessible diagnostic tool ($\partial D/\partial T$) to probe fundamental many-body physics transitions relevant to ultracold atoms trapped in optical lattices.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to quantitatively characterize the crossover between a Slater insulator (weak coupling) and a Mott insulator (strong coupling) in the Hubbard model.

**Main result.** A clear, measurable thermal criterion based on the sign change of $(\partial D/\partial T)_U$ is established, determining the crossover boundary at $U_{	ext{cross}}/t = 4.0(2)$.

**Method.** The research employs advanced Quantum Monte Carlo simulations (AFQMC) and Exact Diagonalization (ED) to calculate thermal properties.

**Model / system.** The system is the half-filled two-dimensional Hubbard model on a square lattice, governed by the Hamiltonian $\hat{H} = \sum_{k\sigma} \varepsilon_k \hat{c}^\dagger_{k\sigma}\hat{c}_{k\sigma} + U \sum_i (\hat{n}_{i\uparrow}\hat{n}_{i\downarrow} - rac{1}{2}(\hat{n}_{i\uparrow} + \hat{n}_{i\downarrow}))$.

**Key observables.** Double occupancy ($D$), the temperature derivative of double occupancy $(\partial D/\partial T)_U$, thermal entropy density ($s(U)$), and antiferromagnetic correlation length ($\xi$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** $U/t$ ratio, Temperature $T/t$, Lattice dimension (2D).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on theoretical relations like the Maxwell relation connecting $\partial s/\partial U$ to $(\partial D/\partial T)_U$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the consistency of multiple methods—including entropy minimum, correlation length maximum, and the sign change of $(\partial D/\partial T)_U$—in locating the crossover boundary.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically introduces the physical problem, develops a measurable thermal criterion using derivatives of double occupancy, validates this criterion against other signatures (entropy, $\xi$), and concludes by providing a direct experimental roadmap for identifying the crossover in optical lattices.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The interaction-driven crossover from a Slater insulator to a Mott insulator in the Néel-ordered ground state of the Hubbard model is a fundamental paradigm of strongly correlated electrons, yet its quantitative characterization has remained elusive. Here we establish a clear and experimentally accessible thermal criterion for this crossover via the sign change of the temperature derivative of double occupancy, $(\partial D/\partial T)_U$, near zero temperature. In the Slater regime, $(\partial D/\partial T)_U>0$ reflects the major role of charge fluctuations; in the Mott regime, the anomalous $(\partial D/\partial T)_U<0$, a manifestation of the Pomeranchuk effect, signals the dominance of low-energy spin superexchange physics. Using exact diagonalization and {\it numerically exact} quantum Monte Carlo simulations, we demonstrate that this criterion determines the crossover boundary at $U_{\rm cross}/t=4.0(2)$ for the half-filled two-dimensional Hubbard model. Furthermore, we obtain a consistent boundary independently from the maximum in the antiferromagnetic correlation length, which also arises from the superexchange physics. These two thermal signatures are theoretically unified through the local minimum of thermal entropy versus interaction $U$ at low temperatures. Our results offer a direct, measurable, and physically intuitive framework to identify the Slater-Mott crossover in optical lattice experiments.

</details>

### [Optomechanical system with tunable dissipative and dispersive couplings](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10318v1)

**Authors:** Quansen Wang, Yuefan Wu, Doudou Wang, Genyuan Xu, Jiawei Liang, Qiang Zhang, Yongmin Li

**Summary.** The paper presents an optomechanical system using a Fabry-Perot cavity coupled to a mechanical resonator, demonstrating unprecedented control over the coupling regime. By manipulating the physical parameters of the mechanical element, the authors can continuously tune the ratio between dissipative and dispersive couplings across several orders of magnitude. This capability makes it a powerful tool for investigating quantum phenomena in massive mechanical resonators.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a versatile, highly controllable platform for studying open quantum systems dynamics, allowing precise tuning between dissipation-dominated and dispersion-dominated regimes, which is crucial for ground state cooling research.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The goal is to develop an optomechanical system capable of continuously tuning both dissipative and dispersive couplings to explore quantum effects.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrated the ability to tune the coupling ratio from dissipation-dominated ($	ext{ratio} > 1$) to dispersion-dominated ($	ext{ratio} < 1$) regimes using a single experimental platform.

**Method.** The study combines theoretical modeling using transfer matrices with experimental measurements on an optomechanical setup involving a Fabry-Perot cavity and a movable mechanical resonator.

**Model / system.** The physical system is an optomechanical device comprising a Fabry-Perot cavity coupled to a string mechanical resonator. The coupling strength is controlled by varying the resonator's diameter, material, and position relative to the cavity field.

**Key observables.** Dissipative coupling constant ($g_\gamma$), dispersive coupling constant ($g_\omega$), and their ratio ($	ext{ratio} = g_\gamma / g_\omega$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The theoretical tuning range for the coupling ratio spans from 25 down to 0.02, covering over three orders of magnitude.

**Assumptions / limitations.** In the advanced analysis sections, assumptions include treating the MR interaction as a 'shadow effect' and approximating beam waists at different points along the cavity.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the schematic setup for tunable coupling (Fig. 1), stability improvement using dual-cavity techniques (Fig. 2), measured trends of linewidth and frequency shift vs. position (Fig. 3), and calculated coupling strengths (Fig. 4).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the physical platform and theoretical framework (transfer matrices, coupling definitions) to presenting experimental results showing tunable regimes using different resonators, and finally detailing advanced analytical derivations for field propagation through dielectric media.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We demonstrate an optomechanical system with tunable dissipative and dispersive couplings using a Fabry-Perot cavity and a string mechanical resonator. By varying the diameter and material of the mechanical resonator, and the relative location between the mechanical resonator and the cavity, the relative strengths of dissipative and dispersive coupling could be tuned continuously from dissipation-dominated regime to dispersion-dominated regime. In our experiments, the dissipative-to-dispersive coupling ratios of 1.3 and 0.6 are achieved by using two different mechanical resonators, corresponding to a transition from dissipation-dominated to dispersion-dominated optomechanical system. Theoretically, the coupling ratio could be tuned from 25 to 0.02 by optimizing the mechanical resonator, spanning over three orders of magnitude. These two distinct coupling regimes are achieved with the same experimental platform. The capability to freely adjust the coupling ratio provides a versatile platform for exploring quantum effects of massive mechanical resonators and quantum-limited measurements.

</details>

### [Sensitivity Enhancement near High-Order Exceptional Points via Dissipative Couplings](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10865v1)

**Authors:** Yuanjie Zhang, Jiaojiao Li, Zhihuang Luo  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10865v1>

**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper proposes using a four-channel dissipative coupling model in an atomic system to achieve highly sensitive quantum sensing by exploiting high-order Exceptional Points (EPs). It demonstrates that fourth-order EPs offer superior sensitivity scaling compared to second-order EPs. Crucially, the work also analyzes the noise robustness trade-off, providing a comprehensive guide for realizing such sensors.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly applies advanced concepts from non-Hermitian physics (EPs) to a tangible AMO platform (cold atoms/EIT), providing quantitative metrics for sensing performance enhancement while also addressing the crucial issue of noise robustness, which is vital for experimental realization.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is developing highly sensitive quantum sensing schemes by exploiting high-order Exceptional Points (EPs) in non-Hermitian systems while overcoming the experimental difficulty of tuning to isolated, extremely sensitive points.

**Main result.** The proposed four-channel dissipative coupling model supports fourth-order EPs over a broad parameter range, exhibiting significantly higher sensitivity enhancement compared to second-order EPs for small disturbances.

**Method.** The analysis involves deriving an effective Hamiltonian from the system's dynamics (governed by master equations) and calculating the eigenvalue splitting ($\Delta\lambda$) near EP crossings using perturbation theory.

**Model / system.** The model is a four-channel dissipative coupling system realized in a thermal atomic vapor cell using Electromagnetically Induced Transparency (EIT). The dynamics are governed by an effective Hamiltonian derived from the full master equation for ground-state coherences.

**Key observables.** Sensitivity enhancement factor ($S$), eigenvalue splittings ($\Delta\lambda \propto |\epsilon|^{1/4}$ vs. $|\epsilon|^{1/2}$), and relative error ($E_r$) near EPs.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Laser detunings ($\Delta_A, \Delta_B$), coupling strengths ($
u, \omega$), disturbance parameters ($\epsilon_{\Delta}, \epsilon_{v}$), and the order of the EP (second vs. fourth).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on adiabatic elimination of excited states in the EIT process; a later section notes that higher-order EPs are more susceptible to noise than second-order EPs.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the schematic model, the hypersurfaces defining different EP orders (Fig. 2), the scaling of eigenvalue splittings ($\propto |\epsilon|^{1/4}$ vs. $\propto |\epsilon|^{1/2}$) (Fig. 3), and comparisons of sensitivity enhancement ($S$) and relative error ($E_r$) for both second- and fourth-order EPs (Figs. 4 & 6).

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the general non-Hermitian model and its Hamiltonian structure, then details the physical realization using a four-channel dissipative coupling in an atomic system via EIT. The core analysis compares the sensitivity scaling and robustness of fourth-order versus second-order EPs under external disturbances.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

High-order exceptional points (EPs) emerging in non-Hermitian systems have attracted broad interest for their significantly enhanced sensitivity to perturbations. However, quantum sensing schemes based on high-order EPs remain scarce, due to the experimental challenge of fine-tuning the system to such an extremely sensitive isolated point. Here we propose a four-channel dissipative coupling model that supports both fourth-order exceptional surfaces and second-order exceptional volumes. This non-Hermitian model can be realized in a thermal atomic system, and its complex energy spectra can be determined via electromagnetically induced transparency spectroscopy. The proposed model exhibits a characteristic fourth-order response to multiple physical quantities such as the laser detuning and the distance between optical channels, significantly surpassing the response of second-order EPs. We further reveal the sensitivity-robustness trade-off under experimental noise. Our work opens a route toward high-performance sensing leveraging higher-order EPs.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10984"></a>
### [Nonreciprocal quantum rotation sensing via virtual-excitation enhancement in a spinning cavity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10984v1)

**Authors:** Lu-Qi Yang, Yu-Meng Ren, Peng-Bo Li  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10984v1>
