**Summary.** The paper details a novel method for highly sensitive rotation sensing using a spinning cavity coupled to matter qubits. By leveraging the Sagnac effect, the angular velocity modifies the system's effective Hamiltonian in a direction-dependent (nonreciprocal) manner. This modification enhances quantum sensitivity by utilizing virtual excitations, allowing for differential readout schemes that surpass standard measurement limits.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to AMO physics as it combines cavity quantum electrodynamics (QED) with advanced sensing techniques. The use of virtual excitations and directional control in a superconducting/optical resonator platform represents a cutting-edge approach for realizing high-precision measurements beyond standard linear response theory.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper proposes a nonreciprocal quantum metrological scheme for estimating rotational angular velocity ($\Omega$) with high precision.

**Main result.** The rotation sensitivity is enhanced by exploiting virtual excitations generated through ultrastrong coupling, leading to an intrinsically nonreciprocal response contrast between opposite driving directions.

**Method.** The analysis uses theoretical techniques including the Schrieffer-Wolff transformation and Bogoliubov diagonalization to derive effective Hamiltonians and calculate the Quantum Fisher Information (QFI).

**Model / system.** The system is a hybrid light-matter platform: a spinning ring cavity coupled to a two-level system (TLS) and an auxiliary bosonic mode. The Sagnac effect converts $\Omega$ into a direction-dependent detuning that modifies the effective light-matter dressing.

**Key observables.** Quantum Fisher Information ($QFI(\Omega)$), lower-polariton spectral response ($\omega_{-}(\Omega)$), and bundle emission coincidence counting rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Angular velocity ($\Omega$), Sagnac-Fizeau shift ($\Delta F(\Omega)$), ultrastrong coupling regime, and the directionality parameter ($s=\pm 1$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes operation in the dispersive regime ($|\Delta_{\pm}(\Omega)| \gg \lambda_{a\sigma}$) and utilizes approximations for QFI based on projecting onto the lower-polariton branch.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the sensing chain ($\Omega 	o \Delta F(\Omega) 	o H_{	ext{eff}} 	o QFI$), showing $	ext{QFI}_{	ext{proxy}}$ landscapes that demonstrate distinct, nonreciprocal distributions for opposite drive directions (RD vs. LD).

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically derives the effective Hamiltonian using sequential transformations (Schrieffer-Wolff followed by Bogoliubov), calculates the resulting polariton eigenfrequencies and coupling strengths, quantifies sensitivity via QFI, and finally analyzes the nonreciprocal contrast achievable through differential readout.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum sensing with high precision and sensitivity plays an important role in quantum technologies and quantum information processing. Here, we propose a nonreciprocal quantum metrological scheme for estimating rotational angular velocity in a hybrid light-matter platform, where the setup consists of a spinning ring cavity coupled to a two-level system and an auxiliary bosonic mode. Through the Sagnac effect, the angular velocity is converted into a direction-dependent detuning, which modifies the effective light-matter dressing of the hybrid system. As a result, the angular velocity is encoded not only into the renormalized hybrid-mode spectrum, but also into the virtual excitations generated by ultrastrong coupling. These virtual excitations modify the polaritonic frequency response to rotation and enhance the quantum Fisher information (QFI) associated with angular velocity estimation, without requiring direct extraction of virtual excitations. Moreover, since the Sagnac-Fizeau shift enters the virtual-transition energy denominators, the metrological response becomes intrinsically different for opposite driving directions, leading to a tunable nonreciprocal sensitivity contrast. In addition, we also discuss a readout scheme and show that bundle emission coincidence counting can serve as an auxiliary direction-dependent readout channel. Our results provide a route toward exploiting nonreciprocal light-matter dressing and virtual excitations as resources for quantum rotation sensing.

</details>

### [Quantum statistics in an extended collider coupled to a qubit](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11147v1)

**Authors:** Rishav Chaudhuri, Sai Satyam Samal  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11147v1>

**Summary.** The paper investigates how coupling an extended mesoscopic collider to a qubit affects the measurable mutual statistics of incoming particles. By analyzing multiple theoretical benchmarks against measured probabilities, the authors rigorously prove that only one specific benchmark correctly extracts the true quantum statistical signature. This highlights the critical dependence of physical inference on the precise measurement protocol used in nanoscale quantum experiments.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous theoretical framework for interpreting quantum transport measurements in nanoscale devices, showing how measurement choice (the 'benchmark') can fundamentally alter the inferred physical statistics, which is crucial knowledge for experimentalists using mesoscopic tools.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to determine the correct diagnostic benchmark for extracting the mutual quantum statistics (bosonic vs. fermionic) of colliding particles when the scattering process is mediated by an extended mesoscopic collider coupled to a qubit.

**Main result.** Only a specific benchmark, $B_4 = S(11; |\psiangle)CWP(11)CW$, faithfully captures the true mutual statistics, as other choices yield spurious results. This confirms that the correct diagnostic tool depends intricately on the collider's geometry and coupling details.

**Method.** The analysis involves deriving scattering matrices ($S_{	ext{eff}}$) for single and two-particle wave packets in the presence of a qubit, calculating post-scattering density matrices ($ho_{	ext{sys}}$), and systematically comparing multiple theoretical benchmarks ($B_1$ through $B_4$).

**Model / system.** The system is an extended mesoscopic collider (modeled with tunneling points separated by L) coupled to a two-level impurity qubit. Scattering processes are analyzed for incoming fermionic and bosonic wave packets.

**Key observables.** Mutual statistics (bunching/anti-bunching), single-particle transmission/reflection probabilities ($P(1 	o 1)$, $P(1 	o 2)$), and the two-particle probability difference, $P(11; |\psiangle)F - B_4$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Orbital phase $\phi = kL$, qubit coupling parameters ($\eta_m, 	heta_m$), and the state of the post-selected qubit $|\psiangle$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that incoming particles interact only via statistical interaction (neglecting Coulomb interactions) and relies on approximations for calculating correlation terms in the continuum limit.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the three physical setups: point-like, extended, and qubit-coupled. Figure 2 plots differences between calculated probabilities and benchmarks ($B_1, B_2$) to demonstrate statistical transmutation.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the effective scattering matrices for the coupled system (Eqs. 1-3), then calculating single-particle and two-particle probabilities dependent on the qubit state ($ho_{	ext{sys}}$). The core of the work is a systematic comparison of multiple statistical benchmarks ($B_1$ to $B_4$) to isolate the physically meaningful signature.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Mesoscopic colliders provide an effective platform for probing the mutual statistics of quantum particles. Recent experiments have successfully extracted the mutual statistics of fermions, and more exotic anyons using quantum point contacts (QPCs). Coupling a point-like collider, such as a quantum point contact, to a two-level impurity or qubit can induce statistical transmutation of fermions, causing them to display boson-like bunching tendencies. Here, we extend the analysis to an extended collider. We investigate the scattering of two incoming fermionic and bosonic wave packets in the presence of post-selection on the impurity state, and systematically analyze the possible benchmarks used to characterize bunching and infer the underlying mutual statistics. We show that only a specific benchmark faithfully captures the mutual statistics of the colliding particles, while alternative choices can produce spurious statistical signatures. Hence, the correct benchmark for probing the quantum statistics depends on the intricate details of the mesoscopic collider.

</details>

**Summary.** This review surveys advanced mathematical techniques, particularly generating functions, used to solve kinetic equations describing particle aggregation and fragmentation. It provides analytical solutions for several idealized physical models, establishing universal scaling behaviors like $s^{-3/2}$ decay in steady states. The work is crucial for understanding non-equilibrium dynamics in systems ranging from aerosols to planetary rings.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical kinetics, the rigorous mathematical techniques—solving complex nonlinear differential equations derived from conservation laws—are highly relevant to open quantum systems dynamics where master equations govern evolution.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper reviews generating function techniques used to find exact analytical solutions for aggregation and fragmentation equations across various physical regimes.

**Main result.** It derives explicit closed-form solutions for specific discrete coagulation models and establishes universal asymptotic power-law decays ($s^{-3/2}$) in source-driven systems.

**Method.** The primary method involves using generating functions, Laplace transforms, and solving the resulting nonlinear differential equations derived from Smoluchowski-type kinetic equations.

**Model / system.** The models are aggregation and fragmentation processes (coagulation-fragmentation kinetics), described by master or rate equations for particle size distributions $n_s(t)$. These cover discrete, continuous, two-component, and source-driven systems.

**Key observables.** Concentration functions $n_s(t)$ or $n(v, t)$, total aggregate density $n(t)$, and steady-state power-law decay exponents.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Aggregation kernels ($K_{i,j}$), source terms ($P_s$), homogeneity index ($\lambda$), and system dimensionality (number of components).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Smoluchowski equations assume spatial uniformity; exact analytical solutions are generally restricted to specific, simplified kernel forms or initial conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically reviews the mathematical framework by introducing generating functions for various kinetic models (discrete, continuous, multi-particle). It then presents derivations and explicit solutions for idealized cases before discussing general asymptotic behaviors and limitations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this work, we review and revisit the generating function techniques that provide exact analytical solutions for aggregation and fragmentation equations across several physical regimes including spontaneous and collisonal shattering. For discrete coagulation-fragmentation equations with size-independent rates under monodisperse initial conditions, we show the derivation of sevaral explicit closed-form solutions. We also briefly report the exact solutions for continuous, three-particle, $D$-particle collisions and two-component generalizations. Source-driven aggregation yields steady distributions featuring a universal $s^{-3/2}$ power-law decay and a cutoff mass scaling $s_{*} \sim t^{2}$.

</details>

### [Jittery Quantum Boomerang Effect](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10067v1)

**Summary.** This paper analyzes the time evolution of spin-polarized electrons in a disordered Rashba 2DEG to reveal a 'jittery quantum boomerang effect.' The authors show that longitudinal motion transitions from WAL behavior toward Anderson localization, while transverse motion is forced back to zero by symmetry constraints. This work bridges concepts from SOC physics, disorder effects, and non-equilibrium dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides a detailed real-time picture of transport phenomena (WAL $	o$ localization) driven by SOC and disorder, offering insights into how symmetry dictates long-term quantum dynamics in mesoscopic systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the real-time dynamics of a spin-polarized wave packet in a disordered Rashba two-dimensional electron gas to identify a 'jittery quantum boomerang effect.'

**Main result.** The system exhibits distinct longitudinal and transverse return mechanisms: longitudinal motion shows a crossover from Weak Antilocalization (WAL) to Anderson localization, while transverse motion returns to zero due to symmetry constraints.

**Method.** The analysis combines numerical simulations using Chebyshev expansion with analytical derivations based on the quantum kinetic equation in both weak and strong disorder regimes.

**Model / system.** The model is a spin-polarized wave packet propagating in a disordered Rashba 2DEG, governed by a Hamiltonian including Rashba SOC and random on-site potentials. The dynamics are tracked via expectation values of position, momentum, and spin polarization.

**Key observables.** Time evolution of displacements ($\langle x(t) angle$, $\langle y(t) angle$), momentum ($\langle k_x(t) angle$), and spin components ($\langle \sigma_{x,y,z}(t) angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Disorder strength ($W$), scattering time ($	au$), SOC strength ($\alpha$), and the weak-scattering limit.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on asymptotic constraints derived from Anderson localization for strong disorder, and approximations like the quasi-particle picture in the weak-disorder regime.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate k-space probability density evolution across different scattering regimes (weak vs. strong) and show time traces of displacements ($\langle x(t) angle$, $\langle y(t) angle$) demonstrating the boomerang effect.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops equations of motion for position, momentum, and spin in the weak-disorder limit using the quantum kinetic equation. It then analyzes the asymptotic behavior under strong disorder constraints to explain the full 'boomerang' reversal mechanism.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the dynamics of a spin-polarized wave packet in a disordered Rashba two-dimensional electron gas and identify a jittery quantum boomerang effect in which longitudinal and transverse motion return to the origin through fundamentally distinct mechanisms. Starting from an initial state with finite momentum along $x$ and spin polarized along $z$, we calculate the time evolution by combining a Chebyshev expansion of the time-evolution operator with a disorder ensemble average. In the weak-scattering regime, equations of motion derived from the quantum kinetic equation reproduce the numerical trends and show that impurity scattering acts as a viscous damping mechanism that suppresses the transient Zitterbewegung and drives the transverse displacement back to $y=0$ at long times. In contrast, the longitudinal dynamics show a Drude-like saturation at weak disorder. These results are consistent with the vanishing intrinsic spin Hall conductivity in the disordered Rashba model and with experimental observations of a transient intrinsic spin Hall effect in the time-domain. As disorder increases, the longitudinal dynamics evolve to a partial return toward the origin, which signals a transition from weak antilocalization to Anderson localization in 2D.

</details>

**Summary.** The authors present an exact Green-function theory to study microwave scattering in superconducting circuits coupled to SSH waveguides. By integrating out the waveguide structure, they reduce the problem to a manageable effective Hamiltonian. This allows for precise control over complex non-Hermitian phenomena, enabling the design of devices that exhibit tunable filtering or controlled amplification based on topological and internal circuit parameters.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, unified theoretical tool for designing superconducting quantum devices by treating topological structures not just as passive elements, but as active components that reshape non-Hermitian dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops an exact Green-function theory to analyze non-Hermitian single-microwave-photon scattering by finite superconducting circuits embedded within a structured Su–Schrieffer–Heeger (SSH) waveguide.

**Main result.** The framework unifies the analysis of various non-Hermitian phenomena, demonstrating how topological waveguides can be used to design complex microwave functionalities like tunable filtering and spectral separation of pole/zero responses.

**Method.** It employs an exact Green-function reduction technique to integrate out the extended SSH waveguide environment, resulting in a finite-dimensional effective non-Hermitian Hamiltonian for scattering analysis.

**Model / system.** The system consists of superconducting microwave circuits (Model I: flux-controlled two-qubit interferometer; Model II: mediator-assisted scatterer) coupled to an SSH superconducting waveguide reservoir. The physics is governed by the SSH Hamiltonian and subsequent effective Hamiltonians derived via Green's functions.

**Key observables.** Scattering amplitudes, exceptional-point diagnostics, coherent-perfect-absorption (CPA) conditions, lasing thresholds, transmission/reflection spectra, and singular values of the scattering matrix S(E).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Flux ($\phi$), dimerization parameter ($\delta$), gain/loss imbalance ($\eta$), and energy ($E$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes coupling only to the central unit cell of the SSH waveguide, and relies on standard Green's function techniques for open quantum systems.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate schematics of Model I and Model II; spectra show how flux and dimerization control interference patterns (bright/dark branches); diagnostics map out near-EP regimes using singular values ($\sigma_{\min}, \sigma_{\max}$) in the presence of gain/loss imbalance ($\eta$).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the general Green-function reduction method for open systems, then applying it to Model I (interferometer), and finally extending the analysis to Model II (mediator) to show enhanced spectral control over pole-like vs. zero-like scattering branches.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We formulate an exact Green-function theory for non-Hermitian single-microwave-photon scattering by finite superconducting circuit subsystems embedded in an SSH waveguide. The structured SSH environment is integrated out exactly and enters the local scattering problem as an energy-dependent matrix self-energy, reducing the full open system to a finite-dimensional effective non-Hermitian Hamiltonian. This reduction places scattering amplitudes, exceptional-point diagnostics, coherent-perfect-absorption conditions, and lasing thresholds within one unified framework. Within this approach we analyze two superconducting devices. A flux-controlled two-qubit interferometric scatterer exhibits a broad bright branch and a narrow quasi-dark branch whose interference is reshaped by the SSH environment and changes qualitatively across the two dimerizations. A mediator-assisted two-qubit scatterer generates an additional energy-dependent complex coupling, reorganizes the dressed spectrum, and produces clearer dimerization-sensitive transparency-versus-absorption windows together with a pronounced separation between zero-like and pole-like scattering branches. In the active regime, near-exceptional-point hybridization enhances the pole-dominated response while deepening the singular-value valley associated with near-coherent perfect absorption. These results show how structured topological waveguides can be used not only to host scattering, but also to design non-Hermitian superconducting microwave functionalities.

</details>

### [Exceptional Points as Manifestations of Analyticity Breakdown in the 't Hooft Model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10141v1)

**Authors:** Kejun Liu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10141v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10141_figures/2606.10141_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: (a) FSS of the EP exponent ν(N) (m = 0, identical for g2Nc = 0.5, 1, 2); ν∞= 0.4993 vs the analytic 1/2 (dashed). (b) Eigenvalues near the EP (fine γ-mesh, N = 2000): two real branches coalesce at γc and split into a ±|Im M 2| square-root envelope. (c) The threshold scales linearly with the coupling, γc = 7.966 g2Nc. (d) Beyond γc, the complex-eigenvalue count rises in steps of two at γ(k) c ≃k γ(1) c : a uniform EP cascade locked to the equidistant spectrum.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10141_figures/2606.10141_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: (a) The threshold γc(K) from the J-fraction (4): exactly 2π at the two-pole level, converging to 7.966 by depth K = 5 (stable to K = 256). (b) Propagator norm ∥e−iV t∥: bounded for γ &lt; γc, linear in t at γc (parallel to the dotted ∝t), exponential for γ &gt; γc — the Jordan secular law.</figcaption>

(b) Skin rate κ = α exactly (points on the dashed line). (c) Edge fraction and mean IPR grow with α, the spectrum staying real to 10−12.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper analyzes the breakdown of causality in the t Hooft model by studying its meson two-point function under non-Hermitian deformation. It rigorously locates this singularity at an Exceptional Point ($\gamma_c$), predicting a universal signature: linear growth of the propagator norm exactly at $\gamma_c$. This provides the first analytically controlled instance of EP physics within a confining gauge theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous analytical control over an EP singularity in a confining gauge theory is highly valuable. The universal signature (linear growth) provides a concrete diagnostic tool applicable across different physical systems, including those studied in AMO physics or quantum optics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the breakdown of analyticity (causality) in a quantum response function when the underlying system undergoes non-Hermitian deformations, specifically locating this breakdown at Exceptional Points (EPs).

**Main result.** They establish that the EP manifests as a square-root branch point ($
u=1/2$) in the resolvent, characterized by a linear growth of the propagator norm (Jordan secular law) exactly at the critical coupling $\gamma_c$.

**Method.** The analysis employs exact solvability techniques from the 't Hooft model, utilizing Jacobi continued fractions to find the EP threshold and analyzing the time evolution of the system's propagator norm.

**Model / system.** The study uses the exactly-solvable t Hooft model for 1+1D large-$N_c$ QCD. The core physics involves deforming the meson two-point function (resolvent) via a term analogous to an imaginary chemical potential, leading to non-Hermitian dynamics.

**Key observables.** The critical coupling $\gamma_c$, the branch point exponent $
u=1/2$, and the three-regime signature of the propagator norm ($	ext{bounded} 	o 	ext{linear growth} 	o 	ext{exponential}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling constants $g^2 N_c$ determine the threshold $\gamma_c$; the exponent $
u=1/2$ is fixed by the Jordan form.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the exact solvability of the 't Hooft model and its mapping to non-Hermitian Wannier-Stark ladders for physical analogy.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate finite-size scaling confirming $
u=1/2$, the convergence of $\gamma_c$ from Jacobi fractions, and the characteristic three-regime behavior of the propagator norm vs. coupling strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the physical problem—analyticity breakdown in a confining theory via non-Hermitian deformation. It then develops the mathematical framework using the t Hooft model's resolvent, calculating $\gamma_c$ and $
u$. Finally, it details the observable consequences: the secular growth at $\gamma_c$ and the connection to other phenomena like the NHSE.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We use the exactly-solvable t Hooft model of 1+1D large-N_c QCD as a rigorous laboratory for the breakdown of analyticity of a causal response function, the meson two-point function. A PT-symmetric deformation i gamma(x-1/2) of the light-cone meson operator, the analogue of an imaginary chemical potential, drives the lowest two mesons to an exceptional point (EP) at gamma_c. Recasting the resolvent as a Jacobi continued fraction yields gamma_c in closed form: 2 pi g^2 N_c at the two-pole level, converging to 7.966 g^2 N_c by depth five -- an analytic, not numerical, threshold. The square-root exponent nu=1/2 is fixed by the 2x2 Jordan form and confirmed by finite-size scaling to N=1999. The breakdown has an unambiguous time-domain signature: the propagator norm is bounded for gamma < gamma_c, grows linearly at gamma_c (the Jordan secular law), and exponentially beyond -- observable, since the deformed operator is a non-Hermitian Wannier-Stark ladder, in photonic and topolectrical analogues. The threshold is locked to confinement, gamma_c propto g^2 N_c, and recurs as a uniform EP cascade; a second, non-reciprocal deformation yields an exactly-exponential non-Hermitian skin effect. This is the first analytically-controlled instance of exceptional-point analyticity breakdown in a confining gauge theory.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper reviews the application of Random Matrix Theory to study wave scattering in chaotic, open quantum systems. It develops a theoretical framework showing that complex resonance poles dictate universal statistical measures like the Wigner time delay and transport coefficients. This methodology is crucial for analyzing energy dissipation and coupling effects in mesoscopic conductors.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful, universal theoretical toolkit for analyzing open quantum systems where dissipation or coupling to external baths is crucial. The connection between spectral statistics (resonances) and transport properties ($	au_W$, $g$) is fundamental for understanding mesoscopic physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper reviews random matrix approaches to characterize chaotic wave scattering and transport in open quantum systems.

**Main result.** It establishes that key transport observables like the Wigner time delay are determined solely by the complex resonance poles of the scattering matrix, exhibiting universal statistics governed by system symmetries and openness.

**Method.** The analysis employs effective non-Hermitian Hamiltonian formulations and utilizes Random Matrix Theory (RMT) techniques, including maximum entropy methods and Selberg integrals, to calculate statistical moments.

**Model / system.** The model is a wave-chaotic system coupled to external leads (open system), described by the scattering matrix S(E). The theoretical framework extends from Hermitian Hamiltonians to non-Hermitian effective Hamiltonians incorporating coupling channels and absorption.

**Key observables.** Scattering matrix S(E), Wigner time delay ($	au_W$), resonance poles ($E_n, \Gamma_n$), transmission eigenvalues $\{T_k\}$, conductance ($g$), and shot noise power ($p$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Symmetry index $eta$ (related to Dyson's classes), number of channels $M$, energy $E$, and the coupling strength/loss rate.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the resonance approximation, assuming slow variation of couplings over the scale of individual resonances, and often assumes the large channel limit ($N 	o \infty$) for universality.

**Figures summary.** Not specified (based only on notes).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by introducing the effective Hamiltonian formalism, deriving relationships between scattering observables ($	au_W$, $\Phi(E)$) and resonance poles, then detailing advanced statistical calculations using RMT tools like the Poisson kernel and Selberg integrals to find moments of transport quantities.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We review random matrix approaches to chaotic wave scattering and transport in open systems. Starting from the effective non-Hermitian Hamiltonian formulation, we discuss the scattering matrix, reaction matrix, time delays, and complex resonances as complementary probes of open chaotic dynamics. We emphasize universal statistics governed by symmetry, openness, and channel coupling. Topics include the maximum-entropy description of fixed-energy scattering and its applications to quantum transport, energy correlations, resonance and eigenfunction statistics, and selected wave-chaotic phenomena induced by finite absorption. The focus throughout is on non-perturbative methods and universal structures underlying open quantum and wave chaotic systems.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper analyzes active rotator networks where oscillators interact via Kuramoto coupling while simultaneously experiencing mixed-sign local feedback disorder. The study maps out the competition between collective phase alignment (drift) and pinning effects across different parameter regimes. It demonstrates that this type of disorder alone can fundamentally alter the system's macroscopic dynamics, providing a rich model for understanding non-equilibrium collective behavior.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed study of non-equilibrium collective dynamics in an active matter context. The interplay between disorder (mixed-sign feedback) and coupling strength offers insights into how local interactions can stabilize or destabilize macroscopic coherent states, relevant to understanding complex biological or physical systems far from equilibrium.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the competition between local pinning effects induced by mixed-sign feedback disorder and global phase alignment/drift in active rotator networks.

**Main result.** Mixed-sign local feedback alone can significantly reshape the balance between pinning and drifting dynamics, even when the intrinsic drive is homogeneous. Strong coupling can restore drift under certain conditions, while strong disorder generally suppresses it.

**Method.** The research employs numerical simulations to construct regime maps in the parameter space defined by feedback disorder strength ($\sigma_A$) and coupling strength ($K$). Analytical limits are used to benchmark these numerical findings.

**Model / system.** The model is a fully connected network of active rotators governed by an equation incorporating a common intrinsic drive, Kuramoto-like coupling, and local feedback amplitudes drawn from a zero-mean Gaussian distribution. The dynamics describe collective synchronization and drift in excitable systems.

**Key observables.** Mean absolute late-time drift ($D$), and the fractions of oscillators exhibiting positive ($f_+$) or negative ($f_-$) late-time drift.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Feedback disorder strength ($r = \sigma_A/\omega$) and coupling strength ($\kappa = K/\omega$). Key regimes include weak vs. strong disorder/coupling, and the presence of intrinsic frequency disorder.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations such as the coherent-phase approximation for strong coupling, and comparisons are drawn between systems with fixed positive drive versus those with zero-mean distributed intrinsic frequencies.

**Figures summary.** Figures map out regime boundaries in the feedback-disorder–coupling plane showing how drift ($D$) and drifting fractions ($f^+, f^-$) change. Comparisons are made against uncoupled limits ($\kappa=0$) and finite-size effects are analyzed.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the model by first examining the uncoupled limit, then mapping out parameter regimes in the coupled system using numerical simulations, and finally contrasting results when intrinsic frequencies are zero-mean versus fixed positive drive.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Active rotator models provide a minimal phase description of excitable and oscillatory systems, and have long been used to study mutual entrainment, synchronization, and collective transitions. Here, we investigate fully connected active rotator networks with Kuramoto coupling, where a common intrinsic drive competes with local feedback amplitudes drawn from a zero-mean Gaussian distribution. This produces a competition between local pinning and collective phase alignment. Using mean absolute late-time drift and the fractions of positive and negative drifting oscillators, we construct numerical regime maps in the feedback-disorder-coupling plane. At weak coupling, increasing the feedback disorder strength suppresses drift, while stronger coupling can restore positive late-time drift when feedback disorder is not too strong. We interpret these regimes using analytical limits for the uncoupled and coherent strong-coupling cases. We also examine finite-size effects and zero-mean distributed intrinsic frequencies. Together, these results show that mixed-sign local feedback alone can reshape the balance between pinning and drifting in coupled active rotator networks, even when the intrinsic drive is homogeneous.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10054"></a>
### [Scaling law of asymptotic freedom in collective charging of quantum batteries](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10054v1)

**Authors:** Gentaro Watanabe, Chunlin Chen, B. Prasanna Venkatesh  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10054v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper rigorously derives universal scaling laws governing how efficiently stored energy can be extracted from a collection of $N$ coupled quantum batteries. It establishes that while generic charging leads to an asymptotic freedom characterized by $\sim 1/N$ decay, specific conditions allow for significantly faster convergence, including exponential scaling. The findings provide crucial theoretical bounds for designing high-efficiency quantum energy devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides fundamental theoretical guarantees regarding energy extraction efficiency in quantum systems, directly impacting the design principles for quantum energy storage devices and understanding collective quantum phenomena.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper establishes a universal scaling law for the collective charging of quantum batteries (QBs) and investigates the phenomenon of 'asymptotic freedom,' where stored energy becomes nearly fully extractable.

**Main result.** It proves that the ergotropy-to-energy ratio approaches unity, exhibiting generic asymptotic freedom with at least $\sim N^{-1}$ scaling, but can achieve much faster convergence ($\sim N^{-b}, b>1$ or exponential) if the battery state becomes asymptotically pure.

**Method.** The analysis involves deriving rigorous finite-$N$ upper and lower bounds on the ergotropy-to-energy ratio by analyzing the residual population $\delta(N)$ within the framework of collective charging dynamics.

**Model / system.** The system consists of $N$ identical, non-interacting quantum batteries (QBs), modeled as qudits. The dynamics are studied using concepts related to total energy ($E_B$) and ergotropy ($	ext{Erg}$), often involving the GKLS master equation for open charging.

**Key observables.** Total Energy ($E_B$), Ergotropy ($	ext{Erg}$), Figure of Merit ($	ext{Erg}/E_B$), Residual Population ($\delta(N)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of batteries ($N$), dimension of the qudits ($d$), and the asymptotic behavior of the residual population ($\delta_\infty$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes identical, non-interacting QBs and often requires assumptions about the battery state remaining mixed or approaching purity in specific limits.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the concept of asymptotic freedom showing generic $\sim N^{-1}$ scaling, alongside examples demonstrating faster convergence rates such as power-law ($\sim 1/N^b$) and exponential ($\sim e^{-bN^2}$) scaling for different charging regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops by first establishing the general $1/N$ universal scaling law (Theorem 1) based on residual population bounds. It then analyzes specific cases, distinguishing between scenarios where the asymptotic residual population is zero or non-zero, leading to derivations of faster convergence rates in those special limits.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We establish a universal scaling law for collective charging of quantum batteries, independent of microscopic details. We prove that the ergotropy-to-energy ratio approaches unity at least as fast as $\sim N^{-1}$ with the number of batteries $N$, implying generic asymptotic freedom. We further show how the universal $1/N$ scaling can be overcome: when the battery state becomes asymptotically pure, the convergence can be substantially faster, including $\sim N^{-b}$ with $b>1$ and even exponential scaling in $N^2$. Rigorous finite-$N$ upper and lower bounds on the ergotropy-to-energy ratio are further derived, providing nonasymptotic guarantees for the universal $1/N$ scaling.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.10004"></a>
### [Wave packets from the spectrum](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10004v1)

**Authors:** ChunJun Cao, Oliver Friedrich, Marin Girard, Nicolas Loizeau, Ashmeet Singh  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10004v1>

**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

**Summary.** The paper introduces 'quantum mereology' to decompose complex Hamiltonians into weakly interacting factors that resemble localized lattice theories. By minimizing interaction terms across these factorizations, the authors successfully map dynamics from random matrix models onto propagating wave packets. This provides a systematic theoretical pathway for extracting emergent, quasi-integrable degrees-of-freedom from highly complicated quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful mathematical tool for understanding how complex, non-local quantum dynamics can be systematically reduced or reinterpreted as simple, local physics governed by emergent lattice modes, which is highly relevant for characterizing many-body systems in condensed matter theory.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is developing a formal framework, termed 'quantum mereology,' to decompose an arbitrary quantum Hamiltonian into weakly interacting factors that mimic local lattice dynamics.

**Main result.** The authors show that by minimizing interaction terms in factorizations, one can successfully re-interpret the dynamics of highly non-local random matrix models (like GUE) as wave packet propagation on an emergent 1D lattice structure.

**Method.** The methodology involves iterative Hamiltonian factorization ($\hat{H} \simeq \hat{H}_q \otimes \hat{H}_r$) by minimizing a loss function related to the interaction term, followed by mapping the resulting quasi-integrable model onto fermionic operators via Jordan-Wigner transformation.

**Model / system.** The study applies to general quantum systems described by Hamiltonians ($\hat{H}$), including random matrix models (GUE) and continuum field theories like the Pauli field Hamiltonian. The focus is on finding emergent, localized degrees-of-freedom (DOFs).

**Key observables.** Wave packet evolution $|\langle i|\psiangle|^2$, effective dispersion relation $E_{	ext{eff}}(k)$, and the minimization of the interaction strength $	ext{Tr}(\hat{H}_{	ext{int}}^2)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension $d$ of the Hilbert space, the coupling constants ($h_\ell$), and the excitation energy ($	ext{Eq}$) derived from spectral analysis.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Key assumptions include approximating mutual information by $rac{1}{2d}	ext{Tr}(\hat{H}_{	ext{int}}^2)$ and assuming that the optimal factorization can be found via local minimization of this loss function, which may fail for overlapping DOFs.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate wave packet evolution comparing non-local vs. localized dynamics; they also show spectral density plots confirming minimum interaction points (Bessel peaks) and MDS embeddings suggesting emergent 1D circular geometry.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from demonstrating the transformation of random matrices to local theories, developing the iterative factorization algorithm based on minimizing interaction loss, deriving theoretical constraints using asymptotic eigenvalue densities (GUE/Wishart), and finally applying this framework to known integrable models like the Pauli field.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The freedom to change Fock basis seems to ensure a minimum amount of locality in lattice theories in the following sense: If $\lbrace (\hat a_i^\dagger\,,\,\hat a_i)\rbrace$ for $i=1,\dots,n$ is a lattice of creation and annihilation operators and if a given Hamiltonian $\hat H$ induces highly non-local dynamics on that lattice, then it will usually be possible to change to a new set of operators $\lbrace (\hat b_i^\dagger\,,\,\hat b_i)\rbrace$ in terms of which the dynamics appear less non-local. We demonstrate this by turning a highly non-local random matrix model into a local, 1D lattice theory where particles can propagate in localized wave packets. More generally, we show that any Hamiltonian can be made to look like such a theory, with the lattice dispersion relation and the non-integrability of the theory depending on the spectrum of $\hat H$. We argue that our results are a step towards quantum mereology for fields.

</details>

**Summary.** This experimental study uses $^{33}$S NMR on NiPS3 to probe its electronic structure, arguing that the material hosts a spin-charge-intertwined Zhang-Rice triplet state. By analyzing Knight shifts and observing a power-law divergence in the relaxation rate near the Néel temperature, the authors provide evidence for nematic charge fluctuations driving the magnetic ordering.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work connects advanced spectroscopic techniques (NMR) directly to fundamental concepts in strongly correlated materials theory, specifically identifying signatures of emergent quantum states like the Zhang-Rice triplet and coupling them to magnetic phase transitions via critical dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to provide microscopic evidence for a Zhang-Rice triplet state in the van der Waals antiferromagnet NiPS3 and understand the origin of its magnetic exciton coherence.

**Main result.** NMR measurements reveal strong hybridization between S 3p and Ni 3d orbitals, supporting a spin-charge-intertwined ground state. Furthermore, critical slowing down observed via $^{33}$S relaxation suggests nematic correlations drive the magnetic transition.

**Method.** The research employed solid-state Nuclear Magnetic Resonance (NMR) spectroscopy on enriched single crystals of NiPS3, measuring Knight shifts and nuclear spin-lattice relaxation rates ($T_1^{-1}$).

**Model / system.** The physical system is NiPS3, a van der Waals antiferromagnet. The theoretical framework involves modeling the ground state as a Zhang-Rice triplet (ZRT) state arising from strong hybridization between ligand holes (S 3p) and transition metal moments (Ni 3d).

**Key observables.** Temperature dependence of $^{33}$S and $^{31}$P Knight shifts, power-law divergence of the $^{33}$S nuclear spin-lattice relaxation rate ($T_1^{-1}$), and the local Electric Field Gradient (EFG) tensor components.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Néel temperature $T_N = 155$ K; Quadrupole frequency $
u_Q = 6.4 	ext{ MHz}$; Critical exponent related to power-law divergence ($\propto |T - T_0|^{-0.464}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the observed NMR signals are sensitive probes of local spin and charge fluctuations, allowing for the differentiation between different types of magnetic correlations (e.g., nematic vs. simple AFM).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the crystal structure details related to the EFG tensor components and show the temperature dependence of various spectroscopic measurements, such as Knight shifts and relaxation rates.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the material's magnetic properties (AFM ordering), then using NMR spectroscopy on $^{33}$S and $^{31}$P to probe local spin susceptibility via Knight shifts. Finally, it analyzes the temperature dependence of $T_1^{-1}$ to pinpoint critical charge fluctuations associated with nematic correlations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum-entangled states underpin many emergent phenomena in quantum materials, yet their direct experimental identification remains a challenge. NiPS$_3$, a van der Waals antiferromagnet exhibiting a resolution-limited magnetic exciton in its ordered phase, has been proposed to host a many-body entangled Zhang-Rice triplet state. Here, using $^{33}$S nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) on $^{33}$S-enriched NiPS$_3$ single crystals, we provide microscopic evidence for this charge-transfer state. The $^{33}$S and $^{31}$P Knight shifts as a function of temperature reveal a unified spin-triplet configuration arising from strong hybridization between a self-doped hole in the S $3p$ orbitals and a hole in Ni $3d$ orbitals. Furthermore, the $^{33}$S nuclear spin-lattice relaxation rate exhibits a power-law divergence as it approaches the Néel temperature $T_N=155$ K, indicating critical slowing down of collective charge fluctuations consistent with spin-nematic correlations. These results reveal a spin-charge-intertwined ground state and establish the microscopic foundation for the exceptional coherence of the magnetic exciton in NiPS$_3$.

</details>

### [Determining universal spectra from probability distributions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10128v1)

**Authors:** Charles Kacir, Joseph Moscoso, Amy Nicholson, Thomas R. Richardson, Cade Rodgers  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10128v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** The paper develops methods to extract universal spectral information about strongly interacting quantum matter by analyzing the probability distributions of two-particle correlation functions. It combines numerical lattice techniques with analytic large-$N$ expansions, showing that ratios of cluster energies provide scale-independent predictions. This methodology is crucial for understanding physics in regimes like unitarity where simple perturbative approaches fail.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it tackles the difficult problem of extracting fundamental, scale-invariant physics from complex many-body correlators in strongly interacting systems, a core challenge in modern quantum field theory and AMO research involving ultracold atomic gases near Feshbach resonances.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central goal is determining universal spectral information about interacting quantum systems by analyzing probability distributions derived from two-particle correlation functions.

**Main result.** The raw moments of the distribution are shown to be equivalent to the energies of $n$-body clusters, suggesting that ratios of these energies can yield cutoff-independent predictions for universal physics.

**Method.** The authors employ both numerical lattice computations and analytic expansions in the large-$N$ limit, utilizing cumulant expansions ($\kappa_n$) of the logarithm of correlation functions to extract spectral information.

**Model / system.** The study concerns strongly-interacting quantum matter fields (fermions $\psi$) interacting via bosonic auxiliary fields ($\phi$), relevant to theories like QCD and low-energy EFTs. The analysis focuses on two-fermion correlation functions $C_2(	au)$ in the context of non-relativistic, two-component fermions.

**Key observables.** Two-particle correlation function ($C_2(	au)$), cumulants ($\kappa_n$), and ratios of cluster energies ($E_{2n}/E_4$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Large Euclidean time ($	au 	o \infty$), the limit of large number of species ($N 	o \infty$), and parameters related to unitarity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes that the distribution approaches a log-normal form, which simplifies the extraction of spectral information from moments. A key assumption is that universal physics can be extracted by removing non-universal cutoff effects via ratios.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares direct calculations of $n$-body energies against log-normal predictions for the $N=2$ correlator; Figure 2 shows cumulants plateauing, suggesting convergence to a stable spectral regime; Figure 3 illustrates differences in correlation function derivations between BCS and density channels.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the link between moments and cluster energies. It then develops two complementary theoretical frameworks—a lattice approach and an analytic large-$N$ expansion (comparing 'density' vs. 'BCS' channels)—to calculate correlation functions, ultimately deriving conditions for universal spectral determination.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The probability distribution of a two-particle correlation function computed over background auxiliary field configurations, used to generate the interactions, has been shown to inform about the spectra of universal $n$-body clusters [1]. Here, we utilize two approaches, a numerical lattice computation and an analytic expansion in the limit of large numbers of identical species, in an attempt to refine the initial predictions. Exploratory calculations in these directions are presented, and future investigations laid out.

</details>

**Summary.** The research investigates how periodic driving sequences affect quantum coherence in NV ensembles used for magnetometry. While these sequences successfully extend the measurable dephasing time ($	ext{T}_2^*$), the authors demonstrate that this improvement does not automatically boost DC magnetic-field sensitivity. They establish that finite-pulse Floquet analysis is necessary to accurately predict sensing limits by focusing on the phase transduction slope rather than just the coherence envelope.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a crucial theoretical refinement for quantum sensing, showing that simply maximizing coherence time is insufficient; the specific mechanism coupling detuning to phase accumulation (the transduction slope) must be optimized.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To determine if extending the effective dephasing time ($	ext{T}_2^*$) via periodic driving in NV ensembles necessarily leads to improved DC magnetic-field sensitivity.

**Main result.** Extended coherence times are not guaranteed to enhance DC sensitivity because the signal is governed by the detuning-to-phase transduction slope ($d\Phi/d\Delta$), which can be suppressed by phase wrapping effects during periodic driving.

**Method.** The study combines detuning-resolved stroboscopic spectroscopy with finite-pulse Floquet analysis to model coherence decay in periodically driven spin ensembles.

**Model / system.** The system is a high-density Nitrogen-Vacancy (NV) ensemble in diamond, modeled as an effective two-level or spin-1/2 system subjected to periodic control pulses like WAHUHA sequences. The dynamics are analyzed using the one-cycle unitary propagator derived from Floquet theory.

**Key observables.** Effective inhomogeneous dephasing time ($	ext{T}_2^*$), DC magnetic-field sensitivity, and the detuning-to-phase transduction slope ($d\Phi/d\Delta$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The WAHUHA sequence period ($	au$) and the detuning ($\Delta$) are key parameters controlling the Floquet phase landscape.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis moves beyond ideal Average Hamiltonian Theory (AHT) to incorporate finite pulse widths and variable interpulse delays, which modify the one-cycle evolution.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate stroboscopic FFT spectra showing linear branches at short delays and characteristic period-doubled ($2T$) responses due to phase wrapping at longer delays. Signal slope maps compare Ramsey vs. WAHUHA protocols across varying delay times.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the physical system (NV ensembles) and the theoretical framework (Floquet analysis). It then analyzes how periodic driving extends coherence ($	ext{T}_2^*$) but critically demonstrates that this extension does not translate to better sensing performance because of spectral reshaping effects governed by $d\Phi/d\Delta$.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

High-density nitrogen-vacancy (NV) ensembles are promising platforms for solid-state quantum sensing, but their performance is limited by dipolar interactions and inhomogeneous dephasing. Periodic decoupling sequences such as Waugh-Huber-Haeberlen (WAHUHA) can extend the observed stroboscopic decay time. However, it remains unclear that a longer effective dephasing time yield improved magnetic-field sensitivity. Here, we show that WAHUHA control increases the effective inhomogeneous dephasing time of a dense NV ensemble from $T_2^\ast$ of 0.9 $μ$s to $T_{2,eff}^\ast$ of 31 $μ$s, while producing little improvement in dc magnetic-field sensitivity. Using detuning-resolved stroboscopic spectroscopy and finite-pulse Floquet analysis, we show that the long-lived signal arises from phase wrapping and quasi-energy branch folding of the one-cycle unitary. These effects reshape the stroboscopic spectrum and suppress the detuning-to-phase transduction slope, $dΦ/dΔ$, which governs the dc magnetic-field response. Our results demonstrate that, under periodic driving, an extended effective dephasing time does not necessarily translate into enhanced dc sensitivity and establish finite-pulse Floquet analysis as a practical framework for evaluating coherence in spin ensembles.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.10991"></a>
### [Geometry still matters in quasi-one-dimensional single-file transport](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10991v1)

**Summary.** This work investigates single-file particle transport in quasi-1D channels, demonstrating that residual transverse geometry significantly influences large-scale collective transport laws. By deriving an exact fluctuating hydrodynamics equation, the authors show that geometric effects modify key observables like diffusivity and current fluctuations compared to idealized 1D models. This highlights that confinement is not merely a restriction of dimensionality but actively shapes the system's thermodynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous theoretical framework showing how geometric constraints (transverse confinement) persist and modify macroscopic transport properties even when the primary dynamics are restricted to one dimension, which is relevant for understanding confined quantum systems or mesoscopic devices.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To determine if residual transverse geometry affects large-scale transport laws in quasi-one-dimensional single-file systems, challenging the standard assumption that such effects are negligible once overtaking is forbidden.

**Main result.** The finite width of the channel dictates the coefficients of the effective 1D fluctuating hydrodynamics, causing collective diffusivity to become non-monotonic with density, a deviation from purely 1D models.

**Method.** Derivation of exact large-scale one-dimensional fluctuating hydrodynamics by coarse-graining microscopic Brownian dynamics and calculating transport coefficients via linear response theory and free energy analysis.

**Model / system.** Brownian particles confined in a channel with finite transverse width, exhibiting single-file transport (no overtaking). The system is analyzed using continuum field theories derived from the microscopic dynamics.

**Key observables.** Collective diffusivity ($D_S(ho)$), integrated current fluctuations ($\langle Q^2_t angle$), and tracer displacement fluctuations ($\langle X^2_t angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Finite channel width, density ($ho$), large length scales ($\Lambda$), and the interaction potential (e.g., hard-core repulsion).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on deriving an effective 1D hydrodynamics from a full confined geometry, assuming local equilibrium approximations for calculating pressure and transport coefficients.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate how geometry enters through transport coefficients, showing density dependence of current/tracer fluctuations that differs qualitatively from strictly 1D hard-rod descriptions; robustness is shown across different channel shapes and potentials.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the microscopic dynamics and derives the general fluctuating hydrodynamics equation. It then focuses on determining the effective pressure and transport coefficients for the single-file geometry using partition function methods, culminating in predictions for large-scale fluctuation observables under various initial conditions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Single-file transport means no overtaking: particles move in a narrow channel while preserving their longitudinal order. This simple constraint has profound dynamical consequences, most notably tracer subdiffusion, and has made single-file transport a paradigmatic form of confined many-body motion, observed from molecular transport in zeolites to single-file diffusion of colloids in narrow channels. The standard view is that, once overtaking is suppressed, collective transport reduces to that of a strictly one-dimensional file. Here we show that this reduction fails in experimentally relevant finite-width channels: even when exchange is forbidden, the transverse equilibrium structure controls the transport laws and can qualitatively reshape them. Starting from the Brownian dynamics in the full confined geometry, we derive an exact large-scale one-dimensional fluctuating hydrodynamics for the longitudinal density, whose coefficients are fixed by the confined equilibrium equation of state. In the minimal hard-core setting, this yields a collective diffusivity that can become non-monotonic in density. This geometric anomaly propagates to exact large-scale predictions for integrated-current fluctuations, tracer displacement fluctuations and the associated density profiles. The effect is robust to the interaction potential, channel geometry, initial preparation and microscopic dynamics. Quasi-one-dimensional single-file transport therefore defines a distinct regime in which forbidding overtaking does not erase geometry from collective transport.

</details>

### [Layer-parity-dependent interfacial coupling in Nb$_3$Cl$_8$/graphene van der Waals heterostructures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10741v1)

**Summary.** This work demonstrates that the surface polarization of the Mott insulator $	ext{Nb}_3	ext{Cl}_8$ critically controls its interaction with graphene. By fabricating heterostructures on surfaces exhibiting different polarizations, the authors show distinct electronic coupling strengths and measurable changes in transport gaps, highlighting layer-parity engineering as a tool for controlling correlated material properties.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit link drawn between crystal symmetry breaking (polarization) and tunable electronic coupling in a correlated material platform is highly relevant for designing novel quantum devices based on interfacial engineering.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how the layer-parity-dependent out-of-plane polarization of a Mott insulator ($	ext{Nb}_3	ext{Cl}_8$) dictates the interfacial coupling strength and electronic properties when coupled with graphene.

**Main result.** Different surface polarizations lead to distinct coupling strengths, evidenced by measurable differences in hybridization gaps (25.2 meV vs 30.0 meV) and charge transfer magnitudes in transport measurements.

**Method.** The research combines experimental characterization using AFM/KPFM with magnetotransport measurements on fabricated devices, corroborated by DFT calculations.

**Model / system.** The system is a van der Waals heterostructure formed by coupling monolayer graphene (MLG) to $	ext{Nb}_3	ext{Cl}_8$, which exhibits layer-parity dependent polarization due to its Mott insulating nature.

**Key observables.** Hybridization gap ($\Delta$), charge transfer magnitude (inferred from Landau fan slopes), carrier density ($n$), and surface potential/polarization state.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Layer parity (odd vs. even layers), hybridization gap values (25.2 meV, 30.0 meV), and polarization direction (upward vs. downward).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The coupling mechanism is strongly dependent on the local surface termination/polarization state of the Mott insulator.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate AFM phase maps showing layer-parity dependence, KPFM potential mapping, and transport data (R-T, B-field) comparing devices built on different $	ext{Nb}_3	ext{Cl}_8$ phases.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the physical phenomenon of layer-parity dependent polarization using AFM/KPFM. It then builds dual-gate Hall devices on these distinct surface phases to measure transport properties, comparing results (e.g., gap size, carrier density) between phases associated with different polarizations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Strongly correlated two-dimensional systems provide compelling platforms for investigating exotic quantum phenomena. Niobium chloride (Nb$_3$Cl$_8$), a single-band Mott insulator, exhibits a remarkable out-of-plane polarization in its topmost layer that oscillates with layer parity, manifesting as an odd-even effect. Using atomic force microscopy (AFM) and Kelvin probe force microscopy (KPFM), this layer-parity-dependent polarization can be effectively characterized through surface morphology and potential mapping, enabling the unambiguous identification of different surface phases. We then fabricated dual-gate Hall devices by coupling different surface phases of Nb$_3$Cl$_8$ with monolayer graphene to investigate how the topmost-layer out-of-plane polarization influences interfacial coupling and the resulting transport behavior. Our results reveal significant phase-dependent variations in charge transfer, carrier densities, and hybridization gaps (25.2 meV for Phase 1 and 30.0 meV for Phase 2). Density functional theory calculations corroborate these experimental findings, showing that distinct out-of-plane polarizations in the topmost layer lead to different orbital overlaps and interfacial coupling strengths. These findings highlight the critical importance of surface polarization and orbital orientation in engineering the properties of strongly correlated van der Waals heterostructures.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper analyzes the spectral properties of weighted random networks by studying the Local Density of States (LDoS) distribution. Using the cavity method, it determines that the LDoS exhibits a universal power-law tail with an exponent of 3. Furthermore, it characterizes the corresponding eigenvectors as weakly multifractal, providing quantitative measures like fractal dimensions and scaling exponents.

**Why it may be interesting.** The findings on weak multifractality and non-trivial IPR scaling provide quantitative benchmarks for understanding spectral localization transitions in complex disordered quantum systems, which is relevant to many open quantum system models.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the local density of states (LDoS) distribution and the multifractal nature of eigenvectors in weighted random networks.

**Main result.** The LDoS distribution is found to exhibit a universal power-law tail with an exponent of 3, indicating that the corresponding eigenvectors are weakly multifractal. The system shows unusual logarithmic scaling for the Inverse Participation Ratio (IPR).

**Method.** The analysis heavily relies on the cavity method and advanced techniques from Random Matrix Theory, including perturbative expansions and saddle-point approximations.

**Model / system.** The model is a weighted Erdős-Rényi graph ensemble characterized by weighted adjacency matrices $J$. The investigation analyzes spectral properties across different phases (extended, localized, multifractal).

**Key observables.** Local Density of States ($ho_i(\omega)$), Singularity Spectrum ($f(\alpha)$), Fractal Dimensions ($D(q)$), and the Inverse Participation Ratio ($I_2$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis is sensitive to the weight distribution, particularly requiring a non-zero probability mass at zero for certain exponents. The system behavior is analyzed in the thermodynamic limit ($N 	o \infty$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Key approximations include truncating moment expansions (e.g., $O(p^{-2})$) and using Laplace's method to derive asymptotic forms for the LDoS distribution.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the global density of states, the LDoS power-law tail behavior, and the singularity spectrum derived from eigenvector statistics.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops approximations for the resolvent elements using the cavity method to determine the LDoS distribution. It then relates the asymptotic tails of this distribution to the scaling properties of the eigenvectors, deriving fractal dimensions and symmetry relations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the local density of states (LDoS) distribution of a general class of weighted Erdős-Rényi graphs. Using the cavity method, we obtain a good approximation to the full LDoS distribution and compact expressions for its power-law tails, which we show to have exponent $3$ in the extended phase. We deduce that the eigenvectors in the continuous part of the spectrum are extended but (weakly) multifractal, and we extract expressions for the associated fractal dimensions and the singularity spectrum. We also demonstrate that the inverse participation ratio in this multifractal phase exhibits an unusual logarithmic scaling with system size, which is neither fully-extended nor localised by the usual definitions. Finally, we verify that some symmetry properties (derived from the non-linear sigma model), which have been shown to hold for many systems exhibiting multifractality, also hold in our case, both for the LDoS distribution and the singularity spectrum.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10266"></a>
### [A Recrossing-Free Dividing Surface in Quantum Mechanics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10266v1)

**Authors:** Pouya Khazaei  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10266v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10266_figures/2606.10266_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. (a) Phase-space geometry at t0 = 0. The stable and unstable manifolds, W s(t0) and W u(t0), intersect at the bounded quantum transition-state trajectory Xb(t0); the ver- tical dashed line x = xb(t0) is the corresponding instanta- neous dividing surface. Because Xb(t0) lies at this intersec- tion, it anchors a recrossing-free dividing surface. (b) The bounded trajectory xb(t), which stays localized for all time and whose projection traces the time-dependent recrossing- free dividing surface. Parameters: the barrier-top model for cis–cis proton transfer in malonaldehyde, with Gaussian wavepacket parameters q0 = −1, p0 = 3.6, A0 = 1</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10266_figures/2606.10266_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">

Lagrangian descriptor of the quantum Hamiltonian flow for the barrier-top model motivated by cis–cis proton transfer in malonaldehyde, evaluated at t0 = 0. The de- scriptor L(x0, p0, t0) is computed over a finite time window τ = 4000 a.u. using α = 0.2. Sharp features in the forward and backward contributions identify the stable and unstable manifolds, respectively. Their intersection, marked by the dot, gives the bounded quantum transition-state trajectory and hence the recrossing-free dividing surface.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper resolves a long-standing theoretical conflict by proving that a recrossing-free dividing surface exists in the exact quantum flow. It achieves this by analyzing the system's invariant manifold structure, which dictates a unique bounded trajectory defining the reaction barrier. This confirms that classical concepts of reactive flux are fundamentally preserved in quantum mechanics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous mathematical foundation for applying classical concepts like transition state theory to quantum dynamics by showing how geometric invariants (invariant manifolds) dictate non-classical transport features.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To demonstrate that a recrossing-free dividing surface, necessary for defining one-way reactive flux in quantum mechanics, exists despite apparent conflicts with the uncertainty principle.

**Main result.** The existence of a unique bounded trajectory, anchored by an intersection of stable and unstable invariant manifolds, confirms that the geometric framework of classical transition state theory extends to the exact quantum flow.

**Method.** The analysis uses a trajectory-based formulation derived from the Schrödinger equation, employing techniques from nonautonomous dynamical systems theory, specifically establishing exponential dichotomy.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled using quantum reaction dynamics, exemplified by the cis–cis proton transfer in malonaldehyde. The underlying physics involves solving coupled equations derived from the continuity and Hamilton-Jacobi equations, leading to an effective Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** One-way flux of the standard quantum probability current ($j$), the rate coefficient $k(t)$, and the unique bounded trajectory $X_b(t)$ defining the dividing surface $\Sigma(t)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The system dynamics are analyzed in regimes where linear nonautonomous approximations hold, particularly for Gaussian wavepacket evolution.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The initial analysis relies on approximating the dynamics by retaining only quadratic terms, although this approximation is shown to be exact for specific wavepacket dynamics against a harmonic barrier.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 schematically illustrates the proton transfer reaction. Figure 2 shows phase-space geometry, illustrating how the intersection of stable and unstable manifolds defines the instantaneous dividing surface $x = x_b(t)$.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the theoretical conflict, derives the necessary quantum equations of motion using trajectory methods, proves the existence of a unique bounded solution via exponential dichotomy, and finally applies this framework to calculate reaction rates using flux conservation across the resulting recrossing-free surface.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

For nearly a century, a recrossing-free dividing surface in quantum mechanics has been thought impossible. One-way reactive flux seems to require simultaneous trajectory-level knowledge of position and momentum -- an apparent conflict with the uncertainty principle. We show that this obstruction is not fundamental. The exact quantum flow can admit stable and unstable invariant manifolds whose intersection defines a unique bounded trajectory. This trajectory anchors a moving dividing surface that reactive quantum characteristics cross exactly once, producing a one-way flux of the standard quantum probability current. The geometric framework underlying classical reaction dynamics therefore carries over to the exact quantum flow, in a fundamentally quantum form.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10397"></a>
### [Improved selector behavior in ultrathin chromium-doped V$_2$O$_3$ films](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10397v1)

**Authors:** Johannes Mohr, Tyler Hennen, Yudi Wang, Xiaoyu Xu, Loc Vinh, Dirk J. Wouters, Rainer Waser, Joyeeta Nag, Daniel Bedau  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10397v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
