**Summary.** This work investigates using ultrathin chromium-doped V2O3 films as promising selector elements for advanced memory devices. By achieving stable switching down to 5 nm, the authors demonstrate that interfacial amorphous layers, rather than bulk crystallinity, govern the desirable electrical properties like low leakage current and sharp transitions. The findings guide future device design by pinpointing the critical role of interface engineering.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on solid-state electronics, the underlying physics involves Mott transitions and charge localization in correlated oxides, which touches upon concepts relevant to strongly correlated electron systems studied theoretically.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing reliable selector elements for next-generation memory and neuromorphic devices using ultrathin chromium-doped V2O3 films.

**Main result.** The switching effect is maintained down to 5 nm, with improved low leakage current and abrupt transitions observed. The electrical behavior suggests that the switching mechanism is dominated by a thin amorphous interfacial layer rather than the bulk crystalline material.

**Method.** Electrical characterization (I-V sweeps) combined with advanced structural/compositional analysis using TEM, EELS, and EDX mapping.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a device structure based on chromium-doped V2O3 acting as a Mott-insulator exhibiting negative differential resistance via an electronically triggered metal-insulator transition. The study compares performance in both crystalline and amorphous film forms.

**Key observables.** Switching effect, Negative Differential Resistance (NDR), leakage current ($\mu	ext{A}$ range), forming voltage, and interfacial elemental distribution.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Film thickness down to 5 nm; doping concentration (15% Cr); operating voltages relative to threshold voltages.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The improved properties are attributed to the formation of a thin amorphous layer at the TiN interface, which dictates the resistive switching mechanism.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate device cross-sections, I-V curves showing NDR behavior after forming, and elemental maps revealing complex dopant distribution and Ti diffusion into the electrode interface.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the potential of Cr:V2O3 for selectors to presenting electrical measurements across varying thicknesses. It then uses TEM/EDX to analyze structural changes (amorphous layers) that correlate with the observed improved electrical performance, concluding with scaling predictions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Devices based on the negative differential resistance effect in chromium doped V$_2$O$_3$ are considered to be promising as selector elements for use in emerging memory technologies, as well as for neuromorphic applications. It is shown by electrical measurements, that the switching effect is maintained for very thin films down to 5 nm, and even improved properties such as a low leakage current and an abrupt transition are observed. For these thicknesses, the behavior of crystalline and amorphous films becomes very similar; most strikingly, a forming step is required in both. Transmission electron microscopy reveals this to be likely due to a thin amorphous layer that forms at the interface to the TiN electrode. Elemental mapping further shows a complex distribution of the chromium dopants, as well as a diffusion of Ti into the layer from the electrode, which might be responsible for the improved properties.

</details>

### [Pair creation amplitudes for a real scalar field coupled to a time-dependent surface in d+1 dimensions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10800v1)

**Authors:** C. D. Fosco, B. C. Guntsche  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10800v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10800_figures/2606.10800_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Pair-creation rate γ(ω) of Eq. (55) (in units of b2K2 s/(4π)) as a function of ω/Ks, for several values of λ/Ks. **Summary.** This theoretical paper investigates pair creation from a scalar field interacting with a moving boundary. It advances the calculation to fourth order, demonstrating that while lower orders confirm standard quantum field theory relations (like the optical theorem), higher orders reveal complex non-linear physics, including new second-harmonic emission and vacuum depletion effects.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to open quantum systems and quantum optics because it provides a rigorous framework for calculating non-perturbative vacuum excitation processes (like the Dynamical Casimir Effect) beyond simple leading-order approximations, showing how nonlinearities manifest in multi-pair channels.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study calculates pair creation amplitudes for a real scalar field due to its interaction with a time-dependent, deforming surface in d+1 dimensions.

**Main result.** The calculation establishes that while the leading order probability correctly reproduces twice the imaginary part of the effective action (the optical theorem), higher orders reveal nonlinear effects, such as genuine second-harmonic emission and vacuum depletion corrections.

**Method.** The authors employ perturbative quantum field theory techniques, expanding transition amplitudes in powers of the surface deformation parameter ($\psi$) and comparing results derived from exclusive scattering amplitudes with those obtained from the imaginary part of the effective action.

**Model / system.** The system involves a massless real scalar field coupled to a time-dependent boundary defined by $\Sigma$. The interaction is modeled via an action term proportional to $\delta(x_d - \psi(\mathbf{x}_q))$, analyzed in the limit of Dirichlet boundary conditions.

**Key observables.** Pair creation probability density, radiated power per unit solid angle ($rac{d\Omega}{Aq} rac{dW}{dk_0}$), and the total decay probability $P^{(2)}$ and $P^{(4)}$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The surface deformation function $\psi(\mathbf{x}_q)$, the coupling constant $\lambda$, and the dimensionality $d+1$. The analysis focuses on high-order corrections (up to fourth order).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calculation relies heavily on parity selection rules for non-vanishing terms, and several results are derived in the limit where the boundary condition is strictly Dirichlet ($\lambda 	o +\infty$).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates an angular distribution ($\propto \cos	heta$) for a harmonically oscillating bump. Figure 2 compares the pair-creation rate $\gamma(\omega)$ vs. $\omega/K_s$ for varying coupling strengths, showing spectral departures. Figure 3 shows the cutting rule relating the discontinuity of the effective action to the two-particle phase-space integral.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically calculates the amplitude expansion ($A = \sum A^{(n)}$) and the resulting probability density ($	ext{dP} \propto |A|^2$) up to fourth order. It then rigorously compares these direct calculations with established identities involving the imaginary part of the effective action, highlighting deviations at higher orders.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the pair creation phenomenon for a real scalar field $\varphi$ in the presence of a surface that undergoes time-dependent deformations, while imposing Dirichlet-like boundary conditions. Including terms up to fourth order in the departure of the surface from an infinite plane, we present results for the angular dependence of the emission rate for the vacuum-to-pair process as a function of the geometry and the dynamics of the surface, as well as of the momenta of the emitted pair. We check the consistency of the leading contribution with previous results obtained from the imaginary part of the effective action, and clarify how the relation between exclusive probabilities and the imaginary part of the effective action is modified at fourth order by the opening of a two-pair channel.

</details>

### [Robust self-testing based on Gisin's arbitrary-input Bell inequality](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10983v1)

**Authors:** Rajdeep Paul, Alok Kumar Pan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10983v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This paper develops a comprehensive framework for robust self-testing of quantum states using Gisin's arbitrary-input Bell inequality. It uses an SOS approach to find optimal theoretical bounds and then extends this theory to account for inevitable experimental noise. The result provides quantifiable fidelity thresholds, allowing researchers to determine the minimum required quality of measurements needed to certify nonlocality.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides rigorous theoretical tools to bridge the gap between idealized quantum theory predictions (optimal Bell violation) and practical experimental limitations imposed by noise, which is crucial for assessing real-world quantum device performance.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of robustly certifying the quantum nature of a state and measurements using Gisin's arbitrary-input Bell inequality (GBI) in realistic, noisy experimental settings.

**Main result.** They derive dimension-independent optimal quantum bounds for the GBI violation and establish concrete fidelity bounds that quantify how close an imperfect measurement must be to the ideal case to still certify entanglement/nonlocality.

**Method.** The core methodology involves using a systematic Sum-of-Squares (SOS) approach for optimization, followed by developing a robust framework that analyzes the trace distance between ideal and noisy measurement outcomes.

**Model / system.** The system is a bipartite quantum state $|\psiangle_{AB}$ undergoing measurements defined by $n$ settings on both parties. The analysis generalizes to arbitrary number of inputs ($n$) and incorporates noise models ($\epsilon$).

**Key observables.** Gisin's Bell Inequality violation $\langle G_n angle$, State Fidelity $F_s(r)$, Observable Fidelity $f_o(r)$, and the relative observed violation $r$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The number of measurement settings ($n$), the error parameter ($\epsilon$), and the relative observed violation ($r$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The robustness analysis assumes that errors primarily arise from imperfect observable implementations, allowing for bounding the trace distance between ideal and noisy measurements.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the Swap circuit used for self-testing and show trade-offs between increasing $n$ (more settings) versus increased vulnerability to noise.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically builds from deriving the optimal quantum violation using SOS techniques for general $n$, detailing specific cases ($n=3, 4$), introducing the swap circuit realization, and finally developing a comprehensive robust analysis quantifying fidelity bounds based on measurement errors ($\epsilon$).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Self-testing refers to the strongest device-independent (DI) certification method that validates the nature of a quantum system and devices solely based on the observed statistics. We demonstrate the self-testing of state and measurements based on the Gisin Bell inequality (GBI) featuring arbitrary inputs for both parties. We introduce a systematic and elegant sum-of-squares (SOS) approach that enables the dimension-independent derivation of the optimal quantum violation of GBI. We derive the state and the interrelation between the local observables directly from the optimization condition. Since the practical experimental scenario involves inevitable noise and imperfection, we present a comprehensive strategy for robust self-testing.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10486"></a>
### [Virial stress in systems of active Brownian particles in the presence of translational and rotational inertia](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10486v1)

**Authors:** Chandranshu Tiwari, Sunil P. Singh, Roland G. Winkler  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10486v1>

**Summary.** This paper rigorously derives the stress tensor for active Brownian particles incorporating both translational and rotational inertia in 2D. It distinguishes between periodic and confined geometries, finding that while an equation of state holds in bulk periodic systems, confinement introduces wall-induced polarization that invalidates simple constitutive relations. The findings emphasize that inertial effects necessitate including angular-velocity contributions beyond just the 'swim stress' to accurately describe mechanical stress.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed mechanical description of non-equilibrium matter driven by self-propulsion and inertia, which is crucial for understanding active matter systems relevant to biological or synthetic soft matter physics. The rigorous derivation of stress components beyond simple swim stress is highly valuable.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study elucidates the stress tensor within a system of active Brownian particles augmented with translational and rotational inertia (ABP+TRI). The goal is to understand how these inertial effects modify the mechanical description of activity, especially when comparing periodic versus confined geometries.

**Main result.** In periodic systems, an equation of state exists for the bulk stress. However, in confined systems, strong wall polarization breaks this simple relationship, and the swim stress alone is insufficient to characterize the total stress tensor due to inertia effects.

**Method.** The authors derive stress tensors using generalized virial theorems applied to the equations of motion (including inertial terms) for both periodic and confined boundary conditions. Langevin simulations are used to validate theoretical predictions across various parameter regimes.

**Model / system.** The model consists of $N$ self-propelled point particles whose dynamics include translational damping, rotational damping, and explicit translational ($m$) and rotational ($I$) inertia terms. Interactions involve repulsive Lennard-Jones potentials, and the system is analyzed in 2D under periodic or confined boundary conditions.

**Key observables.** Stress tensor components ($\sigma^{(k)}, \sigma^{(sm)}, \sigma^{(av)})$, local/global stress tensors, equation of state dependence, particle density profiles, and local polarization $p(x)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Translational inertia ($m$), rotational inertia ($I$), Péclet number (Pe), reduced mass ($M$), and reduced moment of inertia ($J$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies on applying generalized virial theorems, which are complicated by the inclusion of inertial terms. Approximations are used for correlation functions to derive closed-form stress expressions.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the spatial dependence of local stress components ($\sigma_{i,xx}$) near walls in confined systems, showing polarization and density accumulation, and compare global internal stresses between periodic and confined geometries as a function of inertia parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from deriving general stress tensor formulations using virial theorems for inertial active particles. It then contrasts the results obtained under periodic versus confined boundary conditions, analyzing how confinement breaks simple constitutive relations like the equation of state.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We elucidate the stress in a system of active Brownian particles augmented with translational and rotational inertia (ABP+TRI). Stress tensors are derived for periodic systems as well as systems confined between walls by employing Lagrange's equations of motion of the first kind for the rotational motion. Using Langevin simulations of an ideal active gas in two dimensions, we confirm the existence of an equation of state for periodic systems that depends on translational and rotational inertia in general. Confinement implies a strong polarization of the propulsion direction near a wall and an enhanced density, both of which increase with increasing rotational inertia. This affects the local stress tensor normal to the confining walls, leading to a breakdown of the equation of state. Yet the local stress in the bulk part of the confined systems is identical with that of the periodic system. Importantly, for both kinds of boundary conditions, the so-called swim stress is not included in the local stress tensor; thus, in general, the swim stress is not representative of the stress in systems of ABP+TRIs.

</details>

### [Certification of Network Quantum Sensing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10700v1)

**Authors:** Matteo Rosati, Gabriele Bizzarri, Marco Barbieri  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10700v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum optics experiment` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

At the same time, an adversarial user, Eve, manipulates the network in order to gather as much information as possible about ϕ and tamper with the probe received by Bob. <figure class="paper-figure-card">

The gate comprises a partially polarising beam splitter (PPBS), which allows for polarisation-selective nonclassical interference. **Summary.** This paper presents a novel quantum remote sensing protocol to simultaneously certify high measurement precision and robust security against eavesdropping. By employing offline Pauli-twirling on entangled photon states, the authors force the channel into a predictable form while preserving metrological sensitivity. This provides a practical pathway for achieving quantum-limited performance in real-world, insecure quantum networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly tackles the intersection of quantum metrology and quantum cryptography, providing concrete protocols that resolve long-standing theoretical tensions in secure quantum communication channels.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is simultaneously guaranteeing optimal estimation precision (metrology) and rigorous security against eavesdropping in distributed quantum sensing networks.

**Main result.** The authors introduce a protocol using offline bilateral Pauli-twirling that certifies both high metrological sensitivity and information security, showing the two goals are compatible.

**Method.** They employ theoretical analysis involving bounding estimation errors (MSE) for both legitimate users and eavesdroppers, utilizing quantum Fisher Information derived from Pauli correlators.

**Model / system.** The system models a tripartite interaction between Alice, Bob, and an adversary Eve over an untrusted quantum channel. The protocol is demonstrated experimentally using entangled photons to estimate an optical phase.

**Key observables.** Estimation error (MSE) for legitimate users vs. eavesdropper; Quantum Fisher Information (QFI); Pauli correlators ($\langle P_eta \otimes P_eta angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The estimated phase $\phi$; the number of measurements ($n$); and the fidelity $F(ho_\phi, ho_{\phi+\epsilon})$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Initial analysis assumes i.i.d. attacks from Eve; the derivation relies on small-angle approximations for fidelity calculations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the concept of certifiable SPQR settings, show experimental measurements of estimated phases vs. theory, and compare total versus estimated uncertainties ($\Delta^2\phi$) against theoretical bounds.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops by first defining the security/metrology tension, introducing Pauli twirling as a solution, deriving mathematical bounds using Fisher information for both parties, and concluding with experimental validation using entangled photons.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The distribution of quantum sensors on quantum networks is a key enabler of quantum technologies in interferometry, gravimetry, timekeeping, biological monitoring, and beyond. Yet, guaranteeing the security of these distributed sensors over noisy, insecure networks remains a formidable challenge. Previous efforts to combine quantum metrology and cryptography have encountered an apparently unavoidable tension, proposing bounds for security which are only loosely tied to the achievable measurement performance.   Here we introduce a quantum remote sensing protocol that can rigorously certify privacy and integrity of the estimation. By employing offline bilateral Pauli-twirling, our approach forces the effective quantum channel into a Bell-diagonal form, independently of the attack. Surprisingly, this also preserves metrological sensitivity without introducing additional experimental overhead. Relying solely on public communication alongside an insecure quantum link, the protocol enables legitimate users to exactly quantify their estimation error relative to an eavesdropper controlling the channels.   We experimentally demonstrate this framework by estimating an optical phase using entangled photons, observing that the users' precision consistently surpasses the eavesdropper's capabilities across a broad parameter regime. By unifying quantum cryptography and metrology, our results provide a practical pathway to achieve simultaneous quantum-limited precision and rigorous information security in real-world quantum networks.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10419"></a>
### [Antiferromagnetic order and magnetic polarons in lightly doped Li$_x$CoO$_2$ (x $\sim$ 0.9)](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10419v1)

**Authors:** Sudip Pal, Pampa Sadhukhan, Waqar Suleman, Andrej Pustogow, S. B. Roy  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10419v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This work investigates the magnetism of $	ext{Li}_x	ext{CoO}_2$ by combining magnetization, specific heat, NMR, and EPR measurements. The findings reveal evidence for both weak antiferromagnetic ordering at low temperatures and a persistent weak ferromagnetism arising from magnetic polarons formed by doped holes. The study models these phenomena using polaron hopping dynamics, highlighting the complex interplay between charge carriers, spin correlations, and structural disorder in correlated oxides.

**Why it may be interesting.** The interplay between charge localization (polarons), magnetic ordering ($	ext{AFM}/	ext{FM}$ coexistence), and ionic disorder ($	ext{Li}^+$ diffusion) in a correlated oxide system provides rich physics relevant to understanding emergent quantum states in strongly interacting electron systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the complex magnetic properties of lightly doped $	ext{Li}_x	ext{CoO}_2$ ($x \sim 0.9$), aiming to understand the origin of its low-temperature magnetic order and history dependence.

**Main result.** The material exhibits evidence for weak antiferromagnetic ordering below $T_N \sim 10 	ext{ K}$ alongside a persistent weak ferromagnetic response, which is attributed to the formation and diffusion of localized magnetic polarons.

**Method.** A combination of bulk measurements (magnetization, specific heat), spectroscopic techniques ($	ext{NMR}$, $	ext{EPR}$), and structural analysis ($	ext{XRD}$) were employed across various temperature regimes.

**Model / system.** The system is the layered transition metal oxide $	ext{Li}_x	ext{CoO}_2$. The physics involves localized spins interacting via doped holes, leading to the formation of magnetic polarons which govern the magnetic response and transport properties.

**Key observables.** Magnetization ($	ext{M}$ vs $	ext{T}$, ZFC/FCW), specific heat anomaly near $10 	ext{ K}$, $	ext{NMR}$ spin-lattice relaxation rate ($1/T_1$), and $	ext{EPR}$ signal intensity, linewidth, and $g$-factor.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Low doping concentration ($x \sim 0.9$); low temperature regime ($	ext{T} < 300 	ext{ K}$); magnetic field strength (e.g., $100 	ext{ Oe}$, $9 	ext{ T}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The weak ferromagnetism and history dependence are linked to the formation of magnetic polarons; the low-temperature order is modeled as an antiferromagnetic transition in hole clusters.

**Figures summary.** Figures show $	ext{XRD}$ confirming structure, $	ext{M}-	ext{T}$ curves revealing ZFC/FCW irreversibility and anomalies near $10 	ext{ K}$, and $	ext{EPR}$ spectra demonstrating temperature-dependent signal changes consistent with polaron dynamics.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically presents evidence from multiple techniques: first, bulk measurements establish the low-$T$ AFM transition; second, magnetization hysteresis points to short-range ferromagnetism related to polarons; and finally, $	ext{EPR}$ and $	ext{NMR}$ provide microscopic details on polaron dynamics across different temperature regimes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the magnetic properties of Li$_x$CoO$_2$ (x$\sim$0.9) using bulk magnetization, specific heat, nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) and electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) spectroscopy measurements. The dc magnetization, specific heat and NMR measurements, which probe the macroscopic response, indeed show that this compound partially undergoes an antiferromagnetic transition below $T_N \sim$ 10 K. In addition, we observed a weak ferromagnetic response, which gives rise to the history dependence in magnetization measurements at low fields and is observed at temperatures above room temperature. We propose that there are ferromagnetic clusters at high temperatures due to the formation of magnetic polarons out of doped holes. In EPR measurements performed at the $X$-band frequency, only a fraction of the total spins contribute and show Curie-like paramagnetic behavior as reflected in the temperature dependence of the EPR intensity. The temperature variation of the EPR spectra can be understood in the framework of the diffusion of magnetic polarons.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10604"></a>
### [Propagation and localization of spin excitations at altermagnetic domain walls](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10604v1)

**Authors:** Oksana Peschanska, Jeroen van den Brink, Volodymyr P. Kravchuk  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10604v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper theoretically examines spin wave propagation and localization at domain walls in altermagnetic materials. By analyzing the system's Lagrangian, the authors show that the A$\ell$M interaction strongly modifies the excitation spectrum, particularly when the wall aligns along specific crystallographic directions. The findings predict the emergence of new bound states and field-induced asymmetries, offering insights into controlling magnetic excitations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides detailed theoretical predictions about how subtle symmetry-breaking mechanisms (like altermagnetism) fundamentally alter collective excitations (magnons) at structural defects (domain walls), which is crucial for spintronics applications.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how altermagnetism (A$\ell$M) modifies the propagation and localization of spin excitations at magnetic domain walls in easy-axial $d$-wave materials.

**Main result.** The A$\ell$M effect strongly depends on the domain wall orientation, leading to additional gapped bound states and removing eigenstate polarization degeneracy when the wall is oriented along specific high-symmetry directions like [110].

**Method.** The analysis uses a Lagrangian approach derived from the nonlinear sigma model, linearizing excitations via Holstein-Primakoff representation, and solving resulting differential equations (often mapping to hypergeometric forms).

**Model / system.** The system is an altermagnetic material ($	ext{A}\ell	ext{M}$) characterized by spin compensation and symmetry-induced spin splitting. The dynamics are modeled using a Lagrangian incorporating terms for antiferromagnetic stiffness, easy-axial anisotropy, and the A$\ell$M coupling strength ($A_{alt}$).

**Key observables.** Bound state existence, dispersion relation $\omega(k_y)$, localization area of bound states, and asymmetry in eigenstate intensity distribution.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimensionless A$\ell$M parameter ($\epsilon$), the domain wall orientation relative to crystallographic axes, and the strength of an applied static magnetic field ($B$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Approximations include using the exchange approximation for spin dynamics and linearizing excitations around a static domain wall profile. The mathematical analysis often relies on mapping to solvable differential equations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate dispersion relations comparing AFM vs A$\ell$M cases, showing localization region size dependence on eigenfrequency, and demonstrating spatial asymmetry induced by an external magnetic field.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from the general Lagrangian description of spin dynamics to analyzing planar wave excitations. It then focuses on solving for bound states using reduced Lagrangians, comparing results across different domain wall orientations (nodal vs maximal splitting) and finally incorporating the effects of a static magnetic field.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Altermagnets (A$\ell$Ms) are spin-compensated materials in which opposite-spin sublattices are connected by a symmetry that causes a spin splitting in their elementary excitations. As there is a strong effect of altermagnetism on domain wall properties, it is quite natural to also expect an enrichment of the physics of magnetic excitations at A$\ell$M domain walls. Here, we consider the propagation of spin eigen-excitations along domain walls in easy-axial $d$-wave A$\ell$Ms. Investigating the presence of bound states localized on a domain wall, we find that the effect of the A$\ell$M on the bound states strongly depends on the orientation of the domain wall relative to the crystallographic directions. If the domain wall is oriented along a nodal direction [100] or [010], A$\ell$M does not change the number of bound states; however, it leads to a nonlinear dispersion and a tilt of the wavefront. The effect of A$\ell$M is strongest when the domain wall is oriented along the directions [110] or [$\bar{1}$10], i.e., along the directions of the strongest A$\ell$M splitting in the magnon spectrum. In this case, (i) the additional gapped bound states appear, (ii) degeneracy of the eigenstates with respect to their polarization (right-handed or left-handed precession of the N{é}el vector) is removed, and (iii) the localization area of the bound states strongly depends on the eigenfrequency. The latter may lead to strong localization of the bound state at the domain wall. We further consider the influence of a static magnetic field that is applied along the easy axis, and find that the magnetic field induces an asymmetry between the localization regions on opposite sides of the domain wall and sets an upper limit on the absolute value of the propagating eigenstate's wave vector.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper reports the first direct neutron scattering evidence of long-period helimagnetism in Fe0.5Rh0.5Si, a B20 compound. By combining SANS measurements with low-field magnetometry, the authors construct a detailed field-temperature phase diagram and identify a candidate A-phase region. The work advances the understanding of tunable chiral magnets by linking magnetic anomalies to structural signatures.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides empirical evidence on tuning chiral magnetism using 4d substitutions (Rh) and directly probes emergent topological spin textures via neutron scattering techniques. This is highly relevant for understanding complex magnetic ground states beyond simple mean-field models.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to map out the magnetic phase diagram of the B20 helimagnet Fe0.5Rh0.5Si and characterize a candidate A-phase region within it.

**Main result.** The research successfully combined SANS measurements confirming long-period helical modulation (lambda_h ~ 79 nm) with magnetometry to define a field-temperature phase diagram, supporting the existence of a candidate A-phase region.

**Method.** The study employed a combination of low-field magnetometry (measuring M(H), dM/dH, and d^2M/dH^2) and Small-Angle Neutron Scattering (SANS) on polycrystalline samples.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the 4d-substituted B20 helimagnet Fe0.5Rh0.5Si, which exhibits magnetic ordering driven by crystallographic chirality and Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya interactions.

**Key observables.** Magnetic modulation wave vector (k_s), helical period (lambda_h), differential susceptibility (dM/dH), second derivative of magnetization (d^2M/dH^2), and field-dependent SANS intensity maxima.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Characteristic fields ($H_{c1}, H_{c1m}, H_{c2}$); ordering temperatures derived from magnetometry ($\sim 71 	ext{ K}$) and SANS ($\sim 70 	ext{ K}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The candidate A-phase region is not treated as an unambiguous thermodynamic phase boundary, and the interpretation relies on combining data from polycrystalline samples and transverse SANS geometry.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate M(H) isotherms showing magnetic anomalies; Figure 2 details diagnostic plots of susceptibility and curvature for the candidate A-phase; Figure 3 presents the combined H-T phase diagram incorporating field boundaries and the candidate region.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically uses magnetometry to define low-field phase transitions ($H_{c1}, H_{c1m}, H_{c2}$), then utilizes SANS to provide direct, complementary evidence of the underlying long-period helical magnetic structure, culminating in a combined phase diagram.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

This manuscript reports the first direct neutron-scattering evidence for long-period helimagnetism in the newly identified 4d-substituted B20 compound Fe0.5Rh0.5Si. By combining SANS with low-field magnetometry, we establish the magnetic modulation, construct a field-temperature phase diagram, and identify a candidate A-phase region supported by an independent structural signature. The work is important beyond this single compound because it expands the family of chiral B20 helimagnets into Rh-substituted materials, where spin-orbit coupling, disorder, and Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya interactions can be tuned. It will interest researchers in chiral magnetism, topological spin textures, magnetic neutron scattering, and quantum materials, and provides a foundation for future studies of emergent magnetic phases and topology-driven phenomena in chemically tuned chiral magnets.

</details>

### [Atomic-scale visualization of the toroidal order in a trimeric Dy(III) single-molecule toroic](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10077v1)

**Authors:** Michael J. Jenkins, Madalynn G. Marshall, Jie Xing, Muthu Satheeshkumar, Brandon D. Watson-Sanders, Xiaoping Wang, Christina M. Hoffmann, Gopalan Rajaraman, Huibo Cao, Rongying Jin, Zi-Ling Xue  
**Type:** both · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10077v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** The research reports on the first direct observation of toroidal magnetism in a single-molecule magnet using $	ext{Dy}_3$ complexes. By combining polarized neutron diffraction with variable-field measurements, the authors provide quantitative proof of this exotic magnetic ordering. This integrated methodology establishes a powerful new standard for investigating molecular quantum materials exhibiting complex spin correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a highly advanced experimental blueprint for characterizing exotic magnetic orders (toroidal magnetism) in molecular systems, which is crucial for designing next-generation quantum materials where spin chirality plays a role.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To unambiguously observe and probe the toroidal magnetic moment at the molecular scale within Single-Molecule Toroics (SMTs).

**Main result.** The authors report definitive experimental proof of the toroidal moment using a combined methodology, showing anti-parallelly stacked toroidal moments via variable-field neutron diffraction.

**Method.** The study integrates Polarized Neutron Diffraction (PND), variable-field neutron diffraction, magnetometry ($\mu$-SQUID), and *ab initio* calculations to map local magnetic susceptibility tensors.

**Model / system.** The system is a trimeric Dy(III) single-molecule complex, $[	ext{Dy}_3(	ext{OH})(	ext{teaH}_2)_3(	ext{paa})_3]	ext{Cl}(	ext{OMe})$, which utilizes the large spin of $	ext{Dy}^{3+}$ ions to host toroidal magnetic ordering.

**Key observables.** Toroidal moment, local magnetic susceptibility tensors ($\chi$), field-induced magnetization patterns, and ground state magnetic moments ($\sim 8.77 \mu_B$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Low temperatures (e.g., $5 	ext{ K}$), specific crystal structures derived from neutron diffraction, and energy gaps ($\Delta E = 1.6 	ext{ cm}^{-1}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The combined PND/variable-field approach is necessary to overcome the limitations of earlier indirect magnetometry methods.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate toroidal moment generation (current loops), magnetic susceptibility data ($\chi$ vs $T$), isothermal magnetization ($M$ vs $H$) along different axes, and field-induced magnetic order parameters from neutron diffraction.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the theoretical basis for toroidal moments, then presenting structural confirmations via SCND/SCXRD, followed by detailed measurements of magnetic anisotropy using PND and variable-field diffraction, culminating in a comprehensive model that links structure to the observed toroidal order.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Single-molecule toroics (SMTs) offer a unique platform for next-generation quantum devices utilizing head-to-tail spin alignments in the compounds. Presence of toroidal moments in SMTs has been essentially based on magnetometry and ab initio calculations. Here, we report observation and probe of the toroidal moment in [Dy3(OH)(teaH2)3(paa)3]Cl(OMe) [teaH3: triethanolamine; paaH: N-(2-pyridyl)-acetoacetamide] from mapping of Dy3+ magnetic susceptibility tensors by polarized neutron diffraction (PND). Neutron diffraction under variable magnetic fields demonstrates field-induced magnetization along the c-axis with toroidal moments anti-parallelly stacked, providing definite proof of the toroidal moment. Magnetometry studies confirm the toroidal ground state. For the first time, the combined use of PND, variable-field neutron diffraction, ab initio calculations, and magnetometry is introduced as a robust and quantitative methodology to probe molecular-scale toroidal magnetism. This integrated approach overcomes limitations of earlier indirect methods, establishes a benchmark framework for investigating SMTs, and provides valuable insights for the design of molecular quantum materials.

</details>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4 summarizes the Bean-model interpretation and the fitting procedures used to analyze the remanent response. Figures 4(a) and 4(b) illustrate the basic critical-state picture for the field-training protocol. When the training field satis- fies |HT| ≳Hp, the Bean-like profile after zeroing the ex-</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10300_figures/2606.10300_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 4. (a)(b) Magnetic-flux distribution B(r) arising from vortex pinning, as described by Bean’s critical-state model [2], for the field- training protocol used in this study. Here, Hp denotes the full flux penetration field in the phenomenological Bean-model picture. (a) When the training field satisfies |HT| ≳Hp, the final flux distribution B(r) at zero field is expected to be independent of HT. </div>

**Summary.** This work establishes MOKE as a complementary, highly sensitive optical tool to study vortex pinning in type-II superconductors like $	ext{Ba}_{0.62}	ext{K}_{0.38}	ext{BiO}_3$. By comparing MOKE signals with bulk magnetization data using the critical-state model, the authors confirm that the observed magnetic history dependence is due to trapped vortices, not exotic superconducting order parameters.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on superconductivity, the rigorous application of universal scaling functions ($F_{	ext{Deff}}(h)$) derived from continuum models (like the critical-state model) provides a powerful template for analyzing mesoscopic responses in other condensed matter systems exhibiting flux trapping or domain wall dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates vortex pinning, a critical mechanism governing irreversible magnetic response and energy dissipation in type-II superconductors.

**Main result.** The Magneto-Optical Kerr Effect (MOKE) proves to be a viable optical probe for studying vortex pinning, showing quantitative agreement with bulk magnetization measurements when analyzed via the Bean critical-state model.

**Method.** The authors employed ultra-high-resolution MOKE and detailed DC SQUID magnetization measurements on $	ext{Ba}_{1-x}	ext{K}_x	ext{BiO}_3$ single crystals under various field training protocols.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the oxide superconductor $	ext{Ba}_{0.62}	ext{K}_{0.38}	ext{BiO}_3$. The analysis relies heavily on adapting the Bean critical-state model to describe the remanent magnetic flux profile associated with trapped vortices.

**Key observables.** Zero-field MOKE signal ($\Delta	heta_K$), bulk magnetization ($M$ vs. $H_{	ext{eff}}$), and temperature dependence of susceptibility ($\chi$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Critical current density ($j_c(T)$), penetration field scale ($\mu_0 H_p$), and the training magnetic field magnitude ($|H_T|$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The remanent response is modeled using generalized saturation functions $F_{	ext{Deff}}(h)$, which relate local (MOKE, $	ext{Deff}=0$) and bulk ($	ext{Deff}=2$) measurements to the critical-state model.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate crystal structure, MOKE setup schematics, temperature dependence of both $M$ and $\Delta	heta_K$, and quantitative comparisons showing how both local and bulk responses follow universal saturation functions derived from the critical-state theory.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing the physical problem (vortex pinning), detailing the complementary experimental methods (MOKE vs. Magnetization), developing a unified theoretical framework using the Bean model, and finally applying this framework to quantitatively compare local and bulk measurements while ruling out alternative superconducting mechanisms like TRSB.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Vortex pinning plays a crucial role in determining properties of type-II superconductors. For example, it governs the irreversible magnetic response as well as dissipation caused by vortex motion. Here, we study vortex pinning in the three-dimensional oxide superconductor Ba1-xKxBiO3 using ultra-high-resolution magneto-optical Kerr effect (MOKE) and detailed magnetization measurements. We find that the zero-field MOKE signal in the superconducting state exhibits a pronounced magnetic-history dependence. This behavior closely resembles the remanent magnetization caused by trapped vortices. Furthermore, we demonstrate that the observed evolution of the MOKE signals is well described by Bean's critical-state model for trapped vortices. Our results establish MOKE as a viable optical and mesoscopic probe of vortex pinning in type-II superconductors, providing a new complementary approach to investigate mixed-state phenomena. We also find that the training-field dependence of the MOKE is linear near zero training field, without any anomalies indicative of spontaneous time-reversal-symmetry breaking in an unconventional superconducting state. Our study defines a clear protocol to distinguish vortex-induced MOKE responses from those associated with a time-reversal-symmetry broken superconducting order parameter.

</details>

### [Adaptive identification of low-degree polynomials in quantum singular value transformation: application to nonlinear quantum properties estimation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10994v1)

**Authors:** Jumpei Kato, Akira Tanji, Hiroyuki Harada, Kaito Wada, Kosuke Ito, Naoki Yamamoto  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10994v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10994_figures/2606.10994_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. The illustration of the idea. (Left) When using a polynomial to approximate a target function that is singu- lar at the origin, the conventional approach truncates the polynomial degree based on a worst-case threshold below the minimum eigenvalue of the state. (Right) Our proposed al- gorithm adaptively identifies the cutoff ˆλη with reference to the estimation error tolerance η of the target nonlinear prop- erty, while the conventional one takes a property-independent worst-case threshold. The cutoff ˆλη yields a sufficiently low- degree polynomial in the left figure.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10994_figures/2606.10994_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. The overall pipeline of the two-stage algorithm.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10994_figures/2606.10994_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. The algorithm search steps to find λη = 0.048. The flow progresses from (a) to (d) with gradually decreasing δ. ˆC shows the estimated CDF in red, and C shows the exact CDF in gray. λ∗identified by the algorithm is displayed in green. The red shaded areas display the CDF approximation error bound implied by Eq. (17). (a, b) There are no grid points satisfying Eq. (19), and λ∗= 0 is returned. </div>

**Summary.** The paper introduces an adaptive two-stage algorithm to estimate complex quantum properties like entropy by leveraging Quantum Singular Value Transformation (QSVT). Instead of relying on overly conservative worst-case bounds derived from state rank or minimum eigenvalues, the method first identifies a spectral cutoff $\hat{\lambda}_{\eta}$ specific to the required accuracy. This allows for using much lower-degree polynomials, drastically reducing the necessary number of quantum state copies required for accurate estimation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a significant algorithmic improvement for resource estimation in quantum information theory, directly addressing the computational bottleneck associated with high-degree polynomial approximations required for entropy estimation.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is efficiently estimating nonlinear quantum properties, such as von Neumann or Rényi entropy, of an unknown quantum state $\rho$ using the Quantum Singular Value Transformation (QSVT).

**Main result.** The authors propose a two-stage spectral cutoff method that allows for the use of significantly lower-degree polynomials compared to traditional worst-case bounds based on minimum eigenvalues or rank.

**Method.** They develop an adaptive algorithm where Stage 1 identifies a certified spectral cutoff $\hat{\lambda}_{\eta}$ from the state's spectrum, and Stage 2 uses this cutoff to determine the polynomial degree for QSVT estimation.

**Model / system.** The system involves estimating properties of an unknown quantum state $\rho$ (represented by a density matrix) in the sample-access model. The techniques are applied generally to various nonlinear functions $f(\rho)$ that define the target entropy or moment.

**Key observables.** Von Neumann entropy ($S(\rho)$), Rényi entropy ($R_\alpha(\rho)$), and general nonlinear properties $\text{tr}[f(\rho)\rho]$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Target accuracy ($\eta$, $\epsilon$), failure probability ($\vartheta$), spectral cutoff ($\hat{\lambda}_{\eta}$), and the polynomial degree ($D$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes that identifying a certified spectral cutoff $\hat{\lambda}_{\eta}$ is feasible, which bypasses reliance on knowing the minimum non-zero eigenvalue or rank.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the comparison between conventional truncation methods (requiring high polynomial degrees) and the proposed adaptive algorithm using the identified spectral cutoff $\hat{\lambda}_{\eta}$.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a two-stage algorithmic structure: first, an iterative search process (Algorithm 1) to identify the certified spectral cutoff $\hat{\lambda}_{\eta}$ based on error tolerances; second, applying QSVT with a degree determined by this cutoff to estimate the desired quantum property.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Estimating properties of unknown quantum states via quantum singular value transformation (QSVT) often requires high-degree polynomials to handle small eigenvalues of density matrices. Specifically, the existing approaches determine the polynomial degree by relying on overly conservative worst-case bounds based on the minimum non-zero eigenvalue or the rank of the density matrices. In this work, we propose a spectral cutoff method that truncates the negligible eigenvalue tail depending on the task, the target accuracy, and the state, which enables the use of significantly lower-degree polynomials. To implement this, we develop a two-stage algorithm to estimate nonlinear properties, particularly von Neumann entropy and R{é}nyi entropy. In the first stage, we execute a search algorithm to identify the spectral cutoff directly from the unknown quantum state. In the second stage, we estimate the nonlinear properties utilizing QSVT with the degree of polynomial adaptively determined by the cutoff. This two-stage algorithm significantly improves the overall estimation cost compared to known bounds, even without knowing the minimum eigenvalue or the rank.

</details>

**Summary.** This work analyzes phase transitions in the Ising-Heisenberg model on the extended Lieb lattice. By mapping it onto an effective Ising model, the authors map out a complex phase diagram showing both continuous critical lines and dome-shaped regions of discontinuous thermal transitions. The results are strongly confirmed by Monte Carlo simulations, providing a detailed understanding of magnetic ordering under external fields.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed characterization of both continuous (critical) and first-order (discontinuous) transitions in a frustrated quantum spin system provides rich insights into the interplay between quantum fluctuations and classical ordering mechanisms relevant to many condensed matter systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the nature (continuous vs. discontinuous) and location of thermal phase transitions within the spin-1/2 Ising-Heisenberg model.

**Main result.** The phase diagram reveals distinct regions characterized by four ground states (QAF, MD, FRI, FM), featuring both dome-shaped surfaces of discontinuous transitions and continuous transition lines.

**Method.** The authors employ an exact mapping technique using the Decoration-Iteration Transformation to reduce the model to an effective Ising model, supplemented by Monte Carlo simulations for verification.

**Model / system.** The system is a spin-1/2 Ising-Heisenberg model defined on the extended Lieb lattice subjected to an external magnetic field. The Hamiltonian involves couplings ($J_1, J$) and the field ($h$).

**Key observables.** Ground-state phase diagram, finite-temperature phase transitions (continuous vs. discontinuous), magnetization ($\langle m_I angle, \langle m_H angle$), and magnetic susceptibility ($\chi$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction ratios ($J_1/J$), external field strength ($h/J$), and temperature ($k_B T/J$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on an exact mapping of the complex model onto an effective Ising model, which is rigorously tested against Monte Carlo results.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate global phase diagrams in 3D parameter space ($J_1/J - h/J - k_B T/J$), showing distinct surfaces for continuous (blue) and discontinuous (red) transitions. Isothermal magnetization curves confirm plateaus associated with different phases.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the complex Hamiltonian on the Lieb lattice, deriving an effective Ising model via transformation techniques, mapping out zero-temperature ground states, and finally constructing global phase diagrams showing both continuous and discontinuous thermal transition surfaces.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The spin-1/2 Ising-Heisenberg model on the extended Lieb lattice in a magnetic field is exactly mapped onto an effective spin-1/2 Ising model on the square lattice. The ground-state phase diagram comprises the quantum antiferromagnetic (QAF), quantum monomer-dimer (MD), classical ferrimagnetic (FRI), and classical ferromagnetic phase. The MD-FRI ground-state phase boundary extends to finite temperatures as a dome-shaped surface of discontinuous thermal transitions bounded by a line of Ising critical points. The QAF phase is enclosed by a surface of continuous thermal transitions evolving from the QAF-MD and QAF-FRI ground-state phase boundaries. Monte Carlo simulations fully confirm the existence and nature of both continuous and discontinuous thermal phase transitions obtained by exact and approximate analytical calculations.

</details>

### [Nonreciprocal photon bundle emission](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10379v1)

**Summary.** The paper explores generating directional two-photon photon bundles using a coupled resonator system driven by quantum squeezing. By analyzing the system's dynamics under different input directions, the authors demonstrate that squeezing breaks symmetry, enabling emission only from one direction. This offers an all-optical method to control chiral quantum light sources.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it combines quantum squeezing (a key tool in AMO) with nonreciprocal effects and multiquanta light generation, providing an all-optical control mechanism for quantum sources.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates nonreciprocal photon bundle emission, a phenomenon controlled by directional quantum squeezing.

**Main result.** Directional quantum squeezing selectively induces two-photon bundle emission when the probe field enters from one direction while prohibiting it from the opposite direction.

**Method.** The dynamics are analyzed using Hamiltonian derivations and master equations, supplemented by Bogoliubov transformations and correlation function calculations.

**Model / system.** The system is a compound setup involving two coupled optical resonators (microcavities) and a two-level atom within the context of quantum optics. The interaction is modeled via an effective Hamiltonian incorporating squeezing parameters.

**Key observables.** Two-photon super-Rabi oscillation, second-order correlation functions ($g^{(2)}$), and photon populations in specific quantum states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Squeezing parameter (r), detuning ($\Delta$), cavity decay rates ($\kappa$), and atomic decay rate ($\gamma$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The rotating-wave approximation is used, and thermal effects are neglected due to high resonance frequencies.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically show the setup with input ports (Port 1/2) and pumping mechanisms. They illustrate super-Rabi oscillations via population dynamics and confirm nonreciprocity using $g^{(2)}$ correlation functions for different input directions.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the physical model, derives Hamiltonians for left/right inputs, analyzes the resulting super-Rabi oscillations under dissipation, and finally uses correlation functions to quantify the directional asymmetry of the photon bundle emission.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum squeezing, a cornerstone of quantum optics and photonics, has played a key role in achieving ultra-precision sensing and realizing nonreciprocal engineering. However, the nonreciprocal multiquanta emission has remained largely unexplored by using directional quantum squeezing. Here, the one-way photon-photon bundle emission in a compound system consisted of two coupled optical resonators and a two-level atom is investigated. It is found that the directional quantum squeezing induces the asymmetric frequency detuning and photon hopping interaction between the two resonators, leading to the directional excitation of the two-photon super-Rabi oscillation. In particular, by harnessing intrinsic dissipation of the system, two types of two-photon bundle emission can be selectively induced for the probe field input from one direction while it is prohibited with the probe from the other direction. This finding bridges the broad fields ranging from nonreciprocal physics to quantum squeezing optics and multiquanta emission control through an all-optical approach, which can enable potential applications in chiral quantum emitters and backscattering-immune photonic communications.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10680"></a>
### [Hawking--Page Universality, Thermodynamic Dipoles and Categorical Defects](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10680v1)

**Authors:** Emilio Torrente-Lujan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10680v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This work unifies the understanding of black hole phase transitions (Hawking–Page and Davies) by mapping them onto a topological dipole structure defined by winding numbers. The authors derive universal constants for these ratios across various dimensions and geometries. They further advance this framework by incorporating categorical defects, suggesting a deeper connection between gravity's thermodynamics and abstract algebraic structures.

**Why it may be interesting.** While primarily in gravity/QFT, the rigorous application of topological invariants (winding numbers) to characterize phase transitions and universal ratios echoes methods used in analyzing critical points in condensed matter systems, particularly those involving symmetry breaking or emergent order parameters.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper reconsiders the Hawking–Page phase transition by analyzing a common thermodynamic vector field whose zeros include both Davies and Hawking–Page critical points.

**Main result.** It establishes that these transitions can be characterized by universal ratios ($C_S, C_T$) derived from topological charges (winding numbers) associated with the dipole structure of the free energy landscape. Furthermore, it extends this analysis to incorporate categorical defects.

**Method.** The authors use a thermodynamic vector field approach to calculate winding numbers and signed first moments at critical points on black hole phase diagrams. They then normalize these moments to derive universal ratios independent of specific scaling parameters.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on the thermodynamics of Anti-de Sitter (AdS) black holes, including Schwarzschild–AdS, Reissner–Nordström–AdS, and Kerr–AdS geometries in arbitrary dimensions. The analysis is performed using the on-shell free energy $F(S; Y_i)$.

**Key observables.** Universal ratios ($C_S, C_T$), topological charges (winding numbers $w_D=-1, w_{HP}=+1$), and the dimensionless barrier constant $B$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dimensionality ($d$), angular velocity ($\Omega$), and defect parameters associated with non-invertible symmetries.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that universal ratios can be extracted by normalizing thermodynamic quantities based on a shared functional form (shape argument). Extensions involve assuming control over both black hole saddles and defect sectors for full analytic treatment.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically illustrate the phase diagrams, showing the locations of the Davies (D) and Hawking–Page (HP) points, and demonstrate how higher moments can distinguish between different configurations sharing the same total charge and first moment.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by defining the common thermodynamic vector field, calculating topological charges for simple AdS black holes to find universal ratios ($C_S, C_T$), generalizing these findings to arbitrary dimensions and rotation (Kerr-AdS), and finally proposing an extension using defect-resolved free energies within a categorical framework.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We reconsider the Hawking--Page transition using the common thermodynamic vector field whose zeros include the Davies and Hawking--Page points. In the elementary AdS branch their winding numbers are $w_{\rm D}=-1$ and $w_{\rm HP}=+1$, so the pair has zero total charge but a non-zero signed first moment. After normalization by the Davies scales this moment gives the familiar universal ratios $C_S$ and $C_T$; in four dimensions $C_S=2$ and $C_T=2/\sqrt{3}-1$. We check the construction for Schwarzschild--AdS, grand-canonical Reissner--Nordström--AdS, charged non-rotating black holes in arbitrary dimension, and Kerr--AdS at fixed angular velocity. The same reduced geometry gives a barrier $B=1/3$ in four dimensions and $B(d)=1/[(d-1)(d-3)]$. Finally we propose a formulation involving a defect-resolved version for categorical or non-invertible symmetry sectors.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.10324"></a>
### [Rank Collapse, Fixed Points, and the Renormalization Group Structure of MLP Residual Networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10324v1)

**Authors:** Parviz Haggi-Mani, Irina Rish  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10324v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This work quantitatively tests the analogy between deep neural networks and Renormalization Group theory using MLP residual stacks trained on Markov chains. By measuring effective rank collapse, the authors show that network compression is selective: it occurs only when input correlations are short-ranged. This provides quantitative evidence for a data-driven, spectral coarse-graining mechanism within deep learning architectures.

**Why it may be interesting.** The explicit mapping between network dynamics (rank collapse) and physical concepts like RG flow and fixed points provides a novel computational lens through which to view complex many-body or statistical physics phenomena, potentially guiding the design of quantum machine learning models.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To move beyond qualitative analogies between deep neural network forward passes and Renormalization Group (RG) theory by conducting an empirical study of a tractable architecture.

**Main result.** The effective rank collapse is selective: it occurs for short-correlation length inputs but is absent for long-correlation length inputs, providing quantitative evidence that the MLP selectively coarse-grains based on input spectral structure.

**Method.** The authors trained a pure MLP residual stack on synthetic Markov chain sequences and measured the effective rank ($ho_{	ext{eff}}$) and inter-layer kernel drift (CKA) as functions of network depth.

**Model / system.** The model is an MLP residual stack trained for masked token prediction. The input data consists of synthetic Markov chains whose spectral properties are controlled by their correlation length ($\xi$).

**Key observables.** Effective Rank ($ho_{	ext{eff}}$), Inter-layer Kernel Drift ($\Delta C_{KA}$)

**Important parameters / regimes.** Correlation Length ($\xi$), Network Depth ($L$), Input Distribution (Short-$\xi$ vs. Long-$\xi$)

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that the observed rank collapse corresponds to the physical RG process of eliminating irrelevant degrees of freedom, and that mean-pooling artifacts must be distinguished from true network compression.

**Figures summary.** Figures show $ho_{	ext{eff}}$ profiles demonstrating monotonic decrease (collapse) for short-$\xi$ chains but stability for long-$\xi$ chains. Kernel drift heatmaps illustrate localized high-drift transitions separated by fixed-point plateaus in both regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the RG analogy, details the MLP/Markov chain setup, presents quantitative measurements of rank collapse and kernel drift across depth, and concludes by interpreting these results as evidence for selective coarse-graining governed by input spectral structure.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The analogy between deep neural network forward passes and renormalization group (RG) flows has been repeatedly noted in the literature, but existing treatments remain qualitative: depth is described as a coarse-graining scale, attention is likened to a partition function, and representations are said to flow toward fixed points. No existing work has defined a measurable RG order parameter, tested it under controlled variation of the input distribution, or made quantitative predictions that are empirically verified. We study the simplest architecture for which the analogy is tractable: a pure MLP residual stack trained on masked token prediction over synthetic Markov chain sequences with known spectral properties. We report three findings. (i) The effective rank of the residual stream decreases monotonically with depth after training, consistent with progressive integration of irrelevant degrees of freedom. (ii) This rank collapse is selective: it occurs for chains with short correlation length approximately 1 but is absent for chains with long correlation length approximately 7, measured at the position level to control for mean-pooling artifacts. The network preserves exactly the degrees of freedom relevant to the prediction task, the content of the RG relevance criterion. (iii) Inter-layer kernel drift is concentrated at one or two specific transitions, with the remainder of the network near a fixed point, consistent with a discrete fixed-point plateau. Together these findings constitute the first quantitative, position-level evidence that MLP residual networks implement a selective coarse-graining procedure governed by the spectral structure of the input distribution.

</details>

### [Bootstrap Cone of the Multicritical Deconfined Quantum Critical Point](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.09985v1)

**Authors:** Zhijin Li, Tinhong Shen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.09985v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

**Summary.** This work uses conformal bootstrap techniques to analyze the controversial Deconfined Quantum Critical Point (DQCP) in 2D quantum magnets. By showing that the theoretical constraints (bootstrap cone) perfectly match results from both QMC simulations and fuzzy sphere calculations, the authors strongly argue for a unitary multicritical fixed point governed by SO(5) symmetry. This provides a major theoretical breakthrough in understanding unconventional quantum phase transitions.

**Why it may be interesting.** Although focused on condensed matter magnetism, the rigorous application of CFT and bootstrap methods provides powerful general tools for analyzing critical points in strongly interacting quantum systems, which has implications for understanding emergent symmetries in other many-body contexts.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central problem is determining the true nature of the Deconfined Quantum Critical Point (DQCP) phase transition, specifically resolving the debate between pseudo-criticality and a unitary multicritical fixed point.

**Main result.** The authors provide strong evidence supporting the multicriticality scenario by showing that conformal bootstrap bounds consistently unify results from Quantum Monte Carlo simulations and fuzzy sphere calculations into a single unitary CFT description.

**Method.** The primary method is applying the Conformal Bootstrap program to constrain the parameters of the critical theory, supplemented by comparison with large-scale QMC data and fuzzy sphere regularization results.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on two-dimensional quantum magnets exhibiting transitions between Néel AFM, VBS, and QSL phases. The underlying theoretical framework involves an SO(5) symmetry structure relevant to high-Tc superconductivity.

**Key observables.** Critical exponents, scaling dimensions ($\Delta$), Operator Product Expansion (OPE) coefficients, and the shape of the bootstrap cone in parameter space.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The existence and location of a unitary fixed point characterized by a relevant SO(5) singlet scalar.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the critical theory is governed by a unitary Conformal Field Theory (CFT), allowing for the use of bootstrap constraints to overcome limitations in direct numerical simulations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate how imposing sparseness conditions causes the general bootstrap bounds to collapse from a strip into a sharp cone, which aligns remarkably well with both QMC data and fuzzy sphere calculations.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the theoretical conflict (pseudo-criticality vs. multicriticality), then applying the conformal bootstrap method using advanced techniques like the navigator algorithm to map out the allowed parameter space, culminating in the demonstration of consistency across multiple independent computational approaches.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The deconfined quantum critical point (DQCP) provides a prominent example of the unconventional phase transitions beyond the Landau-Ginzburg-Wilson paradigm and its nature has been controversial for decades. The DQCP has been extensively studied and the results lead to two opposite scenarios with pseudo-criticality or multicriticality. The pseudo-criticality is a prevailing scenario of DQCP which interprets the approximately scale invariant numerical results with the walking behavior near complex fixed points. In contrast, the multicriticality scenario conjectures the DQCP is a unitary fixed point with a relevant $SO(5)$ singlet scalar. In this work we provide substantial evidence for the multicriticality scenario using conformal bootstrap. We start with the observation that the large scale Quantum Monte Carlo (QMC) results nearly saturate the bootstrap bounds. After imposing suitable sparseness condition the bootstrap bound forms a sharp cone in the three-dimensional parameter space. The bootstrap cone is close to the QMC data. We use the navigator algorithm to locate the apex of the cone and extract the extremal solutions. We find striking consistencies between the bootstrap solutions and the fuzzy sphere data of DQCP, including the coefficients in the operator product expansions (OPEs) and the higher spectrum! The bootstrap cone unifies the QMC and the fuzzy sphere data into a unitary conformal field theory with a relevant $SO(5)$ singlet scalar, thus strongly supporting the multicriticality scenario of DQCP. The agreement between the conformal bootstrap, QMC and fuzzy sphere results is a surprise towards solving DQCP and decoding the profound phase diagram of the two-dimensional quantum magnets.

</details>
