**Summary.** This review surveys the field of ${}^{229}	ext{Th}$ nuclear clocks, detailing how its unique low-energy isomeric transition makes it a leading candidate for next-generation frequency standards. It compares trapped ion and solid-state implementations, emphasizing that while both offer high precision, controlling environmental decay pathways (quenching) is key to realizing the clock's full potential.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it bridges nuclear physics with quantum sensing platforms. The study of decay pathways (quenching) in solids involves understanding open quantum systems dynamics, while the comparison between ion traps and crystals touches upon advanced AMO techniques like cavity QED or defect engineering.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is realizing a nuclear optical clock using the low-energy isomeric transition of ${}^{229}	ext{Th}$ to create an ultra-high precision quantum sensor.

**Main result.** The paper reviews decades of research, confirming that while significant experimental challenges exist (e.g., distinguishing true decay from background noise), the potential for a high-precision clock in both trapped ions and solid-state matrices is robust.

**Method.** The review synthesizes results from various techniques including VUV-comb spectroscopy, laser excitation in crystals, $\gamma$-ray spectroscopy, and ion trapping experiments.

**Model / system.** The system centers on the ${}^{229}	ext{Th}$ nuclear isomer ($\sim 8.4 	ext{ eV}$ transition), which is studied in two main platforms: isolated trapped ions (e.g., $	ext{Th}^{3+}$) and solid-state matrices (e.g., fluorides like $	ext{CaF}_2$). The underlying physics involves complex nuclear structure models like the Nilsson model.

**Key observables.** Transition energy ($\sim 8.4 	ext{ eV}$), transition multipolarity ($	ext{M}1$), isomer shift, and sensitivity to fundamental constant variations ($K_{\alpha}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Clock stability targets reaching $10^{-19}$ fractional accuracy; the near-cancellation of strong and electromagnetic contributions leading to high sensitivity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The performance depends critically on minimizing environmental quenching pathways (e.g., defect-mediated decay) in solid-state hosts, while trapped ions offer superior control over systematic errors.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically illustrate the Nilsson quantum numbers for the ground and isomeric states of ${}^{229}	ext{Th}$ within a deformed nucleus.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a historical development structure: first reviewing early $\gamma$-ray spectroscopy attempts, then detailing the shift to optical excitation in solid-state hosts, and finally comparing the systematic advantages and challenges of trapped ion versus solid-state implementations for modern clock operation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Th-229 nuclear isomeric state has the lowest energy of all known nuclear excited states, placing it within the reach of current table-top laser technology. This extraordinary property has made this nuclear isomer an attractive candidate for a nuclear optical clock of incredibly high precision and accuracy, both as isolated trapped Th-229 ions and embedded into solid-state platforms. Activity around Th-229 has surged in recent years, driven by breakthroughs in its direct laser excitation. The underlying nuclear physics that gives rise to this unique isomer will be elucidated, as well as the nearly half-century of efforts that led to its direct excitation. The design and systematics of a Th-229 nuclear clock will be discussed, both in ion traps and in the solid-state. These systematics, such as frequency shifts and quenching channels, can be leveraged both to probe the local chemical environment, and as a control knob during clock operation. Finally, the nuclear clock's high sensitivity to the variations of fundamental constants will be discussed.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper theoretically analyzes inherent flux crosstalk in superconducting qubit circuits by deriving the system dynamics using Lagrangian mechanics. It shows this unintended coupling, arising from time-varying external fluxes, can be harnessed to drive fast single-qubit gates via the coupler element. This approach promises high fidelity quantum control while simplifying hardware requirements.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete theoretical framework for engineering quantum control pulses. For AMO/open systems theorists, the derivation of an unavoidable coupling term (inherent crosstalk) that can be actively exploited is valuable, as it moves beyond treating crosstalk purely as noise to treat it as a controllable resource.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The work investigates inherent flux crosstalk—an unavoidable coupling mechanism arising from time-varying external magnetic fluxes in superconducting qubits—and demonstrates how to harness it for fast, high-fidelity single-qubit gates.

**Main result.** By utilizing the inherent flux crosstalk generated by modulating the coupler element, researchers can achieve fast single-qubit gate fidelities exceeding 99.9%, potentially eliminating the need for dedicated microwave control lines per qubit.

**Method.** The analysis employs Lagrangian mechanics derived from lumped-element circuit models to derive the system Hamiltonian, followed by normal mode transformations and time-dependent perturbation theory for gate simulation.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is a superconducting transmon qubit architecture featuring tunable couplers. The model uses generalized coordinates ($\Phi$) and derives the dynamics via a Lagrangian/Hamiltonian formalism that explicitly includes terms arising from $\dot{\Phi}_{	ext{ext}}(t)$.

**Key observables.** Average Gate Fidelity (AGF), Total Leakage Rate, Pauli error probabilities ($p_a$), and the effective drive strength ($\Omega_p$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** External flux modulation amplitude ($A$), static bias fluxes ($\phi_{st}$), and gate time ($t_g \sim 20 	ext{ ns}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the validity of the Rotating Wave Approximation (RWA) and truncating the Hamiltonian expansion to include only first and second harmonic terms.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the circuit model, the derivation of the Lagrangian/Hamiltonian, and crucially, show AGF vs. gate time curves demonstrating high fidelity achievable when inherent crosstalk is included in the drive calculation.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from deriving the fundamental theory using Lagrangian mechanics to identify the inherent flux crosstalk term; it then applies this understanding by modeling the system's Hamiltonian in normal modes and finally simulates single-qubit gates, demonstrating performance metrics like AGF.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Crosstalk refers to unwanted qubit addressing. This is particularly detrimental when scaling up quantum information systems because unintended interactions limit their overall performance. For superconducting qubits, tunable couplings and frequency tunability achieved through externally applied magnetic fluxes enable high-fidelity entangling gates; however, they also introduce crosstalk through unintended flux coupling. In this work, we investigate the impact of time-dependent external magnetic fluxes in quantized circuits on superconducting qubit couplings. We find that non-trivial cross-voltage driving emerges between capacitively linked qubits when the magnetic flux threading the SQUID loop of a qubit varies in time, in a manner analogous to Faraday's law of induction. Crucially, we show that this effect enables fast single qubit control through the coupler element in standard tunable-coupler architectures, potentially eliminating the need for individual microwave $XY$ control lines.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11056"></a>
### [On pseudogap phase as precursor to a superconducting dome in high-Tc cuprates: Non-analytic T* as a function of doping](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11056v1)

**Authors:** Felix A. Buot  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11056v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper develops a generalized theory showing that the pseudogap phase can precede superconductivity if two doping-dependent conditions on pairing strength and ordering rates are met. It applies this framework to high-Tc cuprates using an entanglement and confinement pairing model, successfully explaining key features like the SC dome structure and strange metal behavior through changes in configurational entropy.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on cuprates, the framework of analyzing phase transitions via entropy changes and rate parameters ($R$) provides a general theoretical tool applicable to understanding complex many-body dynamics in strongly correlated systems beyond standard BCS theory.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To generalize the theoretical condition under which a quantum material exhibiting a pseudogap phase acts as a precursor to a superconducting (SC) dome.

**Main result.** The SC dome formation requires two conditions: decreasing $T^*$ with doping and an increasing configurational-ordering rate ($R$) with doping, explained by a novel entanglement and confinement pairing mechanism.

**Method.** Theoretical derivation using macroscopic order parameters like Configuration Entropy (CE) to characterize the transition from local pair ordering ($	ext{PO}$) to global superconducting order ($	ext{CO}$).

**Model / system.** The study focuses on high-Tc cuprates, modeling them with a novel strong entanglement and confinement hole pairing (ECHP) mechanism that governs the phase diagram across doping levels.

**Key observables.** $T^*$ (pseudogap temperature), $T_C$ (superconducting transition temperature), Configuration Entropy ($	ext{CE}$), and the configurational-ordering rate parameter ($R$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Doping level ($d$), Temperature ($T$), Pseudogap/Superconducting gap magnitudes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes a linear temperature dependence for the Configuration Entropy (CE) and relies on establishing two specific doping dependencies for $T^*$ and $R$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the generic phase diagram showing the SC dome, the relationship between $	ext{CE}$ and $T$ revealing singularities where $R 	o \infty$, and the coexistence of strange metal behavior above $T_C$.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the general criteria for pseudogap precursors to superconductivity, applies these criteria specifically to high-Tc cuprates using the ECHP mechanism, derives the non-analytic relationship between $T^*$ and $T_C$, and discusses implications for strange metal behavior.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We generalize the condition under which a quantum material exhibiting a pseudogap phase is a precursor to a superconducting (SC) dome. The result reveals the non-analytic T* as a function of doping. A well-known example is the high-Tc cuprates. Essentially, the SC dome is generated under two conditions: (1) that the pseudogap T* is a decreasing function of doping, due to the decrease in size of extended pairing of doped holes with doping, and most importantly, (2) that the rate of configurational-ordering parameter is an increasing function of doping as a result of the decrease in extended length of the disordered pairs. These two conditions are provided by the new entanglement and confinement pairing mechanism of high-Tc cuprates. This is a theory that has recently been discussed in the literature by Buot et al. It hinges on a novel strong entanglement and confinement hole pairing (ECHP) mechanism that unravels the microscopic features of the entire phase diagram of both electron and hole-doped high-Tc cuprates.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10730"></a>
### [Unitary Channel Testing Under a Depolarizing Noise Assumption](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10730v1)

**Authors:** Hirak Ghosh, Andrew Jackson, Animesh Datta  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10730v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10730_figures/2606.10730_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. (a) Ancilla-assisted, adaptive tester with arbitrary (finite) sized POVMs. Green arrows denote classical feed-forward: state preparations and measurements may depend on some or all prior measurement outcomes. (b) Non-adaptive, ancilla-free tester with fixed inputs and a two-outcome measurement. (c) m-sequential non-adaptive tester with fixed inputs and a two-outcome POVM.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper provides a comprehensive theoretical analysis of quantum channel testing under depolarizing noise. It establishes optimal query complexities for distinguishing noisy channels from ideal unitaries using both standard and tolerant hypothesis tests. The work is significant because it sets fundamental resource limits on characterizing the fidelity of quantum operations.

**Why it may be interesting.** The results provide rigorous complexity benchmarks for characterizing quantum hardware fidelity, which is crucial for understanding the practical limits of fault-tolerant computation protocols in terms of required experimental resources.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper develops fast algorithms and establishes lower bounds for testing the strength of a quantum channel implemented by a black box, specifically under the assumption of depolarizing noise.

**Main result.** Optimal query complexities are derived for both standard ($\Theta(1/\epsilon)$) and tolerant ($\Theta\left(rac{\epsilon_2}{(\epsilon_2 - \epsilon_1)^2}ight)$) testing scenarios, showing that these bounds hold even when considering adaptive or ancilla-assisted protocols.

**Method.** The analysis combines quantum hypothesis testing theory with information-theoretic tools like the diamond distance and Kullback-Leibler divergence to establish matching upper and lower bounds for query complexity.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on general quantum channels $N: L(H) 	o L(H)$ acting on a system of dimension $d$, specifically analyzing deviations from the identity channel due to an assumed depolarizing noise $\mathcal{N}_\lambda$.

**Key observables.** Diamond distance ($d_\diamond$), Trace distance ($d_{	ext{Tr}}$), and Query Complexity (number of channel uses).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Error parameters ($\epsilon, \epsilon_1, \epsilon_2$) and the noise parameter $\lambda$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes perfect state preparation and measurement, and it focuses on the depolarizing noise model common in fault-tolerant quantum computation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate various access models for testing (e.g., ancilla-assisted adaptive vs. non-adaptive ancilla-free) and summarize the derived optimal query complexities in tables.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes exact relationships between distinguishability measures ($d_{	ext{Tr}}$ and $d_\diamond$) for depolarizing noise, then develops upper bounds using specific algorithms (non-adaptive/ancilla-free), and finally proves matching lower bounds using information theory arguments like KL divergence.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present fast algorithms $\unicode{x2013}$ under the depolarizing noise assumption, often made in fault-tolerant quantum computations $\unicode{x2013}$ to test its strength. Our optimal algorithms answer the following question: is the quantum channel implemented by a given black box identical to a target unitary or $\varepsilon$-far from it in the diamond distance, assuming that the deviation is a depolarizing channel with unknown parameter? Our algorithm has a query complexity of $Θ(1/\varepsilon).$ The query complexity of the relaxed problem of testing whether the black-box channel is $\varepsilon_1$-close to a target unitary or $\varepsilon_2$-far in the diamond distance is $Θ\bigl(\varepsilon_2/(\varepsilon_2 - \varepsilon_1)^2\bigr).$ In both cases, we provide matching lower bounds that hold even for adaptive, ancilla-assisted protocols with multi-outcome incoherent measurements.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes a theoretical optimal control protocol to generate complex quantum non-Gaussian states (like Fock or cat states) in levitated particles. This is achieved by transiently delocalizing the particle's wave function and applying time-dependent modulation to the linear potential term, leveraging weak cubic anharmonicity. The method demonstrates universal control applicable to various mechanical degrees of freedom.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the creation of non-Gaussian quantum resources in a macroscopic, mechanical system, which is highly relevant for quantum sensing and computation beyond standard harmonic approximations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is the deterministic generation and manipulation of quantum non-Gaussian states, such as Fock or Schrödinger cat states, in levitated massive objects.

**Main result.** A theoretical protocol was developed using time-dependent control of the linear potential component while maintaining a static cubic nonharmonicity to achieve universal mode control and state preparation.

**Method.** The approach utilizes optimal quantum control theory applied to the system's Hamiltonian, involving transient wave-function delocalization followed by coherent compression.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is levitated high-mass quantum systems whose motion is modeled by a weakly nonharmonic potential containing harmonic and cubic terms. The dynamics are analyzed using both Schrödinger equation evolution and density matrix master equations incorporating dissipation.

**Key observables.** State-transfer fidelity ($F$), geometric-mean infidelity ($ar{F}$), Wigner functions, and quadrature measurements.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nonharmonicity parameter ($\eta_
u$), motional delocalization ($\Delta X/X_
u$), and maximum tolerable decoherence rates.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on truncating the potential expansion at the cubic term; high-number states challenge this assumption due to increased spatial extent.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the multi-stage protocol (cooling, delocalization via control, compression) and show Wigner functions for target states like $|1angle$ and cat states, alongside infidelity scaling plots versus control time or nonharmonicity strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper outlines a five-step optimal control protocol: ground state preparation $ightarrow$ coherent transfer to low potential $ightarrow$ delocalized state preparation via linear modulation $ightarrow$ coherent compression $ightarrow$ measurement. Extensions cover two-particle Bell state generation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Levitated high-mass quantum systems provide access to unprecedented regimes in both fundamental science and technological applications. However, deterministic generation and manipulation of quantum non-Gaussian states, which are central to many continuous-variable quantum advantages, remain elusive in such platforms. In this work, we propose a theoretical protocol for preparing a continuous-variable degree of freedom of a levitated massive object in a variety of quantum states, including Fock and Schrödinger cat states, without coupling to auxiliary two-level systems. Our approach enhances otherwise weak nonharmonic effects by transient wave-function delocalization and combines this with optimal control of the potential. Specifically, time-dependent modulation of the linear component of the potential, in the presence of a static cubic nonharmonicity, provides a route to universal control of the mode. We analyze quantum state preparation under such control and estimate the required nonharmonicity, motional delocalization, and maximum tolerable decoherence for generating target non-Gaussian states. The proposed optimal-control scheme can also be readily extended beyond single-particle state preparation, for example, to unitary transformations and nonlinear measurements. As a concrete example, we demonstrate mechanical Bell-state preparation for two interacting particles using only local modulation of weakly nonharmonic potentials, while the interparticle interaction remains effectively linear. We emphasize that the protocols presented here apply to different mechanical degrees of freedom, such as center-of-mass motion and libration, and can also be implemented in other weakly nonharmonic systems with a leading cubic nonharmonicity.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10222"></a>
### [Multifractal Signatures of Ageing and Dementia Development: A Multifractal Space-Filling Curve Analysis](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10222v1)

**Authors:** Marta Lotka, Jacek Grela, Zbigniew Drogosz, Jeremi K. Ochab, Paweł Oświęcimka  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10222v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper introduces Multifractal Space-filling Curve Analysis (MFSCA) to analyze brain MRI scans across different stages of aging and dementia. By quantifying the degree of multifractality, the authors demonstrate that this complex spatial organization systematically degrades towards a simpler, monofractal structure as cognitive decline worsens. This suggests that multifractality serves as a quantitative biomarker for neurodegenerative processes.

**Why it may be interesting.** While the application is biological, the core methodology—quantifying complex non-linear correlations in high-dimensional data using fractal geometry—is mathematically rigorous and shares conceptual overlap with characterizing complexity in disordered quantum systems or neural network dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to quantify the nonlinear, scale-free properties of complex biological data, specifically analyzing how spatial organization in brain structures degrades during aging and dementia.

**Main result.** The analysis reveals a progressive weakening of multifractality—a transition from rich multifractal structure towards a homogeneous, monofractal state—as age increases and dementia progresses.

**Method.** A novel Multifractal Space-filling Curve Analysis (MFSCA) is employed to project high-dimensional MRI data onto 1D time series, which are then analyzed using multifractal algorithms like MFDFA.

**Model / system.** The system under study is human brain structure derived from T1-weighted Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI) scans of subjects across various cognitive cohorts (Young Control through MCI).

**Key observables.** Multifractal profiles, generalized Hurst exponent h(q), multifractal spectrum width ($\Delta\alpha$), and asymmetry ($A$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Age groups (e.g., Young vs. Elderly) and cognitive impairment stages (e.g., EMCI vs. MCI).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the loss of multifractality directly correlates with structural deterioration in brain organization, and uses techniques like IPTW to mitigate bias in observational comparisons.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate MFSCA flowcharts, group comparisons showing decreasing $\Delta\alpha$ and $H$ from young controls to dementia patients, and dimensionality reduction plots (t-SNE/LDA) separating the cognitive cohorts.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces MFSCA for multidimensional data analysis, validates it using artificial cascades, applies it to OASIS-1 MRI data across age/dementia groups, quantifies metrics ($\Delta\alpha$, $A$), and uses LDA/t-SNE to show systematic progression along a continuum of disorder.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Multifractality is an effective formalism for quantifying the nonlinear, scale-free properties of complex data. In this study, we propose a novel and efficient methodology, termed Multifractal Space-filling Curve Analysis (MFSCA), for quantifying the correlation structure of multidimensional data. Within this framework, the original multidimensional data - while preserving both local and long-range organisational properties - are projected onto a one-dimensional representation using a fractal space-filling curve. The resulting one-dimensional signal is then analysed using multifractal algorithms. We demonstrate the utility of the method using both artificially generated multifractal structures and real data. In particular, we apply MFSCA to analyse magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) data from Alzheimer patients at different stages of dementia. Based on the results, we estimate the multifractal profiles of the brain for healthy subjects of different ages as well as for dementia patients. The analysis reveals that the spatial organization of brain structures, as measured by the degree of multifractality, progressively weakens with age and the development of dementia. A transition from multifractality to monofractality is observed both in control groups, when comparing the Young Control and Elderly Control groups, and among dementia subjects of similar age but at different stages of the disease, namely early dementia and mild cognitive impairment. Thus, from the perspective of multiscaling properties, the heterogeneous characteristics of spatial brain organization deteriorate under worsening conditions, leading to a homogeneous and weakly correlated structure. These findings not only effectively capture key aspects of brain organisation, but also demonstrate that the multifractality of MRI data can serve as a marker of structural brain changes.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10647"></a>
### [Hawking-Page phase transition for pure Lovelock black holes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10647v1)

**Authors:** Nitesh K. Dubey, Sanved Kolekar  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10647v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10647_figures/2606.10647_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10647_figures/2606.10647_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: The above plots depict the dependence of the normalized Ruppeiner scalar curvature on the electric potential Φ and pressure P. In panels F1, F2, F3, F4 the pressure is kept constant. **Summary.** This work analyzes the Hawking-Page phase transition for black holes in higher-curvature pure Lovelock gravity within AdS spacetime. It uses advanced thermodynamic geometry, specifically the Ruppeiner scalar curvature, to probe universality across different dimensions and charges. The findings reveal that while simple dualities hold in General Relativity, these universal relationships are modified or broken by higher-order curvature terms.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on gravity, the use of Ruppeiner geometry and phase transition analysis directly relates to statistical mechanics concepts of critical points and thermodynamic stability in complex systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the thermodynamic properties and phase structure of Anti-de Sitter (AdS) black holes governed by pure Lovelock gravity, focusing on the Hawking-Page (HP) phase transition.

**Main result.** The normalized Ruppeiner scalar curvature at the HP transition is shown to be a universal constant depending only on spacetime dimension for neutral cases. The simple dual relationship between minimum temperature and $T_{HP}$ breaks down in higher pure Lovelock theories.

**Method.** Thermodynamic analysis, utilizing the grand canonical ensemble, calculating Gibbs free energy ($G$), and employing geometric tools like the Ruppeiner scalar curvature to diagnose phase transitions.

**Model / system.** Static, spherically symmetric AdS black holes described by pure Lovelock gravity in $d$ dimensions. The analysis extends to include electric charge ($\Phi$) and varying the Lovelock order ($n$).

**Key observables.** Hawking temperature minimum ($T_0$), Hawking-Page transition temperature ($T_{HP}$), Gibbs free energy ($G$), and normalized Ruppeiner scalar curvature ($R_N$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spacetime dimension ($d$), Lovelock order ($n$), electrostatic potential ($\Phi$), and thermodynamic pressure ($P$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the validity of the grand canonical ensemble description and relies on interpreting $G=0$ for the HP transition. Approximations are used in analyzing joint limits of $\Phi$ and $P$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate plots showing the dependence of the normalized Ruppeiner scalar curvature ($R_N$) on electrostatic potential ($\Phi$) and pressure ($P$), demonstrating plateaus and sign changes at the HP transition.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the phase structure by comparing $T_0$ (local minimum) with $T_{HP}$ (global free energy crossing). It then uses geometric diagnostics like $R_N$ to characterize the universality of this transition across different Lovelock orders and charge regimes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the thermodynamic properties of static, spherically symmetric Anti-de Sitter (AdS) black holes, focusing on the interplay between characteristic temperatures, as well as on the universality of Ruppeiner scalar curvature at the Hawking-Page (HP) phase transition. In particular, we study the relation between the minimum temperature and the HP phase transition temperature for static, spherically symmetric AdS black holes in pure Lovelock gravity. For the electromagnetically neutral case in Einstein gravity, the minimum temperature in $(d+1)$ dimensions coincides with the HP transition temperature in $d$ dimensions, while in higher pure Lovelock theories this relation is modified by a dimension- and order-dependent factor, reducing to the Einstein result in appropriate limits. For charged AdS black holes, in the grand canonical ensemble, in general relativity, the two temperatures differ by a simple dimension-dependent factor, whereas no universal relation persists in higher curvature pure Lovelock theories. We further analyze the normalized Ruppeiner scalar curvature at the HP transition and show that it is a universal constant depending only on the spacetime dimension for electromagnetically neutral black holes in pure Lovelock theories. The normalized scalar curvature remains a constant, under appropriate conditions, even for the charged static spherically symmetric black holes in the grand canonical ensemble for the Einstein theory case, whereas in general pure Lovelock theories it depends on thermodynamic parameters such as pressure and electrostatic potential, asymptotically approaching a constant in the large-pressure or simultaneous large-potential and large-pressure limits.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.10826"></a>
### [The fidelity of controlled quantum teleportation in a noisy environment](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10826v1)

**Authors:** Wen-Jing Wei, Feng-Li Yan, Ting Gao  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10826v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

**Summary.** This work analyzes how various types of noise affect the success rate (fidelity) of controlled quantum teleportation. By deriving complex analytical expressions, the authors show that fidelity does not always decrease monotonically with increasing noise; instead, it can exhibit non-monotonic behavior. This highlights that the impact of decoherence is highly dependent on both the initial entanglement and which qubit interacts with the noisy channel.

**Why it may be interesting.** The finding that fidelity can increase after an initial drop due to noise is crucial for understanding practical quantum communication channels, suggesting potential recovery or resilience mechanisms beyond simple decay models.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how noise degrades the fidelity of controlled quantum teleportation protocols.

**Main result.** The optimal average fidelity can exhibit non-monotonic behavior, first decreasing and then increasing with the evolution parameter, depending on initial entanglement and channel parameters.

**Method.** The authors derive analytical expressions for the maximal average fidelity ($F_{	ext{max}}$) by modeling noise using generalized noisy channels parameterized by Kraus operators.

**Model / system.** The system involves controlled quantum teleportation utilizing a tripartite entangled resource state $|\Psiangle_{ABC}$. Noise is modeled as local, independent quantum channels ($\Lambda_{ABC} = \Lambda_A \otimes \Lambda_B \otimes \Lambda_C$) acting on the three qubits.

**Key observables.** Maximal average fidelity ($F_{	ext{max}}$) and optimal average fidelity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Channel parameters ($q, k, r$), evolution angles ($\zeta_A, \zeta_B, \zeta_C$), and initial state entanglement parameters ($a_0, a_1$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The noise is assumed to be local and independent across the three qubits. The analysis often simplifies by considering only one or two qubits passing through noise.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate $F_{	ext{max}}$ as functions of channel parameters ($\zeta_A, q$) for different initial state conditions ($a_1$ vs $a_0$), visually confirming the non-monotonic dependence on noise strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first defining the noisy resource state and calculating $F_{	ext{max}}$ generally. It then analyzes specific cases (e.g., only one qubit noisy, all three qubits noisy) to derive simplified analytical expressions and demonstrate the non-monotonic behavior based on parameter regimes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this work, we investigate controlled quantum teleportation in the presence of noisy channels acting on the three-qubit resource state. We employ a series of generalized noisy channels that bridge the dephasing channels and amplitude damping channels while encompassing extensive intermediate scenarios. We provide an in-depth analysis of the degradation of the maximal average fidelity and the optimal average fidelity in controlled quantum teleportation induced by such noisy channels by deriving the analytical expression and examining several special cases. The analytical expression shows that attaining the optimal average fidelity requires Charlie's cooperation in performing a measurement at suitably chosen angles, and is also related to the initial state and the channel parameters. Our analysis reveals that the optimal average fidelity does not always decrease monotonically with the evolution parameter, instead, it first decreases and then increases. This non-monotonic behavior depends on the entanglement of the initial resource state, as well as on the parameters of the channel traversed by the first qubit.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10055"></a>
### [Identical Bosons, large occupation numbers and classical field description](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10055v1)

**Authors:** Gaurav Goswami  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10055v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10055_figures/2606.10055_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1: When the coefficients cn are uniformly distributed within (−1, 1), there is no correlation between the most quantities of our interest (except between σφ and ⟨N⟩in (c), with Pearson coefficient = 0.31 i.e. a weak correlation). In (d), the dashed line corresponds to σφ/|⟨φ⟩| for cns for a coherent state.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10055_figures/2606.10055_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2: When the coefficients cn are uniformly distributed within (0, 2), we begin to see weak correlations between the various quantities of our interest: the values of Pearson coefficient for the sub-figures being 0.55, -0.57, 0.20, -0.20 respectively. In (d), in addition to the dashed line for the case of coherent state, the solid line and dotted line correspond to σφ/|⟨φ⟩| = 1 and 1/2.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10055_figures/2606.10055_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3: Small and large deviations from coherent states: (a) |α|2 = 25, f = 0.1, (b), |α|2 = 25, f = 0.5, (c) |α|2 = 80, f = 2.0. As we increase the deviation from coherent state by increasing f, the ratio σφ/|⟨φ⟩| becomes larger and eventually, classical behaviour is lost.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work rigorously tests the assumption that large occupation numbers automatically imply classical field behavior for Bosons. The authors demonstrate that this is false; instead, the validity of the classical description hinges on how closely the quantum state resembles a coherent state. This has direct implications for modeling phenomena like ultra-light dark matter.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous theoretical boundary condition for the semi-classical limit in many-body physics, which is crucial for interpreting cosmological simulations involving quantum fields (like wave DM).

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the conditions under which a quantum system of identical Bosons, characterized by a large mean occupation number, can be accurately described using classical field theory.

**Main result.** Classical behavior is not guaranteed merely by having a large occupation number; instead, it requires the quantum state to remain close to that of a coherent state.

**Method.** The analysis uses theoretical diagnostics based on comparing the standard deviation ($\sigma_\varphi$) to the expectation value ($|\langle\varphiangle|$) using the criterion $2\sigma_\varphi < |\langle\varphiangle|$.

**Model / system.** The system models non-relativistic, identical Bosons (relevant for ultra-light dark matter or Bose-Einstein Condensates) described by a quantum field operator $\hat{\varphi}(\mathbf{r})$. The state space is analyzed via its expansion in Fock states.

**Key observables.** Standard deviation ($\sigma_\varphi$), mean occupation number ($\langle Nangle$), and the ratio $\sigma_\varphi / |\langle\varphiangle|$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The degree of deviation ($f$) from a coherent state, which governs the proximity to classicality.

**Assumptions / limitations.** It is assumed that the Hamiltonian commutes with the total number operator ($\hat{H}$ commutes with $\hat{N}_{	ext{tot}}$) for simplicity in analyzing the state space structure.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate how increasing the deviation of quantum coefficients from those defining a coherent state increases the ratio $\sigma_\varphi / |\langle\varphiangle|$, causing a breakdown of classical behavior.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the general problem and criterion for classicality. It then analyzes various types of quantum states (random, positive-coefficient, near-coherent) to test if large occupation number is sufficient. Finally, it discusses implications for physical systems like ultra-light dark matter.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

For a system with a large number of identical Bosons, it is common to claim, often without any additional justifications, that, when the mean occupation number in a single particle state is sufficiently large, classical field description will be applicable. This is why e.g. for ultra-light dark matter, the classical field equations are used to compute its dynamics. In this work, we test the validity and robustness of this assumption based on the criterion $2 σ_\varphi < |\langle \varphi \rangle| $ for classical field behaviour and applying it to aribtrary quantum states. We find that an arbitrary state with large occupation number doesn't behave classically while imposing some restrictions on the state vectors can improve the classical behavior. Since coherent states are known to have quasi-classical behaviour, we also ask how much deviation from coherent state can spoil the classical behaviour. Based on this analysis, we find that it is the proximity of the state to a large occupation coherent state rather than large occupation number itself which ensures validity of classical description. Implications of this for ultra light dark matter are discussed.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.11171"></a>
### [Algorithmic and Minimax Complexities in Kernel Bandits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11171v1)

**Authors:** Yunbei Xu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11171v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.11171_figures/2606.11171_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 4</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.11171_figures/2606.11171_page5.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 5</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.11171_figures/2606.11171_page6.jpg" alt="Figure 5">

</div>

**Summary.** This paper develops a unified framework to compare performance guarantees for sequential learning algorithms like GP-UCB and MAMS in kernel bandit problems. It mathematically proves that the information gained by an algorithm based on its specific prior (algorithmic view) can differ significantly from the worst-case guarantee derived over the entire model class (minimax view). This distinction is crucial for selecting appropriate theoretical bounds.

**Why it may be interesting.** While not directly about quantum systems, the rigorous comparison of different complexity measures (algorithmic vs. worst-case) mirrors theoretical challenges in characterizing resource limitations in complex physical models where multiple optimization criteria might apply simultaneously.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to unify and compare three distinct theoretical views of sequential learning performance—algorithmic, information-theoretic/minimax, and class-wide worst-case regret—within the context of kernel bandits.

**Main result.** It demonstrates that algorithmic information gain (from GP-UCB) and class-wide minimax coefficients answer different questions, providing a clean setting in kernel bandits where this distinction is mathematically visible.

**Method.** The analysis uses unified MAIR frameworks, deriving identities linking Bayesian posterior updates to realized algorithmic information gain, and comparing these against saddle-point optimization criteria for minimax bounds.

**Model / system.** The model is a frequentist linear/kernel truth $f^\star(x)$ in an RKHS, observed with Gaussian noise. The setting is generalized using heterogeneous positive-semidefinite (PSD) algorithmic priors ($\Gamma$) and involves comparing performance across structured truths versus worst-case class bounds.

**Key observables.** Regret ($R_T$), Algorithmic Information Gain ($I_{T,\Gamma}$), Minimax Regret-Information Coefficient, and the Model-index Algorithmic Information Ratio (MAIR).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The complexity is governed by the structure of the kernel class ($\Gamma$) and the separation gap between hub and cloud components in the model space.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes conditional R-sub-Gaussian noise for the observations. The analysis relies on establishing specific identities (like the Posterior-reference density-ratio telescope) that link different forms of information accumulation.

**Figures summary.** Not specified, but the notes indicate comparisons between regret bounds derived from UCB, MAMS, and minimax lower bounds in various constructions (e.g., hub-cloud).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing foundational identities linking Bayesian updates to information gain (Theorem 2.1), then unifying this structure via the MAIR objective (Lemma 3.2), applying these concepts to show GP-UCB satisfies an MAIR condition, and finally comparing against finite class bounds using MAMS.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Gaussian-process upper confidence bound (GP-UCB) and decision-estimation-coefficient (DEC) methods may appear, at first sight, to belong to different theories. This paper places the two viewpoints in a common algorithmic-information language for frequentist RKHS bandits. GP-UCB fixes an algorithmic, rather than true, Gaussian-process prior and exploits realized-trajectory complexity together with computational tractability, whereas MAMS optimizes a robust class-wide MAIR/DEC envelope. Through the unified MAIR framework and heterogeneous positive-semidefinite algorithmic priors, we generalize both the GP-UCB analysis and the MAMS algorithm, propose a safeguarded master that combines their advantages, and provide a kernel-bandit construction showing that algorithmic complexity can be more informative than class-wide minimax or DEC certificates in overparameterized models. The resulting message is that algorithmic information and class-wide minimax coefficients answer different questions and can lead to different gaps; kernel bandits provide a clean setting in which this distinction becomes mathematically visible.

</details>

### [Analytical performance evaluation of quantum radar architectures: From single-photon to entangled-noise radars](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10436v1)

**Authors:** Hossein Allahverdi, Ali Motazedifard  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10436v1>

**Summary.** This paper provides a detailed theoretical evaluation of quantum radar systems, comparing single-photon direct detection against noise radars utilizing entangled microwave fields. By deriving analytical range equations involving the Lambert W function, the authors quantify the advantage of quantum entanglement through an enhancement factor (REF). The results suggest that these advanced quantum receivers can achieve practical detection ranges using current microwave detector technology.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges quantum optics (squeezed states, entanglement) with applied physics (radar detection), providing quantitative performance benchmarks for next-generation sensing technologies.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to comprehensively analyze and derive analytical expressions for the maximum detection range of various quantum radar architectures, comparing single-photon direct-detection radars against quantum-entangled noise radars.

**Main result.** Quantum-entangled noise radars show potential for significantly enhanced detection ranges compared to classical systems by leveraging quantum correlations, with implementation feasible at a few kilometers using current technology.

**Method.** The authors derived range equations in terms of the Lambert W function by analyzing detection criteria based on signal-to-noise ratios (SNR) and correlation coefficients ($ho$).

**Model / system.** The analysis models three radar types: direct-detection (single-photon), classical noise, and quantum-entangled noise radars utilizing Two-Mode Squeezed Vacuum (TMSV) states. The system relies on microwave detection techniques.

**Key observables.** Maximum detection range ($R_{\max}$), Parameter Range Enhancement Factor (REF), Pearson correlation coefficient ($ho$), and effective threshold SNR ($	ext{SNR}_{	ext{QI}}^{	ext{th,eff}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Detection threshold ($	ext{SNR}_{	ext{th}}$), transmitted power ($P_t$), scattering cross-section ($\sigma$), and atmospheric absorption coefficients.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the use of advanced detectors (e.g., quantum transducers combined with single optical-photon detectors) and models noise addition to correlated pairs, while noting that conventional antennas limit potential benefits.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate schematic diagrams for direct-detection and noise radar systems, showing how $R_{\max}$ depends on $	ext{SNR}_{	ext{th}}$ and transmitted power for both classical and quantum regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the baseline range equation for direct detection (classical/single-photon), then introducing the correlation mechanism of noise radars, and finally deriving the enhanced range equations specific to quantum-entangled noise radar using correlation metrics like $ho$.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

This article presents a comprehensive analysis of two classes of quantum radars, including quantum direct-detection and quantum-entangled noise radars. In the first case, inspired by the well-established concept of single-photon LiDARs, we investigated the performance of single-photon radars, in which state-of-the-art single microwave-photon detectors are employed to enhance the detection sensitivity and enable the detection of weaker signals. We derived analytical expressions for the maximum detection range of both classes of quantum radars in terms of the Lambert W function, by considering all relevant system, target, and environmental parameters. Our formulation facilitates direct comparison of noise radars with direct-detection radars and suggests that a quantum-entangled noise radar can be regarded as an enhanced direct-detection radar with an effective threshold signal-to-noise ratio. Furthermore, we applied this framework to classical-correlated noise radars and defined the parameter range enhancement factor (REF) to quantify the superiority of quantum-entangled noise radars over their classical counterparts. Moreover, we introduced a rule-of-thumb for approximating the REF. We also examined the influence of limitations imposed by various microwave detection technologies. Our analysis shows that the conventional antennas limit the potential benefits of quantum-entangled noise radar systems. We also demonstrated that the optimal detection method for these radars is a microwave detector based on a quantum transducer combined with a single optical-photon detector. We showed that, with the current technology, implementing a quantum-entangled noise radar with the maximum detection range on the order of few kilometers is possible. Finally, we explored the potential applications of quantum-entangled noise radars.

</details>

Temperature dependence of the absolute phonon free energy, Fph, of elemental cerium calculated within (a) LDA and (b) LDA+DMFT for lattice constants between 4.30 and 5.25 ˚A. The LDA+DMFT phonon dispersions are computed at β = 20 (T ≈580 K) and used within the harmonic approx- imation.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10101_figures/2606.10101_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 4 demonstrates a pronounced correlation- dependent modification of the lattice dynamics across the α–γ transition of the average phonon frequency. The av- erage is obtained from the full phonon dispersions calcu- lated within LDA and LDA+DMFT, shown in Fig. 2 ((a) and (c) panels, respectively), by averaging the phonon frequencies along the chosen high symmetry path in the Brillouin zone. In the compressed volume regime corre- sponding to the α-phase, the two approaches yield nearly identical average frequencies, such that ∆ωavg remains close to zero. This indicates that electronic correlations exert only a marginal influence on the lattice response at small volumes. With increasing...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work develops a comprehensive theoretical framework to calculate the equation of state of cerium by coupling Dynamical Mean-Field Theory (DMFT) with phonon calculations. By treating both electronic correlations and vibrational entropy simultaneously, the authors achieve significant quantitative improvements over standard DFT methods. This highlights that for strongly correlated lanthanides, accurate phase stability requires accounting for both electronic renormalization and lattice vibrations.

**Why it may be interesting.** The methodology—combining advanced many-body theory (DMFT) with lattice dynamics and finite-temperature thermodynamics—is highly relevant for understanding strongly correlated materials where electronic structure dictates vibrational behavior, which is a key area in modern condensed matter physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To rigorously determine the equation of state and thermodynamic properties of elemental cerium, specifically quantifying the role of phonon free energy renormalized by strong electronic correlations.

**Main result.** The inclusion of both DMFT-derived electronic correlations and vibrational entropy significantly refines the predicted equilibrium volume, leading to better agreement with experimental measurements than standard DFT or DFT+U methods.

**Method.** The study combines Dynamical Mean-Field Theory (DMFT) calculations for electronic structure with lattice dynamics calculations that incorporate phonon free energy corrections across varying lattice constants.

**Model / system.** The system is elemental Cerium (Ce), focusing on the $\gamma$-phase, which exhibits strong $4f$ electron correlations. The theoretical framework extends DFT by incorporating DMFT to handle local electronic correlation effects ($U$) and adding vibrational entropy corrections derived from phonon calculations.

**Key observables.** Total energy vs. lattice constant, phonon spectra/dispersions, equilibrium volume ($a_0$), and the total free energy $F(T)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hubbard interaction strength ($U=6.0 	ext{ eV}$), inverse temperature ($eta = 20 	ext{ eV}^{-1}$, corresponding to $\sim 580 	ext{ K}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The fixed self-energy approximation is used for phonon calculations, neglecting the explicit displacement dependence of the local self-energy. The harmonic approximation is used for vibrational free energy.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate lattice parameter dependence of total energy (DFT vs DMFT), correlation-driven phonon dispersions showing volume dependence, and temperature/lattice constant dependence of the full free energy functional.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically builds the thermodynamic description: first establishing the failure of standard DFT for $f$-electron materials; second, incorporating electronic correlations via DMFT to refine the internal energy; third, adding vibrational entropy corrections derived from phonon calculations; and finally, combining all terms to yield a temperature-dependent equation of state.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the thermodynamic properties of elemental cerium by assessing the crucial role of phonon free energy within the framework of dynamical mean-field theory (DMFT). While conventional density functional theory (DFT) often fails to capture the intricate energy landscape of $f$-electron materials, our approach integrates many-body electronic correlations with lattice dynamics to achieve a more rigorous description of the equation of state. We calculate the total energy as a function of the lattice constant at both the DFT and DFT+DMFT levels, subsequently incorporating the vibrational free energy derived from the phonon density of states. Our findings reveal that electronic renormalisation of the force constants significantly alters the phonon spectra, particularly in the strongly correlated $γ$-phase. By applying these phonon corrections to the energy profiles, we observe a substantial refinement in the predicted equilibrium volumes. Using principal-component-based machine learning, we interpolate phonon dispersions continuously from a finite set of first-principles calculations and compare them to experiment, finding significantly closer agreement compared to conventional DFT and DFT+U calculations that neglect dynamical many-body correlations. This study underlines the necessity of accounting for both electronic and vibrational entropy when evaluating the phase stability and structural transitions of lanthanide systems under varying pressures and temperatures.

</details>

### [Extensible links in a broad class of single polymer chain models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11041v1)

**Authors:** Michael R. Buche, Matthew J. Grasinger, Jason P. Mulderrig  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.11041v1>

**Summary.** This paper develops a general, asymptotically correct theoretical framework for modeling single polymer chains whose links possess finite extensibility. By applying advanced mathematical techniques like Laplace's method, the authors derive accurate predictions for chain extension under force. This work provides a significant methodological advance by unifying treatments across different physical models.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical polymer physics, the development of a systematic, rigorous asymptotic theory to handle complex degrees of freedom and stiffness parameters mirrors techniques used in quantum field theory or many-body systems where approximations are necessary.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is developing a generally applicable asymptotic theory to rigorously incorporate link extensibility into discrete single-chain models for calculating thermodynamics.

**Main result.** The developed theory provides an asymptotically accurate approximation for the longitudinal chain extension, $\gamma(\eta)$, that successfully reproduces known results across various polymer models.

**Method.** The authors employ an asymptotic approach using Laplace's method on the partition function when link stiffness ($\kappa$) is large, yielding a systematic expansion in powers of $\kappa^{-1}$.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on single polymer chains modeled as discrete sequences of links. The models include the freely jointed chain and the freely rotating chain, characterized by an energy potential involving link angles and individual link potentials.

**Key observables.** Nondimensional longitudinal chain extension ($\gamma(\eta)$) under an applied force ($\eta$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Link stiffness ($\kappa$), which must be large ($\kappa \gg 1$) for the asymptotic theory to be valid.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory is strictly asymptotic, requiring the link stiffness $\kappa$ to be much greater than one. The approximation builds upon an inextensible reference system.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares the asymptotic approximation to the exact result for the freely jointed chain, showing rapid convergence with increasing $\kappa$. Figure 2 validates this theory using Monte Carlo simulations on the freely rotating chain, demonstrating accuracy across varying parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the general model and the need for a rigorous theory. It then develops the asymptotic expansion for the partition function and derives key observables like $\gamma(\eta)$. The theory is validated first analytically (freely jointed) and subsequently numerically (freely rotating chain).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The physics of polymer chains is often probed using molecular stretching experiments and various idealized single-chain models. The majority of these models consist of a discrete sequence of links, which may be treated as rigid or extensible. Although such models are well established and many specific extensible variants have been proposed, no generally applicable theory has been presented. Moreover, most existing treatments are heuristic rather than systematically and rigorously derived. This critical gap is closed here through the development of a generally applicable asymptotic theory for including link extensibility in a broad class of discrete models for single-chain thermodynamics. The theory is verified analytically using the freely jointed chain and validated numerically using the freely rotating chain. The resulting approximation is first-order accurate in inverse link stiffness, with quadratically decreasing error, and recovers extensible behavior across all link stiffnesses from a single rigid-link reference calculation.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10936"></a>
### [Genuine Multipartite Nonlocality for Arbitrary Input: Maximal Randomness Generation and Robust Self-Testing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10936v1)

**Authors:** Rajdeep Paul, Ranendu Adhikary, Alok Kumar Pan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10936v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10936_figures/2606.10936_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1: Trade-off between relative observed violation (r) and fidelity Fs(r) and Fo(r), ∀O ∈{XA3, ZA3}.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10936_figures/2606.10936_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2: Self-testing circuit for n=3</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a generalized Bell inequality to test genuine multipartite nonlocality across arbitrary numbers of measurement settings. Using advanced algebraic techniques like SOS decomposition, the authors derive the optimal quantum bound, proving that this nonlocality guarantees the extraction of maximal global randomness. Furthermore, they provide detailed analyses quantifying how robust these certified correlations remain when experimental noise is introduced.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides rigorous mathematical tools (SOS decomposition) to certify fundamental quantum properties like nonlocality and randomness in complex multipartite settings, which is crucial for device-independent quantum cryptography protocols.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a Bell inequality capable of detecting genuine multipartite nonlocality (GMNL) in arbitrary $m$-partite systems with an odd number of measurement settings ($n$), while simultaneously certifying maximal global device-independent randomness.

**Main result.** The framework successfully derives the optimal quantum violation, establishes strict separations between local/bi-local and quantum bounds, and proves that $m$ bits of DI randomness can be extracted at this optimum.

**Method.** The authors employ an analytical Sum-of-Squares (SOS) decomposition technique to derive upper bounds for Bell functionals without assuming finite Hilbert space dimensions. Self-testing is achieved by analyzing the conditions under which the quantum expectation value matches the theoretical maximum.

**Model / system.** The study analyzes $m$-partite quantum systems subjected to measurements with $n$ possible settings per party, characterized by a multipartite Bell functional $\Gamma^m_n$. The analysis extends to quantifying robustness against noise using trace distance calculations.

**Key observables.** Bell functional ($\Gamma^m_n$), optimal quantum violation ($\langle\Gamma^m_nangle_{	ext{opt}}^Q$), min-entropy ($R_{i_1...i_m}$), and trace distance between ideal and noisy expectation values.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of parties ($m$), number of settings per party ($n$, must be odd), noise parameters ($\epsilon, \alpha, eta$), and the relative violation $r$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation relies on SOS decomposition to bypass dimension assumptions. Robustness analysis often uses approximations based on small error margins (e.g., trace distance bounds proportional to $eta$).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate trade-offs between relative observed violation ($r$) and fidelity/trace distance bounds, showing how noise parameters affect the achievable quantum correlations.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically derives upper bounds for local ($\Gamma^m_n|_L$) and bi-local ($\Gamma^m_n|_{BL}$) models. It then uses SOS decomposition to find the optimal quantum value $\langle\Gamma^m_nangle_{	ext{opt}}^Q$, followed by detailed sections on self-testing, randomness extraction, and finally, quantifying robustness against noise using trace distance bounds.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Bell nonlocality provides the foundation for device-independent (DI) certification of quantum devices. We introduce a Bell inequality capable of identifying genuine multipartite nonlocality (GMNL) in an arbitrary m-partite scenario with an arbitrary odd number of measurements per party. Since the multi-setting nature of this inequality precludes the use of Jordan's Lemma, we construct an analytical sum-of-squares (SOS) decomposition to obtain the optimal quantum violation without assuming any bound on the Hilbert space dimension. This, in turn, enables self-testing of the shared entangled state and the corresponding measurement observables, up to local isometries, whose existence we confirm using a swap-based certification scheme. In addition, we show that our framework enables the extraction of maximal global DI randomness (m bits) at the optimal quantum violation, thereby exceeding previous limitations in the GMNL regime. Finally, we demonstrate that the architecture of our inequality yields improved robustness to noise as the number of measurement settings grows, ensuring experimental feasibility.

</details>

### [Thermodynamic Approach to Momentum Transport in Dense Fluids](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10858v1)

**Authors:** Christopher Devik Fjeldstad, Jonas Bueie, Astrid S. de Wijn

**Summary.** This work introduces an advanced theoretical framework to calculate fluid shear viscosity by replacing reliance on effective hard-sphere diameters with a function derived from the system's excess internal energy. By applying this method to LJ and WCA fluids, the authors demonstrate high predictive accuracy across wide density ranges, significantly advancing classical transport theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical fluids, the rigorous development of generalized transport equations based on fundamental thermodynamic potentials ($eta u_{	ext{ex}}$) provides a powerful template for developing similar non-equilibrium theories in quantum many-body systems where simple kinetic approximations fail.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a general theoretical framework for calculating transport coefficients, specifically shear viscosity, of dense fluids that extends beyond the limitations of existing theories like Chapman-Enskog theory.

**Main result.** The proposed thermodynamic approach using an exchange function ($\hat{g}$) accurately predicts the shear viscosity of various fluid models (WCA, LJ) with low mean relative errors (2%-4%) at low to intermediate densities.

**Method.** The authors reformulate transport equations by relating the necessary correction term ($X_{	ext{res}}$) in an equation of state to the excess internal energy ($eta u_{	ext{ex}}$), thus bypassing reliance on effective hard-sphere diameters.

**Model / system.** The study investigates dense fluids modeled using Lennard-Jones (LJ) and Weeks-Chandler-Anderson (WCA) potentials, as well as diatomic molecules with harmonic bond interactions. Simulations are performed across a wide range of densities ($ho^*$) and temperatures ($T^*$).

**Key observables.** Shear viscosity ($\eta$), Compressibility factor ($Z$), Mean relative prediction error.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Density range: $0.05 \le ho^* \le 0.8$; Temperature range: $1.5 \le T^* \le 4.0$. Low/intermediate density regime defined as $ho^* \le 0.3$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that the necessary correction term ($X_{	ext{res}}$) can be accurately represented by a function of the excess internal energy ($eta u_{	ext{ex}}$). Limitations exist at high densities or when inelastic collisions are dominant.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare predicted vs. simulated shear viscosity for Mie fluids, showing good agreement at low density; error plots show increasing errors above $ho^* \approx 0.35$.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the general framework using an exchange function ($\hat{g}$), shows how existing theories (MET) fit into it, proposes $X_{	ext{res}} = eta u_{	ext{ex}}$, and then applies this to test predictions against simulations for various fluid models.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a new framework for extending Chapman-Enskog theory beyond the hard-sphere fluid model. Rather than relying on effective hard sphere diameters, the approach makes use of on an exchange function which can be related to the thermodynamic properties of the system. We show that two existing extensions, including modified Enskog theory (MET), fit into this new framework. Based on our approach, we propose an alternative to MET that takes into account the potential interaction energy associated with the inter-particle interactions in the fluid. The proposed expression is applied to predicting the shear viscosity of several different simulated fluid models across a wide set of densities $0.05 \leq ρ^* \leq 0.8$ and temperatures $1.5 \leq T^* \leq 4.0$ in Lennard-Jones units. The fluid models considered include both the Weeks-Chandler-Anderson (WCA) fluid and the Lennard-Jones (LJ) fluid. At low and intermediate density, here taken to be $ρ^* \leq 0.3$, we report mean relative prediction errors between $2\%$ and $4\%$ for both these. Across all densities considered, the largest mean relative errors reported are $4.4\%$ and $8.1\%$ for the WCA fluid and LJ fluid respectively. We also investigate other interaction models, including a diatomic molecular model, in order to better understand the limitations of our approach.

</details>

### [Towards the implementation of a quantum classifier](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10150v1)

**Authors:** Lorenzo Confalonieri, Adrián Pérez Salinas, Stefano Carrazza

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates the feasibility of using a quantum circuit as a binary classifier for tasks ranging from digit recognition to distinguishing particle collisions at colliders. It details a hybrid training methodology involving VQCs and classical optimization, achieving results comparable to small neural networks in simulation. The work highlights both the potential power and the significant limitations (like barren plateaus) of current quantum machine learning implementations.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on ML applications rather than fundamental AMO physics, the reliance on parameterized quantum circuits and the discussion of barren plateaus are central topics in quantum information theory relevant to simulating complex quantum dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The work investigates building a binary quantum classifier using quantum circuits for tasks like image classification (MNIST) and particle physics process discrimination (jet tagging).

**Main result.** A binary quantum classifier can be built, showing performance comparable to small CNNs on jet images, but the overall approach is limited by model expressiveness and current simulation constraints.

**Method.** The study employs a hybrid quantum-classical approach, using Variational Quantum Circuits (VQC) trained via classical optimizers (like COBYLA or Powell) to minimize loss functions (e.g., Mean Squared Error).

**Model / system.** The model is a parameterized quantum circuit Ansatz built with qubits and trainable rotation gates ($R_Y(	heta)$), simulated using the Qibo framework on classical computers.

**Key observables.** Classification accuracy, ROC curve, AUC score, confusion matrices, and test set accuracy comparing quantum results to CNN benchmarks.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of qubits (up to 10 for $32	imes 32$ images), number of Ansatz layers, training data size, and choice of classical minimizer/loss function.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary limitations noted are the potential failure due to Barren Plateaus in gradient-based optimization and that current results are simulations, not real hardware measurements.

**Figures summary.** Figures systematically plot training vs. test accuracy across varying parameters (layers, data size, loss function) for MNIST digits and jet images under 'with' and 'without pile-up' conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the concept of the binary quantum classifier, details the input encoding methods for small arrays and images, describes the VQC Ansatz structure, and then systematically tests performance across multiple datasets (MNIST, LHC jets) by varying optimization parameters and comparing results to classical CNNs.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this work, we investigate the use of a quantum circuit as a binary classification model in the context of quantum machine learning. We call this model, binary quantum classifier.   First, we describe fundamental concepts of quantum computing and introduce the computational tool used: Qibo, an open-source framework for efficient quantum simulations and quantum hardware control. Then, we describe how to design a binary quantum classifier for the classification of images and small arrays of variables by showing how to input data in the circuit, defining a quantum circuit model Ansatz with trainable parameters and a loss function, and implementing multiple minimizers. We test our quantum classifier with two data sets. The first one is the MNIST data set which is composed of handwritten digits (reduced to only handwritten zeros and handwritten ones for binary classification). We study the behavior of different minimizers by increasing the number of layers of the Ansatz. The second data set represents two different high energy collisions that can occur at colliders such as LHC (CERN). Due to in-time proton-proton interactions known as pile-up, we distinguish two different data sets: "without pile-up" and "with pile-up". These collisions can be represented by images of size 32x32 or by six high-level variables that we call features. By increasing the size of the training data set and the number of layers of the Ansatz, we search for the best minimizer. Splitting the data set in training set and test set, we compute: ROC curve, AUC score, confusion matrices and test set accuracy. For "with pile-up" images, we compare the results obtained with the quantum classifier with a small convolutional neural network.   We conclude that is possible to build a binary quantum classifier with a quantum circuit and we highlight its performances and limitations in comparison with classical technologies.

</details>
