<a id="paper-2606.09988"></a>
### [Absence of poor local minima in matrix product states](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.09988v1)

**Authors:** Hao-Kai Zhang, Chenghong Zhu, Shuo Liu, Shi-Xin Zhang, Tao Xiang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.09988v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.09988_figures/2606.09988_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Energy landscape schematics in the absence (a) or presence (b) of poor local minima. (c) Sequential circuit, serving as the preparation circuit for MPS. (d) Brickwork cir- cuit. (e) Sloping brickwork circuit, which reduces to sequen- tial circuit when restricted to a single layer. Each block in the circuits represents a unitary that is universal on its support. The grey arrows indicate that the typical energy landscapes of sequential circuits are free from poor local minima, whereas those of brickwork-type circuits are not.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.09988_figures/2606.09988_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Local minimum correspondence. (a) and (c) depict the MPS representation spaces A[i] MPS and A[j] MPS with orthog- onality centers at site i and j, respectively. The rings repre- sent the gauge orbits. (b) depicts the MPS variety VMPS as a subset in the Hilbert space H. A gauge orbit is mapped to a single physical state by the contraction map ΨMPS. The insets depict the respective energy landscapes. Each physical local minimum is mapped to a continuum of local minima in the representation space with the same energy.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.09988_figures/2606.09988_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. The numerical training curves of (a) sequential cir- cuits of β = 3, L = 1, (b) brickwork circuits of β = 2, L = 4, and (c) sloping brickwork circuits of β = 2, L = 4. The block size β and the number of layers L are selected so that the num- ber of parameters in different circuits are comparable. The loss function is the energy expectation of random backward- evolved Hamiltonians. The darkness of the color marks the system size from 4 to 18 qubits. The number of samples is 50 for each system size. (d) The corresponding local minimum distributions in the form of scatter plots, with the data ex- tracted from the final values in (a-c).</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work mathematically proves that Matrix Product States (MPS) possess an energy landscape free from poor local minima due to effective local overparametrization arising from gauge freedom. By analyzing the convergence dynamics using gradient flow methods, the authors show MPS are robustly trainable compared to general quantum circuits. This provides a crucial theoretical guide for overcoming optimization bottlenecks in variational quantum algorithms.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous mathematical explanation for why certain structured quantum ansatzes (like MPS) are inherently more robust to optimization failures than general circuits, guiding the design of variational algorithms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper resolves the paradox of quantum circuit trainability by proving that Matrix Product States (MPS) energy landscapes are free from poor local minima, unlike general circuits.

**Main result.** The gauge freedom inherent in MPS creates an effective local overparametrization that forces local minima to concentrate near the global minimum, ensuring reliable optimization convergence.

**Method.** Theoretical analysis involving defining and measuring the 'local minimum distribution' using the long-time limit of gradient flow (TDVP/QNGD) on the parameter space.

**Model / system.** The study analyzes variational quantum circuits parameterized by MPS, applied to general Hamiltonians $H$. The comparison is drawn between sequential circuit structures and more restrictive 'brickwork' circuits.

**Key observables.** Local minimum distribution ($P_E(E)$), relative error ($\epsilon$), and the ratio $R(A)$ quantifying gradient cancellation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Bond dimension ($\chi$), system size ($N$), and the structure of the Hamiltonian (e.g., local vs. non-local terms).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the optimization flow converges to a well-defined measure over local minima, which is shown to be independent of the specific MPS representation center.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare training curves showing sequential circuits converging reliably while brickwork circuits get trapped in poor local minima; other figures illustrate gradient cancellation ratios $R(A)$ remaining positive for MPS structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the theoretical framework by defining the optimization map and local minimum distribution. It then proves key theorems demonstrating the invariance of this distribution under changes in the MPS representation center, culminating in numerical evidence comparing sequential vs. brickwork circuit performance.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum circuits suffer from severe trainability issues: even shallow circuits are swamped with poor local minima. Yet matrix product states (MPS), which can be prepared by sequential circuits, are remarkably trainable in practice -- as demonstrated by decades of successful density matrix renormalization group calculations. In this work, we resolve this apparent paradox by proving that the energy landscapes of MPS are free from poor local minima, under the same setting where brickwork circuits are not. The key insight is that the gauge freedom of MPS creates an effective local overparametrization that causes local minima to concentrate near the global minimum, analogous to overparametrized classical neural networks. We rigorously prove that the local minimum distribution of the MPS energy landscape is invariant under moves of the orthogonality center. Numerical experiments further confirm that the optimization of sequential circuits converges to near-optimal solutions even for random Hamiltonians, in stark contrast to brickwork circuits. Our findings highlight the pivotal role of effective local overparametrization in determining trainability, providing a valuable guide for overcoming the trainability bottleneck of variational quantum algorithms.

</details>

### [Coset Ensemble Decoder for Quantum Error Correction with Algorithm-Hardware Co-Design](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11076v1)

**Authors:** Shuang Liang, Jubo Xu, Giulio Bassanino, Qianzhou Wang, Yidong Zhou, Yuncheng Lu, Zhiwen Mo, Paul H. J. Kelly, Bo Yuan, Wayne Luk, Hongxiang Fan

Clustering is a streaming growth-and-merge compu- tation that naturally maps to a seven-stage pipeline (S1–S7). </figure>

**Summary.** This paper presents a novel algorithm-hardware co-design decoder for Quantum Error Correction (QEC) that improves upon existing methods by explicitly considering logically equivalent error cosets. By combining ensemble forest exploration with specialized, resource-efficient hardware architecture, the system achieves superior accuracy and latency trade-offs compared to state-of-the-art decoders on surface codes.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on digital quantum computation, the rigorous analysis of resource scaling, pipeline stalls, and trade-offs between algorithmic complexity and hardware implementation is highly relevant to designing control electronics for complex AMO or superconducting qubit systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is designing a Quantum Error Correction (QEC) decoder that achieves both high logical accuracy and ultra-low latency for reliable large-scale quantum computation.

**Main result.** The proposed coset ensemble decoding co-design significantly improves the accuracy-latency trade-off over vanilla MWPM and UF decoders, while drastically reducing hardware resource consumption (up to 8.2x reduction in LUTs).

**Method.** The authors introduce coset ensemble decoding, which approximates the intractable coset maximum-likelihood rule using ensemble forest exploration, coupled with a domain-specific, pipelined hardware architecture featuring hierarchical ID mapping and multi-bank memory hashing.

**Model / system.** The work analyzes error correction on surface codes under circuit-level depolarizing noise. The decoding process must resolve ambiguities (degeneracy) where multiple physical errors yield the same syndrome but different logical outcomes.

**Key observables.** Logical Error Rate (LER), decoding latency, FPGA LUT consumption, and System Infidelity ($\hat{C}(R)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Code distance ($d$), physical error rate ($p$), and tunable candidate number ($K$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The approach is an approximation to the optimal coset ML decoding; hardware optimizations assume specific concurrent memory access patterns that can be managed by hashing.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare LER vs. code distance for MWPM, UF, and the proposed method; latency breakdowns show bottlenecks in clustering pipelines; architectural diagrams detail multi-bank hashing and hierarchical ID mapping.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from identifying the need to solve coset ML decoding (due to degeneracy) to proposing the ensemble forest exploration algorithm. It then details the hardware co-design, including specific memory management techniques (hashing/ID mapping), followed by extensive benchmarking comparing accuracy and latency against state-of-the-art decoders.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Reliable large-scale quantum computation relies on fault-tolerant architectures, where quantum error correction (QEC) continuously extracts and decodes error syndromes in real time. A critical component in QEC is the decoder, a classical subsystem that must simultaneously deliver high logical accuracy and ultra-low latency. This paper presents a novel algorithm-hardware co-design that improves the accuracy-latency trade-off over existing approaches such as vanilla Minimum-Weight Perfect Matching (MWPM) and Union-Find (UF) decoders. At the algorithmic level, we introduce coset ensemble decoding, which improves UF decoding by explicitly exploiting logically equivalent cosets. Our method performs ensemble forest exploration to generate multiple coset-consistent candidates and aggregates them to approximate coset-level maximum-likelihood decoding. We further reduce computational and memory complexity via reverse-order elimination and lossless graph compression, without sacrificing accuracy. At the hardware level, we design a domain-specific architecture that temporally reuses resources, avoiding the code-distance-proportional resource growth in prior spatial architectures. Several optimizations, such as multi-bank memory hashing and hierarchical ID mapping, are proposed to mitigate pipeline stalls and memory conflicts under highly concurrent access patterns. Under a circuit-level depolarizing noise model, our co-design approach achieves a better accuracy-latency trade-off than prior MWPM- and UF-based decoders, while reducing FPGA LUT consumption by up to 8.2 times compared with reported UF-based decoder resources. The tunable candidate number further exposes a flexible design knob, enabling users to tailor decoding performance to the requirements of different fault-tolerant workloads. Our implementation is publicly available at https://github.com/IMSeonL/coset-ensemble-decoder.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper advances the theory of magic state cultivation by generalizing phase kickback checks beyond previously studied cases. It details a protocol for efficiently cultivating $\sqrt{T}$ magic states on complex codes like the doubled color code using lattice surgery. The results demonstrate improved fault tolerance and provide pathways for realizing early, scalable quantum computation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to theoretical quantum information scientists working on resource estimation and hardware-agnostic protocols, as it provides concrete, advanced techniques for generating necessary non-Clifford gates.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The research aims to generalize phase kickback checks for magic states at arbitrary Clifford hierarchy levels, specifically focusing on efficient cultivation protocols for $\sqrt{T}$ gates.

**Main result.** The authors present a protocol for $\sqrt{T}|+angle_L$ magic state cultivation on the doubled color code and demonstrate that end-to-end simulations show high fidelity when employing lattice surgery escape strategies.

**Method.** The methods involve generalizing phase kickback measurements, implementing protocols on specific Quantum Error Correcting Codes (QECCs) like the doubled color code, and utilizing lattice surgery for code expansion.

**Model / system.** The work analyzes quantum error correction codes, including the 2D and doubled color codes, which are used to protect logical qubits. The goal is to prepare non-Clifford magic states ($T$ or $\sqrt{T}$) required for universal fault-tolerant quantum computation.

**Key observables.** Logical error rate (infidelity), success rate, spacetime volume per shot, and the parity outcome of phase kickback measurements.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Code parameters such as $f=3$ ($	ext{[[15, 1, 3]]}$) and $f=5$ ($	ext{[[49, 1, 5]]}$), and noise regimes (with/without idling noise).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The protocol relies on the ability of the code to support a corresponding transversal phase gate ($P_{n-1}$) during double-check, and simulations often use approximations for computational tractability.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate general phase kickback measurement circuits (Fig. 1) and specific $\sqrt{T}|+angle_L$ check circuits on the doubled color code (Fig. 2). Other figures compare end-to-end simulation performance across different escape strategies and noise models.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first generalizing the phase kickback checks, then presenting a detailed protocol for $\sqrt{T}|+angle_L$ cultivation on the doubled color code using lattice surgery. Finally, it discusses incorporating this into larger fault-tolerant architectures like STAR.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Recently, experimental and theoretical quantum error correction methodology has seen remarkable breakthroughs. In particular, magic state cultivation has been shown to simplify magic-state preparation and make it feasible for near-term devices. However, recent research on magic state cultivation has focused primarily on the cultivation of $T\left| + \right>_L$. Only a few other magic state cultivation methods beyond $T\left| + \right>_L$ have been investigated. Here, we generalize phase kickback checks for magic states at arbitrary Clifford hierarchy levels in specific codes. We provide an example of cultivation of $\sqrt{T}\left| + \right>_L$ in the doubled color code and the corresponding escape strategy using lattice surgery from the color code to large rotated surface codes. Using state vector simulation for un-grown cultivation, we observe a strong consistence between $S\left| + \right>_L$ and $\sqrt{T}\left| + \right>_L$ cultivation's performance on the doubled color code. Finally, we discuss the application of the corresponding $\sqrt{T}\left| + \right>_L$ cultivation, incorporating the STAR architecture and $T$ gates, for early fault-tolerant quantum computing and its potential to shorten gate synthesis in the fully fault-tolerant quantum computing era.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10343"></a>
### [Neural-network solution of subtracted three-body Faddeev integral equations near the Efimov limit](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10343v1)

**Authors:** Lucas A. Souza  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10343v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10343_figures/2606.10343_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Schematic representation of the DNN solver for the subtracted Faddeev integral equation. The logarithmic momentum x = log(q/µ3) is fed into a feed-forward neural network, which outputs the symmetrized spectator vector uθ(x). The binding energy ϵ3 = exp(s3), with s3 = log ϵ3, is a trainable parameter and enters the residual of the discretized integral equation. The total loss combines the equation-informed residual with normalization and, when required, orthogonality constraints. The dashed feedback arrow indicates that gradients flow back through the kernel B(ϵ3, 1/a) to both the network weights θ and the energy parameter s3.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10343_figures/2606.10343_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. The DNN curves closely follow the analytical results; the 2s node at ρ = 2 is correctly captured.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10343_figures/2606.10343_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 4. DNN–deterministic relative deviation ∆E3, Eq. (21), for the first four three-body bound states. The boxplot shows the distribution of ∆E3 over all values of 1/a for which the DNN was continued. The deterministic benchmark is a nu- merical reference obtained from the same discretized kernel, not an exact analytical solution.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10343_figures/2606.10343_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 3. Efimov spectrum E3 as a function of the inverse scattering length for the first four three-body bound-state branches. The gray dashed line marks the atom-dimer thresh- old E2 = −1/a2 in the units used here (ℏ= m = µ3 = 1). The unitary limit is indicated by the vertical black line.</figcaption>

<img src="2606.10343_figures/2606.10343_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 5. Training convergence of the DNN solver for the Efimov ground state at unitarity. (a) Residual loss Lres (red squares) and total loss Ltotal (blue circles) vs. epoch. (b) Trainable binding-energy parameter ϵ(DNN) 3 vs. epoch; the dashed line marks the deterministic reference ϵ(det) 3 = 9.2699 × 10−3.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The authors apply Deep Neural Networks (DNNs) to solve the three-body Faddeev integral equation for bosons near the Efimov regime. By minimizing the residual of the discretized equation while treating the binding energy as a trainable parameter, they create a compact and differentiable representation of the solution. This method successfully reproduces universal physical constants and allows for smooth continuation of the spectrum across varying interaction strengths.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work demonstrates a powerful application of machine learning—using differentiable physics constraints (residual minimization) to solve complex, non-linear few-body quantum problems that are analytically intractable or numerically demanding.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of solving the three-body Faddeev integral equation for identical bosons near the Efimov limit, a problem characterized by scale invariance and renormalization.

**Main result.** A Deep Neural Network (DNN) trained via residual minimization successfully reproduces known universal physics, such as the Efimov ground-state binding scale at unitarity with high accuracy.

**Method.** The core method involves using a DNN ansatz to represent the spectator vector solution of the discretized integral equation, minimizing the residual loss while jointly training the three-body binding energy parameter.

**Model / system.** The system is three identical bosons interacting via a zero-range s-wave force. The physics is governed by the subtracted spectator Faddeev integral equation, which requires renormalization due to ultraviolet divergences.

**Key observables.** Three-body binding energy ($\epsilon_3$), Efimov scaling ratios ($\epsilon(n)/\epsilon(n+1)$), and the dependence of these quantities on the inverse scattering length ($1/a$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The three-body binding scale ($\epsilon_3$) is a trainable parameter, and the system is analyzed across varying $1/a$, including the unitary limit ($1/a=0$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes that the solution can be represented by a DNN ansatz and relies on comparing results against deterministic diagonalization as an *a posteriori* benchmark.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare DNN solutions to exact radial wave functions for Hydrogen benchmarks, plot the continuation of $E_3$ vs. $1/a$, and show training convergence plots for the residual loss and energy parameter.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the physical problem (Faddeev equation near Efimov limit), details the DNN formulation using residual minimization with a trainable energy, validates this approach on the Hydrogen atom benchmark, and finally applies it to trace the universal Efimov spectrum across varying scattering lengths.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We apply a deep-neural-network (DNN) ansatz to the symmetrized spectator vector of the subtracted three-body Faddeev integral equation for identical bosons near the Efimov limit. The network is trained by minimizing the residual of the discretized integral equation, while the positive binding scale associated with the three-body energy is treated as a trainable parameter. Deterministic diagonalization of the same discretized kernel is used only as an a posteriori numerical benchmark. As preliminary validation, the neural-solver strategy is tested on the analytically solvable hydrogen radial problem. At unitarity, the DNN reproduces the Efimov ground-state binding scale with a DNN--deterministic deviation of $0.022\%$, while the first excited state is recovered to $0.002\%$. The deterministic solver recovers the universal Efimov scaling ratio $e^{2π/s_0}\simeq 515.03$, and the neural method traces the bound-state branches as a function of the inverse scattering length $1/a$ by continuation from the unitary solution. These results indicate that DNN-based residual minimization can provide a compact and differentiable representation of a renormalized few-body integral-equation solution in a regime governed by discrete scale invariance.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10872"></a>
### [Quantum Colorings of Spheres](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10872v1)

**Authors:** Olivier Lalonde  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10872v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10872_figures/2606.10872_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: The graph G19. The graph6 representation of this graph is RxLAKA@AgYAWDGO?O?@??A?W@@OC@ .</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10872_figures/2606.10872_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: The exact remote state preparation protocol proving corollary 1.12.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10872_figures/2606.10872_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: An alternative representation of the graph G19, used in the proof of theorem 4.5.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper tackles the problem of determining when the quantum chromatic number of spheres equals their dimension. It achieves this by developing advanced techniques rooted in Clifford algebra representations and analyzing algebraic constraints derived from graph homomorphisms. The results provide precise classification theorems, linking quantum colorability to the existence of Hadamard matrices and demonstrating applications in quantum communication protocols.

**Why it may be interesting.** The deep connection drawn between quantum information theory (remote state preparation, error correction codes) and classical combinatorial problems (graph coloring) provides a powerful theoretical framework for understanding resource limitations in quantum computation.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates determining the quantum chromatic number of spheres ($\chi_q(S^{n-1})$) and establishing necessary and sufficient conditions for this number to equal the dimension $n$. It also explores generalizations involving rank-$r$ colorings.

**Main result.** It establishes that $\chi_q(S^{n-1}) = n$ if and only if $n \in \{2, 4, 8\}$ in the rank-one case, linking this to the existence of Hadamard matrices. Furthermore, it provides an exact remote state preparation protocol for real qubits.

**Method.** The analysis heavily relies on representation theory applied to Clifford algebras, analyzing unitary representations and projectors derived from graph homomorphisms between spheres and Grassmannians. Proofs involve algebraic constraints derived from these quantum structures.

**Model / system.** The primary model is the quantum coloring of spheres ($S^{n-1}_F$), where $F$ can be $\mathbb{R}$ or $\mathbb{C}$. The mathematical machinery involves analyzing $*$-subalgebras generated by unitaries representing Clifford algebras on $m$ generators.

**Key observables.** Quantum Chromatic Number ($\chi_q(G)$), Orthogonal Rank ($\xi_R, \xi_C$), and the existence of specific unitary representations or maximal code spaces ($P$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension $n$, the rank $r$ of the coloring, and the order of potential Hadamard matrices.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes finite dimensionality for the underlying Hilbert space and relies on establishing equivalences between quantum colorability conditions and algebraic properties of unitaries.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 illustrates an exact remote state preparation protocol used to prove a key corollary regarding real-valued states, demonstrating resource efficiency in quantum communication.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing known results for small dimensions (like $n=2, 4, 8$), then proving the necessity of using real representations over complex ones. It develops algebraic machinery based on Clifford algebras and graph homomorphisms to derive necessary and sufficient conditions for $\chi_q(S^{n-1}) = n$.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Cameron, Montanaro, Newman, Severini and Winter gave a construction which shows that, for $n \in \{2,4,8\}$, any graph $G$ which admits a real $n$-dimensional orthogonal representation is quantumly $n$-colorable. This result can be recast as the statement that the real sphere $S^{n-1}$ is quantumly $n$-colorable for these values of $n$. We investigate possible extensions of their construction. We first show that their hypothesis that the orthogonal representation be real-valued is required by proving that there is no analogue of this for the complex spheres, which all have quantum chromatic number strictly bigger than the dimension except in two dimensions. We also provide candidate finitary witnesses of this and show for the first time that the real and complex orthogonal ranks are distinct as a byproduct. For the real case, we show that if $S^{n-1}$ is quantumly $n$-colorable, then either $n=2$ or $n$ is a multiple of 4, and show that the converse holds whenever a Hadamard matrix of order $n$ exists. Hence, assuming the Hadamard conjecture, this completely classifies the dimensions to which the CMNSW construction can be extended. Our method of proof involves showing the equivalence between the existence of such a construction and the existence of a maximal code space for Clifford-algebraic errors given a clean ancilla, and we believe that the representation-theoretic techniques we use for tackling the latter problem could be of independent interest. It also follows from this equivalence that $S^{n-1}$ admits a rank-one quantum $n$-coloring if and only if $n \in \{2,4,8\}$, thereby settling a conjecture of Zeng and Zhang, as does the fact that for all $m \geq 1$, there exists a catalytic zero-error remote state preparation protocol for real $m$-qubit states with $m$ bits of communication and which consumes $m$ ebits.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.10874"></a>
### [Schmidt Decomposition-Based Methods for Efficient Quantum Image Encoding](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10874v1)

**Summary.** This paper tackles the challenge of implementing complex quantum image encoding protocols on limited NISQ hardware. The authors propose using Low-Rank Approximation based on Schmidt decomposition to compress the required quantum state representation. This technique successfully reduces circuit depth and gate counts across multiple established models while preserving high levels of image reconstruction accuracy.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the resource bottleneck in quantum machine learning/signal processing by providing a mathematically grounded technique to simplify state preparation circuits, which is crucial for advancing practical QIP algorithms on NISQ devices.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing quantum image encoding methods for Quantum Image Processing (QIP) create circuits that are too deep, complex, and resource-intensive for current Noisy Intermediate-Scale Quantum (NISQ) hardware.

**Main result.** Applying Low-Rank Approximation (LRA) via Schmidt decomposition significantly reduces circuit depth (e.g., 97% reduction in FRQI) while maintaining near-perfect image reconstruction fidelity.

**Method.** The authors use the Schmidt decomposition to express the quantum state and then truncate this representation to a low rank, effectively keeping only the most significant entanglement components.

**Model / system.** The study analyzes three existing QIP encoding models: FRQI, QPIE, and NEQR. These methods encode classical grayscale image data into high-dimensional quantum states $|\psiangle$.

**Key observables.** Circuit depth, CNOT count, Mean Squared Error (MSE), and visual quality of reconstructed images.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The Schmidt rank ($r$) used for truncation is the key parameter varied to balance resource efficiency against reconstruction accuracy.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that natural images are compressible in the Schmidt basis, allowing low-rank approximation to preserve essential information while drastically reducing circuit overhead.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate reconstructions at varying ranks for FRQI, QPIE, and NEQR. Tables compare resource metrics (depth, CNOTs) across models before and after LRA. Plots show the relationship between rank and complexity/error.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of resource-intensive QIP encoding, details the Schmidt decomposition and LRA technique, compares its application across FRQI, QPIE, and NEQR using quantitative metrics (depth, CNOTs, MSE), and concludes by demonstrating the feasibility of low-rank methods for near-term hardware.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In quantum image processing, a fundamental step is encoding classical image data into quantum states. This can be achieved using methods such as Flexible Representation of Quantum Images (FRQI), Quantum Probability Image Encoding (QPIE), and Novel Enhanced Quantum Representation (NEQR). However, on real quantum hardware, these encodings can quickly lead to circuits with many gates, large circuit depth, and high qubit usage, which is a problem for Noisy Intermediate-Scale Quantum (NISQ) devices. In this work, we investigate whether low-rank state approximation, formulated via Schmidt decomposition, can help reduce this complexity. The method keeps only the most significant parts of a quantum state's entanglement structure, making state preparation more efficient while preserving most of the image information. We compare the three encoding techniques in their original form and with low-rank approximation, evaluating metrics such as circuit depth, CNOT count, MSE, and visual quality of reconstructed images. The results reveal meaningful trade-offs between accuracy and resource efficiency, with the FRQI model achieving a 97 percent reduction in circuit depth while maintaining a near-perfect reconstruction (MSE of about 0.27). This demonstrates the potential of low-rank techniques for advancing practical quantum image processing on near-term hardware.

</details>

### [The UZH protocol: Separating errors and constructing improved CP2K basis sets and pseudopotentials](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11064v1)

**Authors:** Hossein Mirhosseini, Tiziano M. A. Müller, Matthias Krack, Thomas D. Kühne, Jürg Hutter

**Summary.** This paper introduces the UZH protocol, a systematic workflow designed to diagnose and correct numerical errors in Density Functional Theory calculations that combine Gaussian basis sets and pseudopotentials. By comparing results from three different calculation formalisms, it successfully separates the total error into distinct contributions from the basis set and the pseudopotential itself. This constructive approach allows researchers to generate improved, validated parameter files for accurate simulations across various materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter DFT, the rigorous error decomposition technique is a sophisticated example of developing robust computational frameworks, which shares methodological rigor with advanced many-body theory development.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Reliable DFT simulations require numerical settings whose residual errors are smaller than the chemical/material trends being studied, which is complicated by combining Gaussian basis sets and pseudopotentials.

**Main result.** The UZH protocol successfully decomposes the total computational error into distinct Gaussian-basis and pseudopotential components, allowing for targeted, constructive revisions to generate validated parameter files.

**Method.** A closed-loop workflow involving a three-way comparison between CP2K calculations, SIRIUS plane-wave calculations, and all-electron references to systematically isolate and correct approximation errors.

**Model / system.** The methodology applies to molecular systems, liquids, and condensed phases simulated using DFT within the CP2K/Quickstep framework, focusing on core-valence interactions.

**Key observables.** Error decomposition ($\Delta_{	ext{basis}}$ vs. $\Delta_{	ext{pp}}$), relative bond-length errors (ER), average molecular polarizabilities ($ar{\alpha}$), and equation-of-state error ($\epsilon$ metric).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gaussian basis set size (e.g., DZVP to TZV2P), pseudopotential type (GTH), and the condition number of the overlap matrix $\kappa_B(M)$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The total error is assumed to be a sum of independent contributions from the Gaussian basis approximation and the pseudopotential transferability.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare initial vs. improved total practical errors, showing separation into basis-limited (noble gases) and pseudopotential-limited (transition metals) error sources; Table II guides optimization based on these component comparisons.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of combined code discrepancies, details the UZH protocol's three-way comparison methodology, presents molecular calibration benchmarks to guide basis set improvement, and finally applies this systematic diagnosis to solid-state equation-of-state calculations for targeted parameter refinement.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Reliable density-functional simulations require numerical settings whose residual errors are smaller than the chemical and materials trends being interpreted. In CP2K/Quickstep, this requirement is complicated by the joint use of atom-centered Gaussian basis sets and norm-conserving pseudopotentials: a code-to-code discrepancy usually contains both contributions. We present the UZH protocol, a closed-loop CP2K workflow that calibrates molecularly optimized Gaussian basis sets on small molecules, validates the resulting settings in unary-crystal equation-of-state benchmarks, identifies whether the limiting approximation is the Gaussian basis or the pseudopotential. The diagnosis is then used to revise the parameter files. The central diagnostic is a three-way comparison between production CP2K-GTH-UZH calculations, SIRIUS calculations using the same Goedecker--Teter--Hutter pseudopotential in a systematic plane-wave representation, and all-electron full-potential linearized augmented-plane-wave SIRIUS references. This construction decomposes the practical CP2K error into a Gaussian-basis component and a pseudopotential component. The protocol distinguishes basis-limited noble-gas and heavy-element cases from pseudopotential-limited transition-metal cases, guides targeted revisions with the CP2K basis and pseudopotential optimizers, and produces improved MOLOPT basis sets and GTH pseudopotentials as explicit outputs of the workflow. The UZH protocol is therefore constructive: it does not merely measure or reduce errors a posteriori, but allows turning verification outliers into validated CP2K parameter files for simulations across molecules and condensed phases.

</details>

### [Bosonic Cyclic Codes: Trading Stabilizers for Gaussian Non-Clifford Phase Gates](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.11010v1)

**Summary.** The paper introduces bosonic cyclic codes, a generalization designed to balance error protection against photon loss with the ability to implement diverse logical gates. By developing a 'stabilizer-to-gate paradigm,' the authors show how sacrificing some error resilience can unlock multiple non-Clifford phase gates using passive Gaussian operations. This advances the theoretical understanding of fault tolerance in bosonic quantum memory systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it directly addresses the fundamental engineering challenge in quantum computing: achieving universal gate sets while maintaining high levels of fault tolerance in physical bosonic hardware platforms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is developing bosonic quantum error correction codes that can balance robust protection against physical errors (like photon loss) with the ability to implement a wide variety of fault-tolerant logical gates, especially non-Clifford phase gates.

**Main result.** Bosonic cyclic codes provide a general framework allowing for a measurable tradeoff between error protection and gate count; this enables the realization of multiple non-Clifford gates using passive Gaussian rotations.

**Method.** The authors generalize rotation-symmetric codes to bosonic cyclic codes, analyzing their structure via Fock state supports and developing protocols that convert high-order stabilizers into logical phase gates.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled using bosonic modes (Fock states) in the context of quantum error correction. The theory focuses on constructing and analyzing 'bosonic cyclic codes' as generalizations of known families like cat and binomial codes.

**Key observables.** Error distances ($d_n$ for photon loss, $d_	heta$ for phase rotation), logical Pauli gates ($ar{Z}, ar{X}$), and the ability to generate non-Clifford phase gates (e.g., $ar{T}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Code parameters $(S, n_0, n_1)$, photon loss distance $d_n$, rotation angle periodicity $2\pi/S$, and coupling strengths ($\chi, \Omega$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on specific mathematical structures (e.g., disjoint alternating Fock support for Vandermonde codes) and approximations regarding error detection protocols (e.g., using POVMs combining RIM).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the concept of the tradeoff between gate capability and error protection, showing diagrams of logical states under rotation ($\hat{R}_S$) and detailing multi-step adaptive Ramsey Interferometry Measurement (RIM) sequences for error detection in specific code examples.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first defining bosonic cyclic codes as a generalization of existing structures. It then analyzes the trade-off between error distances ($d_n$ vs $d_	heta$) and introduces generalized codes (cyclic cat/Vandermonde). Subsequent sections detail advanced techniques, including realizing $	ext{SU}(2)$ symmetries, developing specific error detection protocols using RIM, and formalizing a 'stabilizer-to-gate paradigm' for constructing new logical gates.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Bosonic codes offer hardware-efficient approaches to quantum error correction, with the best encodings offering effective protection of idle quantum information against loss and dephasing - particularly rotation-symmetric codes, which include the cat and binomial code families. However, rotation-symmetric codes are only naturally endowed with a single logical Pauli gate, while other logical gates require the use of non-linear operations, obstructing the utility of these codes for realizing quantum algorithms. Here, we balance error protection with controllability by introducing bosonic cyclic codes: a generalization of rotation-symmetric codes that enable the measured tradeoff of error protection properties for fault-tolerant logical phase gates. Through our general construction, we find that sacrificing the detectability of a single photon loss relative to a rotation-symmetric code can yield a number of logical phase gates commensurate with the original rotation symmetry order of the code, all achievable via passive Gaussian rotations. Giving the corresponding generalizations of cat and binomial codes - which we dub cyclic cat and Vandermonde codes, respectively - we further find that many of the desirable properties of these codes transfer to the bosonic cyclic code setting. We go on to discuss the larger $SU(2)$ symmetry and rotation gates of the codes, which yield additional stabilizers and logical Pauli gates, as well as new non-Clifford gates for the smallest `kitten' binomial code, and provide a new error detection protocol. Finally, we introduce a general paradigm for converting higher-order stabilizers to logical gates, as in our generalization of rotation-symmetric codes, and apply it to several multimode bosonic codes.

</details>

**Authors:** Zhao Zhang

**Summary.** The paper develops an advanced theory for the Yang-Baxter Equation governing the Chiral Potts Model and related parafermionic chains. It constructs a novel R-matrix dependent on three spectral parameters, demonstrating how onsite potentials modify integrability structures. This advances the understanding of exactly solvable quantum many-body systems in statistical mechanics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a deep theoretical understanding of integrability in complex quantum spin systems (like CPM), offering mathematical tools (three-parameter R-matrices) applicable to modeling exotic quantum phases beyond simple nearest-neighbor interactions.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is constructing a novel Yang-Baxter Equation (YBE) for R-matrices parameterized by three spectral parameters, extending integrability methods from vertex/edge models.

**Main result.** A new YBE structure is derived for the Chiral Potts Model (CPM), showing how onsite potentials contribute an extra spectral parameter to the R-operator beyond standard nearest-neighbor interactions.

**Method.** The derivation uses functional equations, star-triangle and star-star relations, and techniques involving elliptic functions and parafermion operators to ensure consistency with integrability conditions.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on integrable lattice models, specifically the Chiral Potts Model (CPM), which generalizes the Ising model. The analysis involves constructing R-matrices and Boltzmann weights for these quantum spin chains.

**Key observables.** Boltzmann Weights ($W_n$), R-matrix elements $R(\lambda, \mu, 
u)$, and the Hamiltonian structure derived from the transfer matrix.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Three spectral parameters ($\lambda, \mu, 
u$) are central; coupling constants like onsite potential strength ($U$) and temperature/inverse temperature ($\kappa$) govern the physics.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that integrability can be characterized by satisfying generalized Yang-Baxter or star-star relations, extending known results from simpler models to parafermionic systems.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate diagrammatic proofs of the star-star relation using star-triangle transformations and show the graphical structure of the YBE for the CPM.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds by first establishing the general framework relating R-matrices to Boltzmann weights, then deriving specific functional forms for these weights in the CPM, verifying the consistency via the star-star relation, and finally generalizing the Hamiltonian description using parafermion operators.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A new type of Yang-Baxter equation (YBE) for $R$-matrices parameterized by three spectral parameters is constructed from the star-triangle and star-star relations for the chiral Potts model. As the $Z_N$ symmetric generalization to the Ising model, its Boltzmann weights are known to depend on two variables describing a curve with genus larger than one for $N>2$, except for the self-dual point corresponding to the Fateev-Zamolodchikov chain. This combined with the fact that the quantum Hamiltonians of edge models like Ising contain both nearest neighbor interaction and onsite potential terms results in the extra spectral parameter of the $R$-operator. My construction extends the recent unification of solvable edge and vertex models which recasts Onsager's star-triangle relation from a mere alternative form of the YBE for edge models to its underlying ingredient.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10179"></a>
### [Trainability of IQP Quantum Circuit Born Machines Under Gaussian Initialization](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10179v1)

**Authors:** Gennaro De Luca  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10179v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10179_figures/2606.10179_page3.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 3</figcaption>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 4</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10179_figures/2606.10179_page5.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 5</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10179_figures/2606.10179_page6.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 6</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper provides a deep theoretical analysis into the trainability of Quantum Circuit Born Machines by bounding the variance and concentration of their gradients. It uses advanced tools from probability theory, such as Stein's lemma, to derive necessary scaling conditions on initialization parameters ($\sigma^2$, $\sigma_	heta^2$) that must be met to prevent gradient vanishing in quantum machine learning models.

**Why it may be interesting.** The rigorous mathematical analysis of gradient behavior under specific initialization schemes is crucial for developing practical, scalable quantum machine learning algorithms that can overcome theoretical barriers like barren plateaus.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the trainability issues, such as exponential concentration of gradients or barren plateaus, encountered when training Quantum Circuit Born Machines (QCBMs) using IQP circuits initialized with arbitrary Gaussian parameters.

**Main result.** It derives analytical lower bounds for the gradient variance and establishes probabilistic concentration bounds, providing concrete scaling rules ($\sigma^2 = O(n)$, $\sigma_	heta^2 |\Gamma(a)| \approx O(1)$) to mitigate these exponential decay issues.

**Method.** The analysis employs Stein's lemma and Lipschitz concentration bounds tailored for Gaussian random variables to rigorously bound the variance and deviation of the gradient calculated from the MMD loss function.

**Model / system.** The model is a QCBM trained via MMD loss using IQP circuits. The training process involves calculating expectation values $\langle Z_a angle_{q_	heta}$ which depend on parameters $	heta$ initialized from a Gaussian distribution $N(\mu_	heta, \sigma^2_	heta)$.

**Key observables.** Gradient variance ($	ext{Var}(\partial C / \partial 	heta_k)$), gradient deviation bounds, and expectation values of Pauli-Z observables ($\langle Z_a angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** MMD kernel bandwidth ($\sigma^2$), initialization variance ($\sigma_	heta^2$), ansatz connectivity measure ($|\Gamma(a)|$), and parameter mean ($\mu_	heta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis heavily relies on the assumption of Gaussian initialization for parameters $	heta$ and utilizes Stein's Lemma to derive bounds.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first reformulating the cost function, then deriving explicit expressions for the gradient and Hessian components using expectation identities. It culminates in applying concentration inequalities (like those derived from Gaussian properties) to establish necessary scaling conditions on $\sigma^2$, $\sigma_	heta^2$, and $\mu_	heta$ to maintain non-vanishing gradients.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum Circuit Born Machines (QCBMs) offer a natural approach to generative machine learning by leveraging the Born rule. Recent work has provided a method to classically train QCBMs with Instantaneous Quantum Polynomial (IQP) circuits via the Maximum Mean Discrepancy (MMD) loss. Despite the assumed intractability of sampling from IQP circuits classically, their expectation values can be computed classically, enabling training of these IQP QCBMs. However, quantum machine learning (QML) models have various other challenges, including trainability issues caused by exponential concentration or barren plateaus. While these issues have been explored for parameters sampled from a uniform distribution, little work has been done to rigorously treat the use of arbitrary Gaussian initialization schemes. This work leverages Stein's lemma and Lipschitz concentration bounds for Gaussian random variables to provide an analytical lower bound of the variance of the gradient and a probabilistic concentration bound of the deviation of the gradient from its mean. It discusses strategies to either avoid or encourage exponential concentration, as well as the conditions under which barren plateaus are more likely to occur.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.10230"></a>
### [Variational Approach for Uniform Quantum Permutation Generators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10230v1)

**Authors:** Farzam Nosrati, Nicolás Borrajo, Antonio Fernández Anta, Vincenzo Mancuso  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.10230v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10230_figures/2606.10230_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Three-stage variational quantum-circuit construction for uniform permutation generation. (i) Each candidate swap ˆUi j is realized by a parameterized controlled-SWAP (PCS) gate, with the control qubit prepared by ˆRy(θ). (ii) The PCS-layer architecture is determined by an interaction graph G = (V, E); at each layer ℓ, an ordered subset Mℓ⊆E is chosen to specify the active PCS operations. Gray edges represent admissible interactions, and red edges the selected ones. (iii) For the fixed layered architecture, the variational parameters are optimized by minimizing L(Θ), so that the resulting channel approximates the target uniform permutation map.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10230_figures/2606.10230_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Recursive construction of the non-swap probability matrices Mn and their corresponding linear-topology circuits. (Top) Examples for n = 2, 3, 4, 5 are shown. Each Mn is obtained from the upper and lower blocks inherited from Mn−1, shown in blue and gray, together with two newly added anti-diagonals, shown in red. For even n, the numbered anti-diagonal starts with even entries 2, . . . , n followed by odd entries n −1, . . . , 3, while the other anti-diagonal contains only 1s. For odd n, the roles of the two anti-diagonals are interchanged. (Bottom) The associated quantum circuits implementing the same recursive linear topology.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10230_figures/2606.10230_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Projection-loss optimization for the line-topology PCS ansatz on S 8. The best run over multiple random initializations reaches a residual of order 10−16.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10230_figures/2606.10230_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. Quantum Beneš circuit for n = 8.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10230_figures/2606.10230_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 5. Optimization of the projection loss Lproj for the selected PCS swap sequence. The curve shows the best run over multiple random initializations. The finite residual indicates that the ansatz cannot exactly realize the symmetric projector, and therefore does not implement the uniform permutation average over S 8.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a variational quantum circuit approach to generate uniform random permutations on $n$ qubits under realistic nearest-neighbor hardware constraints. It successfully constructs an efficient linear-depth circuit achieving exact uniformity, while rigorously proving that other known universal circuits fail this strict requirement.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, hardware-aware roadmap for implementing fundamental quantum tasks like random sampling, which is crucial for building fault-tolerant quantum algorithms and simulating complex many-body dynamics where connectivity matters.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of implementing uniform random permutation generation over $S_n$ using quantum circuits while respecting physical hardware connectivity constraints.

**Main result.** A variational circuit framework was developed that achieves exact uniformity with linear depth, $O(n)$, on linear nearest-neighbor topologies, significantly improving upon previous all-to-all constructions. Furthermore, it proves that certain architectures (like the quantum Beneš circuit) can realize permutations but fail to generate a uniform distribution.

**Method.** The authors utilize Variational Quantum Circuits (VQC) parameterized by angles ($\Theta$) and optimize them using projection loss functions ($L_{proj}$) to enforce the target state being proportional to the symmetric projector $\Pi_{sym}$.

**Model / system.** The system is a set of $n$ qubits representing subsystems whose permutations are generated by controlled-SWAP gates. The analysis focuses on circuit architectures constrained to linear nearest-neighbor interactions.

**Key observables.** Circuit size (controlled-SWAP count), circuit depth, and the minimization of the projection loss function ($L_{proj}$) towards zero.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The system size $n$, the variational parameters $\Theta$, and the connectivity graph structure (e.g., linear chain).

**Assumptions / limitations.** It is conjectured that exact uniform generation requires circuit size $\Omega(n^2)$ and depth $\Omega(n)$ for local architectures, contrasting with the logarithmic depth of universal but non-uniform circuits.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the three-stage VQC construction (PCS gates in layers), the recursive structure of the linear quantum circuit ($M_n$), and convergence plots showing loss function minimization for different topologies.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the problem using a general variational framework, then details the specific construction for linear nearest-neighbor topology achieving $O(n)$ depth. It contrasts this with the Beneš network, proving its inherent non-uniformity, and concludes by establishing complexity separations between mere realizability and true uniform sampling.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Uniform permutation generation is a fundamental task in both classical and quantum computation, with applications ranging from cryptography to quantum optimization and quantum error correction. Existing exact quantum constructions typically require all-to-all qubit connectivity and quadratic circuit depth. We develop a variational quantum circuit framework for uniform permutation generation under connectivity constraints, in which the circuit architecture is determined by the underlying interaction graph and the variational parameters are optimized to enforce the target permutation statistics. In particular, we present explicit controlled-SWAP-based unitary constructions that achieve exact uniformity with quadratic circuit size and linear depth \(O(n)\) on linear nearest-neighbor topologies. Our approach, therefore, removes the need for all-to-all connectivity while improving the depth of previous exact constructions by a factor. We further prove that a quantum Beneš-like architecture is intrinsically non-uniform. Despite its logarithmic depth and ability to realize any permutation it cannot generate a uniform distribution over permutations for any choice of variational parameters. These results clarify the role of circuit topology in exact permutation generation and identify variational quantum circuits as a natural framework for hardware-constrained uniform sampling. More broadly, this work suggests that exact uniform permutation generation is a strictly stronger requirement than mere permutation realizability, and lays the groundwork for a formal complexity separation between the two.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

</details>

### [Mean-field models for morphogenetic processes in physiological contexts](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10355v1)

**Authors:** D. Hernández, Alejandro Valdés López, E. C. Herrera-Hernández

**Summary.** This work establishes a rigorous biophysical model for morphogen dynamics in tissues by explicitly accounting for tissue compartmentalization and non-equilibrium processes. It uses coupled reaction-diffusion equations to predict pattern formation, showing that structural details can significantly alter the conditions required for classic instabilities like Turing patterns.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on biology, the mathematical machinery—analyzing non-equilibrium dynamics via reaction-diffusion systems and pattern formation (Turing mechanisms)—is highly relevant to open quantum systems and complex many-body physics far from equilibrium.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a biophysical formalism describing the spatiotemporal evolution of chemical profiles (morphogens) in tissues while accounting for tissue compartmentalization and non-equilibrium maintenance.

**Main result.** The framework successfully models pattern formation, showing that Turing instabilities can occur even when standard conditions are violated (e.g., activator diffusing faster than inhibitor), expanding the parameter space for morphogenesis.

**Method.** Derivation of coupled reaction-diffusion equations from conservation laws and chemical kinetic theory, incorporating explicit spatial structure via intracellular/extracellular domains.

**Model / system.** The system models morphogen dynamics in tissues, distinguishing between non-percolating, percolating, and bi-percolating structures. The governing equations couple intra- and extracellular diffusion, reaction kinetics (like Schnakenberg), and membrane transport processes.

**Key observables.** Morphogen densities ($ho_A, \dots$), Turing patterns, characteristic length scales, and the stability of steady states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Diffusion coefficients ($d_{J,i}, d_{J,o}$), reaction rates ($\alpha_j, eta_{j,k}$), membrane permeability ($e$), and spatial heterogeneity functions.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model uses mean-field approximations for complex tissue structures; it assumes the physico-chemical state is determined by average molecular densities.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate examples of Turing patterns under zero flux boundary conditions, show dispersion relations mapping bifurcation parameters (like membrane permeability and diffusion ratios), and demonstrate pattern formation when activator diffuses faster than inhibitor.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from general biophysical formalism to specific coupled reaction-diffusion equations. It analyzes the linear stability of steady states to predict Turing instabilities, demonstrating how structural details modify classical pattern formation criteria.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

This work introduces a biophysical formalism to describe the spatiotemporal evolution of the chemical profile in tissues, with the novelty of modeling tissue compartmentalization and the mechanism by which cells maintain the system far from thermodynamic equilibrium via production and/or degradation of substances. The models were derived from conservation laws, chemical kinetic theory, and geometric constraints, while considering fundamental properties of tissues to connect theoretical modeling with experimental observations. In a morphogenetic context, each morphogen is described by two coupled reaction-diffusion equations, representing intra- and extracellular dynamics, linked through membrane transport processes such as nonlinear, cross, and anomalous diffusion. We explore the models' morphogenetic potential through diffusion-driven instabilities and discuss how natural tissue heterogeneities influence Turing instabilities and self-organized phenomena. The mathematical structure reveals that two-morphogen systems can produce Turing patterns with multiple characteristic length scales, while the system's dimensionality enables chaotic behavior in well-mixed dynamics. Moreover, due to domain coupling, Turing instabilities are allowed for single-morphogen systems. We used Schnakenberg kinetics to demonstrate that Turing patterns arise even when the activator diffuses faster than the inhibitor (d$<$1), thereby expanding the parameter space for pattern formation. Our results suggest that tissue spatial structure has important consequences for Turing instability mechanisms, in some cases weakening the usual conditions for its emergence while widening the possible patterns it can produce. The proposed framework offers a minimal mathematical basis to explore emergent dynamics in biological and synthetic contexts, with potential applications in developmental biology and tissue engineering.

</details>

### [Spin polarisation signatures of Fractionally Charged Skyrmions in Fractional Quantum Hall states](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10754v1)

**Authors:** Odysseas Williams, Stefan Faelt, Christian Reichl, Werner Wegscheider

**Summary.** This work uses cavity-polariton spectroscopy on GaAs quantum wells to measure spin polarization in Fractional Quantum Hall states. By analyzing the optical response, the authors report that the depolarization away from quantized fillings strongly suggests the presence of Minimal Fractionally Charged Skyrmions (MFCS). This provides experimental evidence for collective spin dynamics in highly correlated electron systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The connection drawn between optical measurements in solid-state systems (GaAs QWs) and exotic topological excitations like Skyrmions provides a tangible experimental realization of complex many-body physics relevant to correlated electron models.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates spin polarization and low-energy excitations within Fractional Quantum Hall (FQH) states.

**Main result.** The research reports evidence for Minimal Fractionally Charged Skyrmions (MFCS), characterized by a depolarization following the empirical law $S=
u^*$ away from quantized fillings.

**Method.** Spin polarization is probed using cavity-polariton spectroscopy on GaAs quantum wells, measuring the optical coupling strength of interband Landau-level excitations.

**Model / system.** The system involves electrons in Fractional Quantum Hall states ($
u$ between $1/3$ and $1$), analyzed within the framework of Composite Fermion (CF) theory. The experiment uses GaAs quantum wells coupled to optical microcavities.

**Key observables.** Spin polarization ($P$), oscillator strength suppression, and the depolarization parameter $S$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fractional filling factor $
u$ ($1/3 \le 
u \le 1$), effective Composite Fermion filling factor ($
u^*$), and magnetic field ($B$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the depolarization away from quantized fillings is governed by the formation of Minimal Fractionally Charged Skyrmions (MFCS).

**Figures summary.** Figures show optical transfer-matrix simulations, lowest polariton energy vs. $B$, and plots of extracted spin polarization ($P$) versus filling factor ($
u$ or $
u^*$), comparing data to theoretical models.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first establishing the experimental measurement technique (polariton spectroscopy) to extract spin polarization signatures across various FQH states, then analyzing the depolarization behavior away from integer/fractional fillings, and finally interpreting this smooth variation as evidence for MFCS formation governed by $S=
u^*$.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate spin polarisation and low-energy excitations in fractional quantum Hall (FQH) states using cavity-polariton spectroscopy of high-mobility GaAs quantum wells. By measuring the optical coupling strength of interband Landau-level excitations over the range $1/3 \le ν\le 1$, we extract the spin polarisation of the electron system as a function of filling factor.   Complete suppression of the oscillator strength of the lowest energy excitation, characteristic of singlet trion formation in fully polarised systems, is reported for the first time in this regime.   At large magnetic fields, fully polarised FQH states exhibit symmetric depolarisation away from their quantised fillings, analogous to Skyrmionic behaviour near $ν=1$. The depolarisation follows an empirical law $S=ν^*$, where $S$ is the number of spin flips per added magnetic flux quantum and $ν^*$ the effective Composite Fermion filling factor. We interpret this behaviour as evidence for Minimal Fractionally Charged Skyrmions (MFCS) formed from bound spin-flip and quasiparticle excitations.

</details>

### [Magnetism and Topology from Circularly Polarized Phonon Floquet Engineering](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10854v1)

**Authors:** Dapeng Yao, Tiantian Zhang, Takashi Oka, Takehito Yokoyama

Schematic of the phonon-driven electron systems on a two-dimensional honeycomb lattice, where a circularly polar- ized phonon in optical branches at the Brillouin-zone center is excited. Electron-phonon coupling enables phonon-assisted nearest-neighbor hopping via phonon emission and absorption processes, which generates an effective next-nearest-neighbor hopping. <figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Orbital magnetization driven by circularly polarized phonons. (a-1) and (a-2) Intrinsic magnetic moment distri- bution for the valence band along the high-symmetry points. (b-1) and (b-2) Berry curvature distribution for the valence band along the high-symmetry points. The results without phonon dynamics are shown in (a-1) and (b-1), and those with phonon dynamics for different Chern numbers are shown in (a-2) and (b-2). Red and blue lines in (a-2) and (b-2) rep- resent ur/a0 = 6% (C = 0) and ur/a0 = 10% (C = −1), respectively. (c-1) and (c-2) Chemical potential dependence of the z component of the orbital magnetization. (c-2) en- larges the region around the band gap. Phonon energy...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.10854_figures/2606.10854_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. Spin magnetization driven by circularly polarized phonons. (a) and (b) Chemical potential dependence of the z-component spin expectation value with the colors repre- senting the Rashba SOC and phonon rotational amplitude, respectively. The on-site potential is set to be δ = 0.02t0. We fix the phonon rotational amplitude ur/a0 = 10% in (a) and the Rashba SOC λR = 0.4t0 in (b). (c) Band structure with the on-site potential δ = 0.02t0, the phonon rotational am- plitude ur/a0 = 8%, and the Rashba SOC λR = 0.4t0. The color represents the spin texture ⟨ˆSz⟩nk/(ℏ/2) of each band at k point. Phonon energy and temperature are set to be ℏΩ= 0.2t0 and kBT = 0.03t0, respectively.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This theoretical paper shows that coupling electrons on a honeycomb lattice to circularly polarized phonons can act as an effective magnetic field. By employing Floquet engineering, the system undergoes a controllable topological phase transition into a Chern insulator. This mechanism not only generates non-zero orbital and spin magnetizations but also provides a novel pathway for realizing topological materials using purely vibrational driving.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it uses time-periodic driving (Floquet engineering) in condensed matter systems—a core topic in AMO physics—to engineer fundamental quantum properties like topology and magnetism using lattice vibrations rather than external magnetic fields.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study demonstrates how circularly polarized phonons can induce electronic magnetization and drive a topological phase transition in a honeycomb lattice via phonon Floquet engineering.

**Main result.** Circularly polarized phonons act as an effective magnetic field, inducing a tunable topological phase transition from a trivial insulator to a Chern insulator, while also generating measurable orbital and spin magnetizations.

**Method.** The authors utilize Floquet theory in the high-frequency limit ($\Omega^{-1}$) to derive an effective Hamiltonian that incorporates phonon-mediated hopping terms, allowing for gap analysis at valley points.

**Model / system.** The model consists of electrons on a two-dimensional honeycomb lattice coupled to time-periodic driving from circularly polarized phonons. The physics is analyzed using the derived Floquet effective Hamiltonian, which generates an effective next-nearest-neighbor hopping term.

**Key observables.** Chern number ($C$), orbital magnetization ($M_z$), and spin magnetization ($\langle S_z angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Phonon frequency ($\Omega$), lattice parameters ($t_0, \delta$), and the strength of Rashba SOC ($\lambda_R$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on a van-Vleck type expansion in the inverse phonon frequency ($\Omega^{-1}$) for the effective Hamiltonian derivation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate band structures showing gap opening due to phonons, phase diagrams mapping the Chern number dependence on SOC strength and phonon amplitude, and schematics of the induced NNN hopping term.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the initial electron-phonon coupled Hamiltonian to deriving the high-frequency effective Floquet Hamiltonian. It then analyzes the resulting band structure near valley points to calculate topological invariants (Chern number) and magnetizations, concluding with a discussion of how phonon pumping controls these signs.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We theoretically show that circularly polarized phonons induce electronic magnetization and drive a topological phase transition via phonon Floquet engineering. Considering the electronic states modulated by circularly polarized phonons on a honeycomb lattice, we show that such lattice dynamics generates an effective next-nearest-neighbor electron hopping, leading to a Haldane-type mass term. Circularly polarized phonon breaks time-reversal symmetry (TRS) and opens a gap at valley points, undergoing phase transition from a trivial insulator to a Chern insulator. Moreover, the orbital and spin magnetizations emerge due to the breaking of TRS. Our results show that circularly polarized phonons serve as an effective magnetic field to engineer magnetism and topology, offering new opportunities for phonon Floquet approaches.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.10001"></a>
### [Fabry-Perot Interference, g-factor Anisotropy, and Gate-Tunable Quantum dot in Chiral Tellurium Nanowires](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.10001v1)

**Authors:** Suresh Ghimire, Mohammad Hafijur Rahaman, Nathan Tanner Sawyers, Madan Mohan Bhandari, Gokul Acharya, Syed Zulfiqar Hussain Shah, Iris Nandhakumar, Pawan Kumar, Zainul Aabdin Khan, Hugh O. H. Churchill, Dharmraj Kotekar-Patil

**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**
